The Red Page
Gloom ricrion
Four novels* deliberately run counter to
the booksellers’ counsel that war-time fiction
must be.composed to distract the reader from
his grim preoccupations. These authors
, callously give us gloom. Edith Wharton
mixes it with a pre-doomed romance; James
Joyce a’supra-human objeetive-
ness of the futile struggles of weak human-
ity ; Ex-Mill-Girl, from the first page, .wal-
lows in undiluted gloom.
“Night with a laugh of anarchy blotted
out the symbols of law and order, gave in-
finity for acres, reclaimed for a short time
the wild that was half tame, and lent a
sense that here fierce deeds had been done,
and might be done again. For why should
not fierce, dark deeds happen here again?”
They do. “It was Witches’ Country, at
the hour wlign their spells were worked:
when Will-o’-the-Wisp carried his wavering
lamp over black moors, blacker pool, and
shaking bog; when Barguest, the moorland
spirit-dog, followed the lonely traveller
caught in the net of darkness, the weird
paws sounding splosh-splosh behind him on
the soppy ground. It was the hour when
children spawned by the bog were found—
to be claimed at length by the bog, after
they had tasted human joy, human sorrow.”
Thus, a little unfairly, Ex-Mill-Ghi sets her
gruesome scene; for the story that follows
depends not upon these diabolical phantasies
but upon the conflict of gross and noble
human wills. The tragedy is one of human
ignorance, not of human superstition.
It is a tragedy of intense interest, with
a powerful and poignant appeal. Helen is
a noble character, drawn with the grim
incisiveness of a Thomas Hardy heroine.
She is held in thrall by her supposed father
—a horrible figure of malignancy, the mad-
ness in his blood acting as the spur for a
vile, purposeful hate. For Helen is not his
daughter; she is the child of a woman who
refused his love; and he lives but to make
the daughter suffer for the mother’s scorn
of him. lie finds a useful tool in a farm-
hand who loves Helen as she loves him, but
who is afraid of" that supposed hereditary
strain of insanity in her. Chance brings a
brutal tramp to the desolate farm ; and the
old man conceives bis final revenge. He sells
Helen to the tramp; and when her lover
runs away from Helen’s desperate wooing,
she accepts the tramp for reasons which
actually convince the reader. This is a
triumph of writing; for the nobility of
Helen's character is balanced by her igno-
rance of the world. The tragedy runs through
a series of dramatic scenes, brilliantly
imagined and described, with a mastery of
language, a sense of drama and a deep know-'
ledge of psychology. But the author, subtly
conscious of the inartistrv of piling horror
on horror, relents at the last moment; and
the dreadful night has a dawn of hope and
happiness.
Helen makes; the masterful woman’s mis-
take. She does the wooing herself. .No
wonder Martin, the ineffective farin-hand
who adores her, runs away. The old man
looks spitefully on.
“Besides, if I’m not mista’en, Helen’s
never played her hussy-card, yet. I ca’ it
her hussy-card. It’s not flirting. She could
flirt no more nor a wild-cat could flirt. She’s
savage. But fro’ Eve up’ards every woman
has had a hussy-card in their pack. Some’s
hardly known it were thear, an’ never used
it. Some lias. Some has bin too proud—
an’ Helen’s proud. It’s showin’ a bit too
much stockin’ i’ some, an' showin’ too little
in others. It’s wearin’ a ribbon to flap i’
somebody’s eyes just when it shouldn't. It’s
owt —an’ it’s nowt, in n way. It’s stickin’
to him with both arms when he’s chivalrous,
so as he can’t get away. Lots play it wi’out
knowin’ it. But Helen”- —-
Helen, in desperation, does play it—to the
limit. “Tim’s tli’ curse on thee,” he says.
“Can ta pass it on, and on. and on, forever?
Can ta not be brave enough to say, ‘lt
shall end here’?”
“Curst, am I?” she cries. “Why? Be-
cause I might lm’ a child that were gladder
or sadder than most, at th’ coinin’ up o’ th’
moon? Or because it might fancy itself a
prince, when its pockets were empty? Or
because it might, in some minute, plunge a
knife into what it loved? Sane men do that
last, every day i’ th’ calendar. . . .What if I
bore an idiot? Somewhere between man and
dog his mind would come. Would he be
less happy? Would he be less loved?”
Her last woman’s resource fails: he goes
away: and in her shame she marries the
tramp. Her life is nothing but a long tor-
ment of cruelty, with the hate-maddened old
man for ever turning the knife in her heart.
And when Martin comes back these two
take their lives into their own hands; and
survive, Strangely, to a life of happiness.
It is a story full of sombre beauty, and
the personality of Helen Stands out from
the ruck of fiction characterisations. As a
first novel, this book, in the style, the story
and the persons of the drama, is a remarkable
and matured achievement in the domain of
Gloom.
S
James Joyce may be regarded as The
Limit in Irish Literature. He is the most
objective of all modern writers; his stuff
sounds exactly like a Leacock pnro'dy of the
Russian Novel. Listen to this : “ 'Goloshes!’
said Mrs. Conroy. ‘That’s the latest. When-
ever it’s wet underfoot I must put on my
goloshes. To-night even, he wanted me to
put them on, but I wouldn’t. The next thing
he’ll buy me will be a diving suit.’ Gabriel
laughed nervously, and patted his tie reassur-
ingly, while Aunt Kate nearly doubled her-
self. so heartily did she enjoy the joke. The
smile soon faded from Aunt Julia’s face, and
her mirthless eyes were directed towards her
nephew’s face. After a pause she asked —
“‘And what are goloshes, Gabriel?’
“ ‘Goloshes, Julia!’ exclaimed her sister.
‘Goodness me, don’t you know what goloshes
are? You wear them over your....over
your boots, Gretta, isn’t it?’
“ ‘-Yes,’ said Mrs. Conroy. ‘Guttapercha
things. We both have a pair now’. Gabriel
says everyone wears them on the continent.’
“ ‘Oh, on the continent,’ murmured Aunt
Julia, nodding her head slowly.
“Gabriel knitted his brows and said*as if
he were ; slightly angered :
“ ‘lt’s nothing very wonderful, but Gretta
thinks it _ very funny because she says the
word reminds her of Christy Minstrels.’ ’’ *
There are pages and pages like that in
Dubliners. Joyce must make Tchekov
jealous.
Yet there is a strange power in this collec-
tion of sketches. Joyce makes you see Dub-
lin, almost makes you live there —so objec-
tively true and "so callously real are his
studies. He flashes a relentlesslv steady,
cold, brilliant light upon the Dubliners, show-
ing tip every half-thought in their brains,
and depicting, ay a visitant from another
planet alight depict, the curious antics of
our human kind. There is no pity here, no
sympathy, just an intent scientific analysis,
an unfeeling probe of cold steel. Dreary,
yes; but horribly, inhumanly true.- Feckless
and sordid lives, most of them : certainly the
worst advertisement the Irishman hasVver
had.
The death of a priest: “There he lay,
solemn and copious, vested as for the altar,
his large hands loosely retaining a chalice.
His face was very truculent, grey and mass-
ive, with black, cavernous nostrils and
circled by a scanty white fur.” The encoun-
ter of two schoolboys with an uncanny old
man. written as a schoolboy would recall the
incident, with nothing explained. A drunken
spree of some rich young men. The inarticu-
late love of a boy for a girl. Two horrible
young men who “pick up” servant-girls in
order to borrow money from them. The
dreary chronicles of a dreary boarding-house,
in which the boarder gets the landlady’s
daughter in trouble, and the mother makes
him promise to marry the girl. A dissolute
clerk, dismissed for incompetence, who goes
on a drunken spree, but can’t raise sufficient
money to get really drunk, and on his return
home takes his revenge on the world by beat-
ing his little son. So the painful catalogue
goes on, a dreary record of futile and insig-
nificant lives, which James Joyce would have
us believe represent the lives of Dubliners.
Yet it is lit by rare touches of pathos and
innocence and beauty; and never once-does
the author drop his inhuman aloofness from
his poor puppets. If absolute objectivity be
an art it is a horrible art. But James Joyce
can write. His book ends appropriately
with this passage :
“Yes, the newspapers were right: snow
was general all over Ireland. It was falling
on every part of the dark central plain, on
the treeless lulls, falling softly upon the
Bog of Allen, and, farther westward, softly
falling into the. dark, mutinous Shannon,
waves. It was falling, too, upon every part
of the lonely churchyard on the hill where
Michael Furey lay buried. It lay thickly
drifted on the, crooked crosses and head-
stones, on the spears of the little gate, on the
barren thorns. His soul swooned slowly as
he heard the snow falling faintly through
the universe and faintly falling,' like the
descent of their last end, upon all the living
and the dead.”
A Portrait of the Artist as a. Young Man
took James Joyce 10 years to write. It has
all the appearance of an autobiographical
novel —though with Joyce one could never
really toll. It is the painfully frank story of
an Irishman from his childhood to his man-
hood. Joyce gets very near achieving the
impossible task that so often confronts an
author—to write one’s own life with a per-
fect candor, to tear ruthlessly aside, in the
interests of his art, those reticences and that
sense of decency without which social inter-
course, life itself, would not be possible. And
Joyce has almost dgne this most difficult
thing.
lie begins at the beginning: “Once upon a
time, and a very good time it was, there was
a moocow coming down along the road, and
this moocow that was down along the rbad
met a nicens little boy named baby
Kuckoo....
“His father told him that story: his father
looked at him through a glass: he had a
hairy face.
“He was baby Kuckoo. The moocow came
down the road where Betty Bryne lived: she
sold lemon platt.
“Oh, the wild rose blossoms
On the little green place.
“He sang that song. That was the song.
“Oh, the green wotlie botheth.
"When you wet the bed. first it is warm,
then it gets cold. liis mother put on the
oil-sheet. That had the queer smell.
“His mother had a nicer smell than his
father. She played on the piano the sailor’s
hornpipe for him to dance. He danced ”
Thus Joyce recalls his earliest memories.
And with the same pitiless realism he shows
the schoolboy.
“He wanted to cry. He leaned his elbows
on the table and shut and opened the flaps
of his ears. Then lie heard the noise of the
refectory every time he opened the flaps of
his ears. It made a roar like a train at
night. And when he closed the flaps the roar
was shut off like a train going into a
tunnel. . ..
“AVas it right to kiss his mother or wrong
to kiss his mother? What did that mean, to
kiss? You put your face up like that to
say good-night, and then his mother put her
face down. That was to kiss. His mother
put her lips on his cheek; her lips Were soft
and they wetted his cheek; find they made a
tiny little noise: kiss. Why did people do
that with their two faces?”
All the strange fancies of the child mind
are here, groping in a threatening, confusing
world for the meaning of things, and unable
to express itself except inarticulately. He
has his futile, innocent love-affair with a
little girl. After an entertainment she came
witli him to his tram : “He heard what her
eyes said to him from beneath their cowl,
and knew that in some dim past, whetheT in
life or revery, he had heard their tale before.
He saw her urge her vanities, her fine dress
and sash and long black stockings, and knew
that he had yielded to them a thousand
times. Yet a voice within him spoke above
the noise of his dancing heart, asking him
would he take her gift to which he had only
to stretch out his hand.... He stood list-
lessly in Ins place, seemingly, a tranquil
watcher of the scene before him.
“ ‘.She. too, wants me to catdi hold of her,’
lie thought. ‘That’s why she came witli me
to the tram. I could easily catch hold of
her: nobody is looking. I could hold her and
kiss her.’
“But he did neither: and, when he was
sitting alone on the deserted tram he tore
bis ticket into shreds and stared gloomily at
the corrugated footboard.”
Joyce goes deeper into the darker side of
childhood. This imaginative, precocious child
is tortured by his unworthiness and the ever-
present fear of Hell. And the priest gives
the class of schoolboys a picture of Hell that
is as grimly realistic as Joyce’s picture of
the mind of his hero. There are seven pages
of the preacher’s address- —a detailed and'
lurid tourist’s guide to Hades. The boy, in
terror, makes his confession, and mortifies
the flesh with an, amazing ingenuity. But
he relapses.
He tries to write poetry; he becomes a
student at the university ; he argues at in-
tolerable length with his fellows over religion
and love and life —-and the dullness of these
conversations is to the author just as impor-
tant as anything else that happens to his
hero. In the end we learn vaguely that
the youth will leave Ireland, though with
a mind still confused and inarticulate, and
with motives unexplained. His futile love-
affair peters out. The tremendous compel-
ling unrest of youth forces his unwilling feet
to wander.
“The spell of arms and voices; the white
arms of roads, their promise of close em-
braces and the black arms of tall ships that
stand against the moon, their tale of distant
nations. They are held out to say: ‘AYe are
alone—come.’ And the voices say with
them : ‘AVe are your kinsmen.’ And the air
is thick with their company as they call to
me, their kinsman, making ready to go, shak-
ing the wings of their exultant and terrible
youth.
“Mother is putting my new second-hand
clothes in order. She prays now, she says,
that I may learn in my own life and away
from home and friends what the heart is and
what it feels. Amen. So be it. AVel-
come, O life! Igo to encounter for the
millionth time the reality of experience and
to forge in the smithy of my soul the un-
created conscience of my race.”
If this is the Irish soul, Joyce has terribly
dissected it. If this book is a masterpiece,
it is a masterpiece of sordid realism, written
by a great literary artist, in a medium that
is painful and depressing. It is life depicted
with a pitiless clarity by an inhuman and
perfectly dispassionate pen.
Summer is a story of lighter, though still
sombre, texture. Edith AVliarton’s work is
always highly polished; anfl she can turn
you out a neat and graceful comedy or an
introspective New England tragedy without
a single split-infinitive or one adjective too
many. Though Summer has not the tre-
mendous impact of her Ethan Frome: it is
in the same key. Here she presents a soli-
tary girl in a lost New England village, over
which the mountain looms with a suggestion
of doom. For Charity had come from the
mountain, the home of a small community of
refugees and ' outcasts, whither even the
sheriff dare not penetrate. Charity, rescued
as a baby from a disreputable mother, is
brought up in the home of a derelict lawyer,
stranded in the desolate village. Enters the
stranger from- the great world outside. Fol-
lows the love affair, that promises so fairly
at first—if for si moment you forget the
shadow of the looming mountain.
“She had given him all she had —but what
was it compared to the other gifts life held
for him? She understood now the case of
girls like herself to whom this kind of thing
had happened. They gave all they had, but
their all was not enough : it could not buy
more than a few moments.”
The man goes away. The shadow* of the
mountain deepens; but at the last moment
Edith Wharton lightens it with a touch of
grey. Exquisite work this, in a locale Edith
Wharton has made her own. But somehow*
the reader misses the tragedy in his admira-
tion for the too-beautiful workmanship. -
Adela Punkhurst litis written a plaj r ,
Betrayed (Fraser and Jcnkinson, Melln, Is.
6d.). In live acts it relates the history of
an imaginary rebellion in Orry, obviously
Ireland, against the British Government.
Desultory lighting goes on until at last the
Orry capitalists, forming the Provisional
Government of Orry, arrange with the Brit-
ish for the concession of Ilome Buie —with
stipulations. The workers are to pay the
price; they are to be enslaved by their
Home Buie capitalist government. ' They
rebel and are beaten ; their leaders are exe-
cuted, and in the epilogue the workers are
seen cowed and enslaved, forced to compete
with cheap indentured Chinamen for their
living. The theme is absurd enough, out the
drama is non-existent. As a tract it may
no doubt aptly express the views of Adela
Pankhurst about England and Ireland ; but
it consists chiefly of elaborate speeches and
elaborate stage-directions. In one act there
is waged on the stage a rebellion in which
the workers’ army is defeated by the British
soldiers, and the Town Hall is shelled and
catches fire. In the next act the workers’
leaders are condemned to death, and each
makes long speeches from the dock. The
whole thing is a dull and vague account of
something that the autffor fails ever to make
real.

If Australian kiddies likq fairy stories
with a moral as obvious as a tank, then
Sulks (Irizzlegrumps , by Aldea Wane, will
please them; but this story, daintily illus-
trated by Dorothy Fry, hardly seems original
or pleasing enough, or its diction simple
enough, to merit the announcement that it
has been copyrighted in Canada and the
U.S.A.. and that dramatic, cinema and other
rights have been reserved.

An interesting publication is Generals of
the British Army, the first part of which has
been issued by Coustkv Life. London ; ss.
A dozen generals are here adequately pic-
tured in colored drawings from life by
Francis Dodd, and brief biographies are
attached. The most striking portrait is that
of General Byng. Generals Allenby, Bird-
wood and Smuts are also included.
Talking about Billjim’s reading matter,
the last publication one would expect to find a wel-
come in the trenches is a Government Gazette, But
this particular Gazette was the Barmaids’ Register,
issued last June, and containing the full names
of 2200 bar-ladies registered under new Vic.
licensing legislation. I sent a copy to a “hard-doer”
Billjim pal in France, and his letter of acknowledg-
ment has meandered home. “That Gazette was a
dinkum whiff of Home. Sweet Home,” he writes.
There has been a big demand for it amongst the
Vies. Even some of the T.T’s have a few favorites
to look up. The officers’ mess has a loan of it at
present.” The Register is a valuable guide to
parents with an unnamed girl babe, as it contains a
wide assortment of Christian names—fancy and
plain, descending from haughty Hyentha to humble
Hannah.
Frank Ilentv: The Australian writer for kids has
to contend with an influence which is not usually
considered when the writer is at work. As a rule,
the Australian youngster is a serious-minded reader.
Billy Bluegum, described as swinging on the eucalyp-
tus-bough in a sedate and decorous manner, creates
just as much interest as Billy wearing a broad but
unnatural grin. Aunt Jane, however, looking over
some books for Little Annie, seizes on Billy-with-the-
grin as “very nice,” and buys the book for its humor
—just the thing for a child, in her opinion. So
when the writer of a neat and simple story for
Annie and Willie takes her MS.‘to the publisher, the
dingbat in the swivcl-chai •, who usually has a
liumor-bump the size of a peanut, suggests that the
author should alter her work to include the broad
grin—not for the enjoyment of VVillie and Annie, but
to sell the book to Aunt Jane. 'This probably
acounts for the blithersomeness of so much Aus-
tralian kid-literature.

A valuable feature of the first part of
Vol. VII of J. H. Maiden’s The Forest-
Flora of N.S.'W, is an article upon our in-
digenous seeds and fruits. He would like to
see the purveyors of false seed “subjected to
the penalties of a Draconian law’’: and he
insists upon the importance of planting only
useful species in re-afforestation operations,
having seen plantations in Australia that,
owing to the wrong species having been
chosen, are now of no Value. .1. 11. Maiden
supplies useful hints for the germination of
seeds that, like those of the wattle, require
special treatment.

but doubtless small comfort, in the Index
to the War-Time Profits Tax Assessment
Act, compiled by Edwin V. Nixon and
Healey H. Redpath (Osboldstone, Melb.,
35.). This pamphlet also contains the full
texts of the Act and regulations.

poisoning.
Continuous self-poisoning in early years, not only
causes specific diseases, but undermines the health
to a degree that can never be repaired.
You can keep your children healthy and vigorous
by the use of Nujol. Nujol is easy to take and
relieves the most obstinate cases of constipation.
Nujol keeps the bowels clean and free from
disease-causing decayed foods and germs. It
acts as an internal lubricant, is not absorbed by
the body, does not form a “habit” and is harmless
in any quantity.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Summer.
Summer has a happy song
Blowing out of yellow places
Where the dimpled sunlight traces,
In the changing shadows strong,
Arabesques the whole day long
On the round hills’ happy faces,
And the patterns of old laces
Fall the merry vales along.
Sweet to lie on wind-blown grass
Making our own melody
In that summer land apart,
Wind and sun of dreams that pass
Changing still for you and me
The warm dimples of the heart!
n.s.w.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
Involution.
In you, as in a crystal clear
That shows revolving time and space,
I see each age of man appear
At once, before my face.
I hurt you with a little word ;
Red flames your cheek, your dear eyes
blaze —•
The savage from his stone-age stirred
At tiger-madness plays.
But quick to sweet remorse you spring,
Your warm, brown hands encompass me;
And to your iron strength I cling—
Courageous, firm and free.
Hope wakens at your earnest hold,
Rearing her citadel of brass,
Where gleaming banners beat and fo;d
Across a sea of glass.
And then you kiss me, and your love
Sings of the golden age of song,
Of stars and those large realms above
r r , A which we move along.
N.S.W.
ZORA CROSS.
The Traitor.
If I could wear a bandage o’er my eyes
As they say Love does, then my heart unwise
Would turn your honey-phrases into truth,.
And they would spring so lusty with their
youth
To storm the wall, and thus the breach
begin ;
My lips the traitor that would let you in!
Which were the best? To lonely be and wise,
Or, fumbling at the latch with bandaged
eyes,
To let the guardian Wisdom vanquished go,
And yield your castle to your dearest foe?
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Two Stars.
A low wind sighing passes,
And sighs and dies anew :
And all the flowers and grasses
Are wet with tears of dew.
The sea and land seem dreaming
Beneath that baleful star —
Hung high and fiercely gleaming—
Of wounds and death and war.
Yet sweet with calm that leavens
The hour and its repining,
Within the western heavens
The Star of Love is shining.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Washing Up.
A dingy, squalid suburb and a squalid, dingy
lane,
And a house to make your heart ache at
its twelve-and-six a week;
But the snow’s on Kosciusko, and I’ve snow-
shoes on again,
And above the splash of washing-up I hear
the mountains speak.
A savage old nor’-wester, frosty “sago” in
his hair.
Is roaring on the summit (he’s a dear old
pal o’ mine),
And the crockery goes crashing, but just
somehow I don’t care,
Though I bought the lot at auction with
a borrowed one-and-nine.
The urchins of the neighbors play their
marbles on the kerb
(They’re pals o’ mine in other moods for
all their frowsy dirt),
But the Gentian Patch on Twynam comes
upon me to disturb
With a knife of recollection that is good,
for all its hurt.
p-be Gentian Patch on Twynam, with the
Blue Lake in its lap.
And Muller’s Peak to nor’ard right across
the old moraine,
The Perisher and Paralyser misted through
the Gap
Where the Snowy River wriggles—but it
gives a chap a pain !
Just a silly sort of wistful sort of what-the-
hell’s-the-odds !
There’s work and washing-up to do, and
chops to buy and fry;
But the man who has a household and who
hasn’t household gods
Makes his life a sadder muddle just the
more he tries to try.
The next-door wop is cursing—she was once
a comely girl!
The kids are at their marbles —they’ll be
butcher-boys in time!
Well, the washing-up is over and my head’s
in such a whirl
And my heart’s beyond describing—so I’ll
finish off this rhyme.
MAORILAND.
BERNARD INGLEBY.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 17, 1918.
Price 6d.
A BALLAD OF REVOLUTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE King sat in Dumbfearful town,
Drinking the blood-red wine;
His brow was twisted to a frown,
His whiskers drooped like twine.
Without, the Revolution roared;
And orators in rows
Their, eager congregations bored
With loud, fantastic prose.
So well they talked, so long they spoke,
Their throats grew rough and sore;
And perspiration madly broke
From every glistening pore.
The veins upon their foreheads swelled;
Their hard-stretched collars burst;
No power could stay the words they
yelled
Till language died in thirst.
The Red Guards howled their fierce
applause
Each time a speaker failed;
Terrific curses filled each pause
And long the women wailed;
The warships fired a loud salute,
The sun went redly down;
And everybody cried for loot
In grey Dumbfearful town.
Oh, blood is redder far than wine,
And flame is far more hot!
(The Royal whiskers sagged like twine
Above the boozing pot.)
And loud without the clamor w 7 ent,
And shriller grew the din;
The discord of the firmament
Ra..g like cow-trodden tin.
And fiercely rolled the flood of speech
Like fifty streams in flood,
With i«fty lessons they must teach
To one low r bank of mud.
The red flags waved, the trumpets blew;
Word-scourged the people rose
Tremendous things to dare and do
Against their age-long foes.
They pulled the palace railings down,
They smashed the palace door,
They broke the throne and kicked the
crown
About the wash-house floor;
But greater, darker deeds than these
The Revolution knew.
(Oh, flags upon the battle-breeze!
The Guards were lame and few!)
Uprising, roaring like the tide,
They hurst each bond and bar.
The royal cat to music died
Wearing a cross and star.
Stern men they were, and unafraid
Of aught that life might bring—
They got a safety-razor blade
And smoothly shaved the King!
N.S.W.
CURSE O' MOSES.

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or
Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and
not exceeding two columns in length, or, say, 3000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper. Clippings. All communications ivill be
regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable IiIS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded-
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or icash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 17, 1918.
THE LAND OF MICAWBER
AUSTRALIS.
One of these days an unkind but not
wholly unreliable historian will write up
Australia as a land of silly children, who
played at prosperity and made a lamentable
mess of the grandest opportunity in all
history. The mess has been with us for
many years, and it grows worse. Australia,
in the glorious times of peace, when it had
no navy and hardly even a semblance of an
army to support, seldom made the two
ends of its public finance meet. It blewed
the best and most accessible of its mineral
riches and hardly ever made the two ends
meet. It realised in a wholesale fashion on
the public lands which cost it nothing, and
had the same old difficulty about making
ends meet. In fact, it didn’t want to make
them meet. It got into a strange baby-like
habit of regarding solvency as a sin. So
it kept taxes down to a minimum and floated
big loans with which to build public works,
and the revenue derived from them, taken
as a whole, being less than the expenditure
on working and maintenance, ends didn’t
meet there either. One of New South
Wales’s big tin gods, George Reid of imme-
morial stomach measurement, did these
three things at practically one and the
same time: (1) Manufactured a small sur-
plus by charging about £700,000 of this
year’s loan interest to next year; (2)
reduced the already inadequate Customs
tariff to the loss of revenue, the discourage-
ment of local industry and the great
encouragement of imports of German and
other foreign goods; (3) charged even the
new cups and saucers for the Jenolan accom-
modation-house to loans. In his own State
the foolish children who were playing at
prosperity regarded Reid as a sort of deity.
And he got vast newspaper support, as dul
other blatant and roaring time-servers like
him. For many years The Bulletin was
the only paper of any note in Australia
which preached the anti-borrowing doctrine,
and it did so amid the pitying smiles of the
foolish children, who said that this was a
neiv country” and “everything would come
right ” Even the immortal Micawber,
whom all readers of Dickens recognise as
a humorous and enchanting ass, had sense
enough to know that a constant deficit in
the accounts leads to disaster. Australia
in the aggregate knows less than Micawbeb,
and has been in that condition for 50 years
and more. The artist is hereby urged to
draw a new Australia —one with short, tight
pants, low shoes, a shabby, brief frock-coat
and a jaunty but superannuated plug hat
on one side of a hairless head. That Aus-
tralia, charging madly at the German
trenches in Flanders, would be a strange
but truthful mixture of boundless valor and
unlimited lOU.
In New South Wales the first Labor Gov-
ernment clambered into office on a promise
that it would reduce the State’s loan expen-
diture, then standing at £1,500,000 a year.
Instead it ran the thing up to £5,000,000,
then £7,000,000, then £9,000,000 a year. It
is waste of energy, however, to call any
politician a liar, a scoundrel, an impostor,
a deceiver, a Judas who sold the com-
munity in order to buy votes, a shameless
fraud, an uncertificated political insolvent,
a political card-sharper and confidence man.
a corrupt agent for the importers of
foreign goods, a stipendiary enemy of his
people, a shortsighted Juggins or any
words to that effect. All the States acquired
a fresh and invigorated borrowing mania at
about the same time, and so long as that
quaint and amusing creature Micawbeb
Australis approved, nothing could be done.
But the original Micawbeb had David
Copperfiei.d’s aunt to fall back upon, and
the aunt paid his debts. We havq only our
uncle to fall back upon, and he wants us to
pay him. Which is a difference.
» * * *
All these things happened, be it remem-
bered, in a time of profound peace, for the
war expenses are a Federal and not a State
affair. And they are still happening in a
time of profound State peace. The tran-
quillity was so profound that of the six
States five showed an increased revenue
for the last half-year (Victoria had an
increase of £543,911 and South Australia
of £335,080 for the half-year, and only New
South Wales announced a small decrease).
Yet the three States which publish expen-
diture accounts all had appalling deficits—
New South Wales one of about £1,000,000,
which is somewhat striking for a six-
months’ effort; Queensland over £370,000;
Westralia over £457.000. For some reason
unknown Wilkins Australis selected this
time of all others for a special razzle of
expenditure, though what exactly he bought
with the. money doesn’t appear. And he
also borrowed all the money he could get
for public works. It is hinted that the
States won’t be able to keep faith with the
Commonwealth in the matter of repaying
the first instalment of certain advances.
What will happen at present interest rates
in the renewal of certain other big loans
that are soon falling due is a mystery.
When certain States with the biggest
revenues per head on earth fail to any tiling
like meet even their current expenses. l«t
alone make some provision for debts
which they are pledged to provide for, some
people would obscurely and basely hint that
they are drunk; some would suggest u
guised insolvency; the great, aggregate
Micawber Australis merely holds to the
faith that this is a new country and every-
thing will come right. Nobody who is
numerous enough to be anybody suggests
that there should be a strenuous reforma-
tion, and that the Premier and Treasurer
who fail to make ends meet as certified by
the Government Auditor should be fired out
of office by the said Government Auditor,
and disqualified as members or Ministers
for a period proportioned to the size of
their offence. It might be a new departure
but it would be a logical one. Making ends
meet is what the Premier and Treasurer are
paid for, and if they don’t do their work
they are collecting salary under bogus pre-
tences.

Affairs both State and Federal are being
run just now on lines which suggest Arte-
mus Ward's circus, and his prophetic kanga-
roo, described as “an amoosin’ cuss.” The
Federal Prime Minister, being struck with
an egg at Warwick (one egg in 670,500
square miles of country) has established a
special Federal police force. The State of
Queensland and the Premier thereof are taking
expensive law proceedings against the Prime
Minister about less than nothing in particu-
lar. The Premier has even demanded, at the
public expense, that the Prime Minister be
run in. Australia has had two referendums
and a general election in quick succession to
decide a Defence matter which Parliament is
paid to decide in any case. The referendums
said “No” and the election said “Yes,” and
the community said it was a great joke and
that there was nothing like it in “The
Mikado.” The Queensland Government pro-
poses to start a life assurance office, and the
New South Wales Government has a vision
of buying up all the coal-mines in the State
and paying for them with its lOU. The
Federal after nearly two years’
consideration of the wheat difficulty, proposes
to build two 5000-top ships to be finished in
about a year. The manager of State Ship-
yards is in consultation with the Common-
wealth Government .shipbuilder, and “pre-
liminary work” is to start shortly. A cable-
gram has been sent to England asking for
advice, and the president of the Inter-State
Shipbuilding Confeience is aiso g.vin,'
advice. Norway is building 127 ships, and
one single shipyard at Seattle (U.S.A.) is
building 20—only one yard among many. It
recently got together an 8000-ton ship in 64
working days. If Hughes was one-tenth as
prompt over ships as he was over that miser-
able egg at Warwick he would have a whole
fleet by this time. A British Minister has
now explained clearly—what most people
guessed long ago—that John Bull isn’t
sending any vessels to Australia for wheat so
long as Canada and the United States can
fill his ships. He hasn’t many wheat ships
available just now, and as they can do three
times the work on the short run to North
America as on the long run to Australia we
are out of the business unless we supply our
own craft. The Argentine Republic and
Africa will also get a preference. And our
wool and meat business may strike a similar
snag unless this country gets to work
at shipbuilding—cheap, hastily-constructed
wooden clippers would do —also unless
Hughes can forget his egg, and his Federal
police, and his penny dignity, and his shindy
with Ryan, and his craving to make hun-
dreds of long speeches, and his talking habits
in the press, and his buzzing-around habits,
and his kittenish custom of rushing wildly at
nothing with his coat-tail flying in the wind.
The State Premiers might, of course, do
something, but they are all too much like
Hughes.
* * * *
Ferhaps it will require a national insol-
vency to wake up the joyous Wilkins
Micawber of Australia to a knowledge of the
general decay of things. The one great satis-
faction is that Australia is still here. If the
talkative politicians in this land of unlimited
public conversation could have dug it up and
sold it piecemeal in London it wouldn’t be
here.
MRS. GRUNDY: A WORD FOR THE
OLD LADY.
Nowhere in the world has a statue been set
up to Mrs. Grundy. Liberty’s torch flames
high in the great republics, and kings in
marble and bronze look inanely over monar-
chical flower-beds and lawns ; but the emblem
of public opinion is a mockery on the lips of
the peoples—a thing to deride in shallow
epigram. Mrs. Grundy has her weaknesses
and her follies. Her eyes are too close
together, and she grasps her umbrella with
a matronly vigor a little too uncompromis-
ing ; but she is a mighty force, and a force
that in the main works towards the righteous-
ness of a fair deal. She is the power behind
the policeman, the person who puts the grip-
ping force into law, the essential strength of
all religious and moral systems, and to a
very large extent the maker of nations and
moulder of men. That she sometimes appears
narroiv and sour is due largely to the fact
that for all her splendid effort she has only
been paid with scorn and laughter; but
exalted to her true positon, honored and wor-
shipped at her true worth and crowned above
the lesser gods, she is capable of smiling
blandly, and substituting a loving and deli-
cate warning for her reproving frown and
determined interference with personal liberty.
Consider the great god Liberty with the
smoky torch and tinsel wreath as she stands
in the light of her works. She looks calm
enough as the artist for the settled republic
carves her; but how many times has she run
amok in the streets, her tangled hair flying
savagely over her half-naked shoulders, an
alcoholic madness in her eyes and her
shapely feet splashing blood to the skirts of
her ragged robe! Admitting that her assault
on the respectability of indecency and the
shames of ordered society were quite jus-
tified, who was it that roused her, lighted
her torch with the taper of hope and pointed
her road to victory? in every case the credit
of her awakening was due to Mrs. Grundy
—that plain, matronly personification of
public opinion who felt that she could stand
even the tantrums of a raging and hysterical
fury better than the smug hypocrisy of an
old well-dressed oppression. Again, when
Liberty had done her dash and, scarlet to the
knees with blood, could see nothing but blood
and more blood on her destroying course,
who arrested her progress, led her into quiet
places, put her sword away to be cleaned
and generally sobered her up and took charge
of her? In every case Mrs. Grundy— Mrs.
Grundy with a Madonna’s compassion in
her eyes for the bitter sufferings that she
saw around her. No one has wrought more
lasting service to the race of man than she.
She has put her mouth to the trumpet of
progress and blown great blasts that were
heard over land and sea; she has fought
tremendous wars and striven nobly for
peace; she has made kings and emperors and
tossed them again on the scrapheap. She
has a lot of minor mistakes to answer for;
but they were generally mistakes which were
due to some grinning jester with fate who
threw dust in her eyes. Judged by her
works, great and small, she has generally
been on the right side. Right or wrong, her
heart was always for decency and truth ; and
that is a lot more than can be said for the
rest of the minor gods.
In the tremendous world-changes that are
now impending, there are evidences that pub-
lic opinion is to take a much bigger pmce in
the scheme of things than it was allowed to
hold in the past. According to the Allies’
ideas, all communities are to be allowed the
right to choose their mode of government
and the big neighbors under whose wing and
protection they desire to live. Side by side
with this is the curious Russian develop-
ment which indicates a pretty wide-spread
desire to live under practically no govern-
ment at all. The very army of that amazing
jumble, which rightly styles itself a revolu-
tion rather than a republic, is to be of even
rank, nobody wearing so much as a stripe,
with the commander-in-chief himself issuing
advice rather than orders. Such a military
machine looks insane; but, eliminating war
itself from the programme, the application of
the idea to civil affairs may have more wis-
dom than those who snort at it suppose.
Somewhere in Gibbon’s history there is a
passage which describes the Slavonians as
living in three or four thousand villages of
the country that is now Russia unchanged
and undispossessed through all changes and
tribal upheavals of surrounding peoples. Pos-
sibly in the genius of the Russian peoples
lies a capacity for practically living without
governments and getting on just as well;
and possibly there is an underlying truth
which Russia may have to teach all peoples
that there has been a great deal too much
government in the world. But the only
thing which can take the place in any degree
of the governmental machine is public
opinion; and it is towards the permanent
enthronement of Mrs. Grundy that the new
movement works if it is going anywhere at
all. That the old lady would be a pretty
severe despot goes without argument; but
' her despotism would be inexpensive, and
after the tremendous war extravagance that
is at least a point in her favor. Clearly the
whole question of the value of the police-
man and the lawyer and the tax-collector, as
well as of the warship, the soldier and the
powder-magazine, is now under review, and
the thought of the people is being clarified
by prayer and fasting—particularly the fast-
ing. That in countries long accustomed to
all the machinery which they have deemed
essential to the communal life—apd especi-
ally in great cities where the real princi-
ples of living have been long obscured —it
seems plain enough that a good deal of the
old mechanism must remain ; but the average
man all over the world is asking himself
to-day the value of the return he has been
in the habit of receiving for the large part of
his earnings which has flowed into the vari-
ous Government treasuries. In Russia most
of the people seem to have decided that they
have been getting less than nothing; in other
lands the matter is still being debated.
» * * *
It is plain, even to people who know
nothing at all about political economy, that
the cultivation of a very few acres of good
land will yield a family all that it absolutely
requires, in return for the expenditure of a
very little toil: it is equally evident that
there are possibilities of labor co-operation
in any community which may lift the aver-
age wealth of the people to a point far
greater than any that had been reached in
any country before the war. If the existing
machines of government could be used for
the purpose of furthering that co-operation
and creating that wealth, they would obvi-
ously be worth while ; but hitherto Govern-
ments have devoted themselves to tasks
which in the main have not made the people
better off than if each man had worked his
few acres to the best advantage for the
benefit of his family without paying any
taxation or seeking the protection of any
Government at all. The inference would
seem to be that government should be re-
duced to the minimum, that unhampered by
any heavy imposts and free from compli-
cated legislation, each individual should earn
a living in bis own way, and that only thus
is the human co-operation likely to be
created which will make greater wealth and
a higher average of comfort. In a word, the
existing machinery is not only far too com-
plicated for its use, but is so bulky and
clumsy that it takes up the space of the
other machinery which is so badly needed,
and exhausts, by its dead weight, the avail-
able motive power. This being so, the big
question that has to be answered is whether
the plain force of public opinion, loosely ex-
ercised, will sufficiently safeguard the rights
of citizens and free them from bondage to
a machine which retards their real progress.
The answer to the question in a country like
Australia would seem to be found in the
little towns and scattered, rural settlements
where, in proportion to population, most real
work is done. The big towns are largely out
of the economic picture. By a better adjust-
ment of tasks three-fourths of their popula-
tion could be removed without altering their
wealth-producing value to the country as a
whole. But the small towns and scattered
settlements receive scarcely anything from
the Government in return for their contribu-
tions to the revenue. Law, in the stricter
sense, scarcely touches them, the policeman
is hardly seen there, public expenditure does
not come within many miles of them; yet
the people get on quite as \freU as if they
were gripped hard in the cogs m the govern-
ment machine. In these places Mrs. Grundy
is in a sense queen, public opinion keeps the
population in step, and that same public
opinion might make for large co-operation
and progress were it not that the Govern-
ment takes the cash and is vaguely supposed
to be doing something which it never does.
The conclusion to which the citizen viewing
these things is forced is that Australia is
very much over-governed. True, in the most
outlying places a man will be hanged if he
commits murder and put in gaol if he steals;
but all this could be managed by a few hefty
local citizens acting under stress of public
opinion just as effectively as by the central-
ised authority and without the moral inter-
vention of 14 Houses of Legislature and the
vast official armies of retainers that sur-
round them. More than this is true, for if
the habit of relying upon the supposed work
of all that vast and complicated burden we
call politics were got rid of there are lots of
other things the local Mrs. Grundy would
attempt for the general good, such as seeing
to the local health, making the local educa-
tion a reality instead of the sham it is,
arranging things on a permanent basis with
the local land monopolist, and telling the
local objectionable to wander away out into
the wilderness and plant himself where there
were no neighbors to offend. But public
opinion will not operate successfully over
very large areas. A Hughes, as Prime Min-
ister, may pledge himself not to continue in
office if a referendum vote goes wrong, and
then ignore his pledge; but if Hughes were
merely the head man of the village he could
do nothing like that. Public opinion would
hold him to his word and compel him to be
as honest as any other citizen. Old Mrs.
Grundy has great work still to do; and
given a chance, she will do it. Under the
local sway of her umbrella-sceptre Australia
might get the district pride which is the
basis of national patriotism, and the district
love of work which is the best basis for
national industry.
THE DEGRADATION OF THE
COMMON WEALTH .
If when he asseverated his intention to
stand or fall by the vote on the Conscription
issue, Hughes had wanted to be reasonably
honest, he would have put it this way:
I tell you plainly the Ministry must have this
power. Unless the Government is given this power
it cannot govern the country, and will not attempt
to do so. Instead, it will go out and come back
again in five minutes prepared to allege that it can
do what it said it could not do and ready to attempt
what it declared that it would not try.
Then there would have been a wild yeb of
derision from Cairns to Ivalgoorlie, and all


the cards being on the table the democracy
could have voted with certainty of getting a
fair deal. The reason Hughes stopped
before arriving at the last sentence or - two
was that he never yet got into the habit
of failing to-secrete an ace up his sleeve. It
is passible, of course, that not a single citizen
was deceived by the pledge. This, indeed,
stands as the one sound justification that
can be advanced by Hughes and the Phew-
sion for their persistence in barricading
themselves in the pantry—that the public
knew they were liars and that they have only
done what people expected them to do. It
requires some hide to make this defence, but
the performance of last week can be inter-
preted in no other words.
By no voluntary act will Hughes or Cook
or any of the gang ever surrender the well-
paid jobs they have their clutches on. Until
they are booted off the premises at a general
election they will hang on, whether wars be
won or lost, whether our army is reinforced
or not. The tragedy of the thing is that
honest and decent men and women, genuinely
anxious to see Australia play her part in the
war with resolute dignity, should have had
to associate themselves with the half-de-
mented and wholly-unreliable little twister
who degrades the public spirit of the Com-
monwealth every day of his life, and never
more effectively than during the last few
weeks. That he is now on the back of the
Liberals, clinging to the party organisation
like an Old Man of the Sea, is a circum-
stance for which Joe Cook is entitled to
most of the discredit. That he is even in
the picture of Australian affairs is a mourn-
ful incident in the national history, and a
sinister reminder of what can be accom-
plished under Party Government. Viewed
in this sense Hughes, hanging to office as
tightly as a dead ’possum to the limb of a
tree, is a spectacle not altogether unwelcome
if it stirs the public to a realisation of the
fly-blown materials they are depending upon
for the salvation of their democracy. Other-
wise the spectacle is revolting. The moral
sense of a man who would go through such
hocus-pocus, imagining that by this grotesque
exhibition he was keeping faith with the
people, is clearly less than that of a troglo-
dyte. A politician who would prosecute a
citizen for making “a false statement,” while
his mind was framing a proclamation of his
own unmeasurable capacity for twisting
words and evading pledges is too contempt-
ible for polite words.
What purpose was served by the farce of
going out and hopping in again, Lleaven
alone knows, unless it was to let the public
see how elastic the conscience of the Phew-
sion is and how hungry the whole crowd is
for office. Instead of redeeming the pledge
to resign the dodge merely accentuated the
breach thereof, blow the Liberal caucus
which sanctioned this monkeying with
decency came to imagine that the net result
would be anything else than to cause dis-
gust among the lookers-on is a problem The
Bulletin is not going to worry about. Nor
is The Bulletin much concerned about the
effect of the dodge having been to keep
Tudor out of the Promised Land. At the
best, under the system prevailing, Tudor
would have been an interloper who had
snehked in through a hole in the fence and
had either to run away or be arrested for
trespass. But though he has no substantial
grievance as a partisan, he should most cer-
tainly have been given assistance by the
Liberals to carry on until a general election
cleaned up the mess. It was the moral duty
of the Hughes Government to resign instead
of making a pretence thereat. It was the
constitutional duty of the Phewsion to pre-
vent a party representing but a minority
from putting on airs; and it was, and is, the
duty of the lot to get back to the electors as
expeditiously as possible. The position now
is an outrage upon self-government and a
menace to Parliament, The Legislature has
been turned into a two-up school where the
coins have heads on both sides. An election
there must be, if the existence of the Com-
monwealth is to escape the danger of clamor
for dismemberment and Parliament is to be
rescued from the log-rolling abyss it has
fallen into.
GERMAN NEW GUINEA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Germans of this territory do not recog-
nise the possibility of the defeat of their
nation in Europe. Their cocksureness as
to the ultimate results is profoundly impress-
ive. Their merchants are pushing their trade
for all they are worth and making more
money than ever they did under their own
rule, an admission they freely grant; the
planters, traders and recruiters are almost
feverishly developing, trading and recruit-
ing ; and not one of them but will laugh
to scorn the idea of the German territory
one day belonging to England, or, more
directly, to the Commonwealth.
The suggestion of the last prospect sends
any German into a state of hysterical
laughter, for the contempt the average Hun
here has for the Australian is amazing.
Australia they covet as the crowning jewel
of the German colonial Empire they have
planned long since, and of which they are
as confident to-day as they were in pre-war
days. They look upon Australians not as
a nation of men, but, as one said, “a muck-
heap of human beings, dumped out of
Europe and elsewhere, lacking every qualifi-
cation that makes for national strength or
for commercial advantage”; or, as another
said, “a people that has no future, that
lives from day to day, asking big wages and
breaking the promises of to-day to ask for
lugger wages to-morrow”; or, as a third
put it, “so full of strife among tnemseUes
that when the time comes we will lap up
Australia as a dog laps up milk.” There
is not the least doubt that Australia is the
magnet of German aspiration, and when
they 40 years ago bluffed British statesmen
into allowing them the possession of Ger-
man New Guinea, they then clearly saw
the strategic importance of the step—saw
that a great deal more clearly than they
realised the great richness and immense
possibilities of New Guinea. That territory
has been a watch tower, as it were, and not
one German official but has studied Aus-
tralia with the one ambition —to lay hands
on the land.
The decision in the first referendum on
conscription is still spoken of in German
New Guinea as proof of Australian insensi-
bility to the dangers of the future. They do
not gloat over the failure as an indication
that Australians are in sympathy with them
or desire an urgent peace at any price ; they
despise them rather. The following, taken
from a German magazine, written by that
amiable person, Maxmilian Harden, will
show the German mind in this matter. The
magazine was the Zukunft, picked up
from a German planter’s table: —
We have the greatest contempt possible for any
human to possess for the Australian, doing as little
work as he can, spending his substance in con-
cubinage, in sport, in gambling (which is his
religion) and in drinking. We who can claim to
be honest laugh at the Quixotic bravery of the Aus-
tralian whose enthusiasm carried him away to face
our Gon-led army.. . .We will break the hearts of
unionists for all time. We are gratified at the
success of our agents in Australia and the power
of German gold. Those Germans who, like Judas,
have turned against the Fatherland in its hour of
trial will be suitably dealt with. Those who have
stood firm and are now interned will be placed over
their captors and in high administrative positions.
....The Australians are the most ignorant people in
the wide world. They cannot speak or write cor-
rectly the British language. Our propaganda in the
land of the Southern Cross has been most successful.
... .We will scatter them over the face of the earth,
whilst we will retain in Australia their women to
be mothers of a new German race in the great
Pacific.
The first referendum produced those re-
marks so full of love and goodwill. It will
be interesting to rpad German magazines on
the last decision.
There is not one planter’s, trader’s or
merchant’s home but has a library> and
always you will find volumes on Australia,
its people, its trade and its resources. On
the walls will be found maps and pictorial
encyclopaedias, and these will show the latest
extensions of railway lines and roads;
varying colors denote farming, pastoral and
mining districts; figures show acreage, num-
bers of stock, and prospective mineral
wealth. These people know more of Aus-
tralia than the average Australian. And
they believe it to be a more desirable place
to possess.
It seems to be unrealised by most Aus-
tralians that German New Guinea is still
owned by Germany and governed by Ger-
man laws. The Australian military admin-
istration is merely in occupation, and with
very limited powers. The terms of this
occupation, assented to by England, are
honoi-ably fulfilled. In themselves they do
not carry any implication whatever that
eventually the territory will pass into Aus-
tralian hands. The Germans never con-
template such a change. Lloyd George’s
idea of a referendum of the natives, as to
the disposal of the territory, should the
Allies triumph, is, of course, a queer piece
of absurdity. But in that connection it is
well to remember that the Germans, even
the German missionaries, have never ceased
to impress on the natives that one day the
German Governor will come back. Cer-
tainly this is an offence, and several Ger-
mans have been sent to the N. S. Wales
internment camp for it; but it still goes on,
and Lloyd George’s referendum might have
a surprising result.
There has been some criticism of the
Australian administration in German New
Guinea. But usually it has been traceable
to personal grievance. Certainly promo-
tions are of the comic-opera kind: the
humble lieutenant goes to his cot at night
to find himself in the morning on the high
road to an administrative field-marshal-
ship. But in all fairness it is impossible to
find serious fault with the official record of
Samuel Pethebridge, the Administrator.
When it is considered that this administrator
—-Australian born and trained, by the way—
on his arrival in German New Guinea found
chaos such as might and would have dis-
mayed many a capable man; found revenues
and their channels destroyed; ’ found the
Germans sullen, treacherous, and attempt-
ing risings almost every day for months;
found trade smashed, and the whole pros-
pects of the territory so blighted that it did
not seem worth an effort to restore it—-
when these things are remembered and the
position to-day is considered, there can be
no question that good work has been done. Not
only has every previous channel of revenue
been restored, but new ones have been added,
until the annual revenues are practically
double what they ever were in the German
days. These revenues carry on the territory
and entail no direct charge on the Common-
wealth for administration. By tact, combined
with firmness, the Administrator has
settled definitely the restlessness of the
Germans; they admit they are getting a
fair deal, such as they might expect under
British military occupation. People in
criticism of the Administration have
demanded the internment of all Germans.
They have made that demand because they
have not understood the terms of the
occupation.
There is also this feature of the admin-
istration that adds lustre to it: not only was
order gotten from chaos, but the Administra-
tion was formed from a medley of men, of
all ages, ranks and qualifications—mostly
middle-aged to old men; bankers and
laborers, doctors and shop-assistants. Yet
every department to-day is showing ability
and working smoothly. Australians may be
proud of their first efforts at military ad-
ministration.
I cannot forbear to specially mention the
work of Colonel (Dr.) Strangman, whose
disappearance in the Matunga has been the
cause of much regret. He had waged war
against malaria with such success that
Rabaul, the chief centre of the German terri-
tory. is now one of the cleanest and safest
parts of all the Pacific. I add to this
praiseworthy effort the backing the' Admin-
istration has given to Colonel Strangman’s
ideals by compelling the Germans all over
the territory to provide better conditions for
health-preserving, especially for the benefit
of native workers. In the same direction the
Administration is employing itself to the
lessening of the awful ravages of venereal
disease among the natives; and on this score
alone if '+ f>ad to retire to-morrow it would
do so with honor.
From my personal point of view the weak
spot in the Administration is the sudden and
undue amount of freedom that is allowed the
native. This has already put him out of
hand, and promises to ruin him. Unin-
structed British public opinion demands,
however, that the native must be pampered,
and this influences the Administration; but
a day is coming when we shall grieve over
the results.
THOS J. McMAHON.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Speech from the Dock.
Challenged by a direct motion of No Con-
fidence when the Reps, met on Friday last,
Hughes was compelled to make whatever
justification he had for the betrayal of the
pledge given in what he himself had declared
to be “the most critical hour of the national
life.” And when he faced the job Hughes
was speaking, not to Tudob nor to the Legis-
lature, but to the people of Australia. It is
to them the pledge was made. It is they
whose judgment in this business counts. For
that reason all that Hughes had to say
about the character of Tudob’s indictment
was without point. The pledge was not one
as betiveen factionists or partisans. It
mattered nothing whether Tudob or the
Labor party generally has acted or spoken
foolishly or wisely. What did matter was
whether Hughes had a plausible excuse for
a deliberate act of evasion. And what was
certain when he finished was that he had
none. He simply said: “Here I was. Here
I am. Here I stick.” There is not a single
word in the long, frothy rigmarole to excul-
pate either himself or his party from the
charge of conduct so base as to be without
parallel in the political history of the Com-
monwealth. Instead, we now have it that
the pledge was an imposture from birth. The
allegation that the government could not be
carried on unless he and his friends proved
their treachery is a manifest untruth. A
new Ministry could easily have proceeded
with the contract if Hughes and his Minis-
ters had been honest men instead of greedy
partisans. The invitation to “collaborate”
with men who had been denounced as liars
and German agents could only come from a
man who supposed that the moral sense of
Australia had been banished to the garbage-
tip. From the day that Hughes succeeded
Fisiieb The Bulletin warned Australia
what to expect. It knew the man. It is
satisfied that everybody else does now.
—♦
Maoriland’s Noblest Borrower.
In Maoriland Treasurer Joe Wabd has a
certain popular regard as “a clever finan-
cier” and “a great man with figures”—a
reputation not difficult to live up to in a
community of which about 90 per cent, takes
its arithmetic on trust, while the remainder
is satisfied to help itself to the gravy.
Giving the adjective an offensive meaning
Joe is a clever financier. He is a boomster
whose policy for 20 years has been the pro-
motion of “prosperity”—for banking insti-
tutions, landlords and mortgagees. But if
financial ability be interpreted to imply
some acquaintance with the economic
thought of the world and a comprehension
of the relationship between public and pri-
vate finance and the social and national
problems of his generation, it is a solemn
fact that Joseph Wabd is worth less than
2d. The man simply has no ideas, and to
read one of his public utterances is to
become bogged in a mass of words exasper-
atingly barren of meaning. His remarks
about war loans and taxation were until
lately entitled to first prize for banality;
but what he had to say the other day about
the exemption of war loans from income tax
beats them. Asked to defend the policy of
exemption and to show how John Gkice,
chairman of the National Bank of Aus-
tralia, was wrong in combating this policy,
Wabd fired off a long rigmarole of which
the essential points were: (1) That the
position was difficult; (2) that money had
to be obtained to win the war; (3) that
most people had not been called upon to
contribute anything to the war expenditure
except through a duty of 3d. a pound on
tea; (4) that this contribution did not go
towards paying for the war, anyway, but to
increase old age pensions; (5) that if in-
come tax was charged on revenue from war
loans the rate of interest would have to be
increased, whereupon all interest rates
would go up, and that would be the begin-
ning of a financial panic. Most of this is,
of course, irrelevant. The rest of it is
inaccurate. The whole of it is twaddle.
Floating tax-free loans at 4-1 per cent, does
not mean that interest is “kept down.” It
means that the interest rate available to
large investors is substantially increased.
Compared with the proceeds of an invest-
ment subject to taxation the yield from a
tax-free investment at 4J per cent, goes
up to 5, 6 and 7 per cent, according to the
income derived—and it is the big investors
whose money makes the millions. Wabd
does not justify this. Nor does he attempt
to meet the further objections to this sur-
reptitious nourishment of Fat —that the
removal of a huge volume of Capital invest-
ment from the area of taxation is a gross
outrage upon all other taxpayers, and that
the presentation of a bond free not only
from the taxation of to-day, but immune
from the taxation of to-morrow, is in time
of war a scandalous act of preference. It
means a gift of something that is not only
valuable at the moment but of incalculable
value in the future. The result must inevit-
ably be to make every addition to the
taxation on income earned subsequently by
other people have the effect of increasing
the capital value of the tax-free investment,
increase the return therefrom and throw a
greater and greater burden upon those who
work for the special benefit of those who
do not. There is no getting away from
this. There iS.no argument by which such
folly—if folly it redly is, and not a mere
trick —can be excused.
The Mystery about Coal.
The time is a bit more than ripe for the
N. S. Wales Government to make a full
statement of its relations with the Newcastle
coal-owners, its position wdtli reference to
mines that are being “worked by the Govern-
ment’’ and the terms of any contracts or
business understandings entered into between
the Treasury and colliery proprietors. Ex-
cept for a few bald assertions regarding “con-
trol” made during the strike the public Ims
been told nothing about what has been going
on. The Bulletin can conceive only one
reason for secrecy, and that is an even more
powerful reason for publicity. It is not the
habit of Governments to work in holes and
corners unless something is happening that
they are not anxious to let the public hear
about. And when Governments are detected
doing this the public is generally justified in
taking the precaution to hold its nose. The
latest occurrence in connection with the
Newcastle mines calls for an immediate lift-
ing of the curtain. After a visit to N. S.
Wales by the Victorian Minister of Mines
Downward, it transpired that a contract
had been entered into with the Holman
Government to supply Victoria with
1,000.000 tons of coal annually for five years
from the Pelaw and Richmond Main mines,
owned by J. and A. Brown, at 15s. a ton
f.o.b. The price agreed upon was exorbitant.
No tenders were called for supplies from
other mines. To carry out the contract
shipping would have had to be commandeered
to shift Brown’s coal. And the result of
turning 1,000,000 tons out of the Brown
mines would have been to throw other mines
idle and start a rumpus, and probably a
strike, over the number of shifts. There
was, of course, a yell, the contract was re-
jected by the Victorian Ministry, and
George Fuller. N.S.W. Chief Secretary,
was heard remarking that “he did. not under-
stand” why the agreement had been annulled.
Now, there are obviously many features
about this business which should not be sup-
pressed, the first and most important being
the general connection between J. and A.
Brown and the N. S. Wales Government
and the arrangement relating to the particu-
lar deal under notice. If it is part of a
“Win-the-War” Ministry’s policy to achieve
victory by enriching Brown, the electors
ought to hear about it. And. incidentally,
they ought to be told whether a further part
of that policy is to promote strikes.

The Everlasting Libel-monger.
There are people in Australia to whom the
attractiveness of stinking fish seems eternal.
If they can find even the shadow of an
excuse for raising a yelp about the “selfish-
ness” or the “godlessness” or the “immoral-
ity” of the Australian people they start
howling in assured confidence of getting
the noise repeated in the newspapers. There
was an illustration of this last week in the
parsonical orations incidental to a Week of
Prayer conducted in a Sydney hall. _ If the
reports of the proceedings are a fair guide
to what happened it would appear that
most of the time was occupied in listening
to disordered declamation about the
“uncleanliness” of the local beaches, the
prevailing habit which leads to every citi-
zen being drunk all the week and under the
table on Sundays, the perils of illegitimacy,
and so on. It all seems like a description
of Sodom and Gomorrah. And these wild
tirades, delivered without either sense of
proportion or restraint, were apparently
listened to without protest. One individual
in an inky suit sprang in a frenzied moment
to the front with this declamatory roar: —
Selfishness is everywhere. God has given us the
best land under Heaven, and from the highest to
the lowest in our land we are stamped with selfish-
ness. We must give. We must replace selfishness
with sacrifice. Australia must take herself and
fling herself into the lap of God. and, from the
Government to the humblest of the people, serve
God in the spirit of sacrifice. Australia must save
her national soul. ‘ .
What in the name of Heaven is the meaning
of a burst of fevered language like this?
Where does it lead to? What does it sug-
gest in the way of individual behavior and
collective conduct? The Bulletin leaves,
the conundrum to someone else. But in
the meantime it would solemnly urge the
next parson who feels called upon to testify
before the Lord and the reporters to take a
firm grip of himself and have a look at the
landscape through the right end of the tele-
scope. Then perhaps he may find out that
it is grotesquely untrue to speak of Aus-
tralian men and women as if they were an
immoral, drunken lot of wasters. To deny
that there is immorality both in the conven-
tional sense and in the wider sphere of
human relationship would be absurd. But
it would be more absurd to imagine that
Australia is singularly unfortunate in this
respect or that the war against the evil
features of society is not waged as strenu-
ously by Australians as by any other people
on earth. The evils of drunkenness and
prostitution are admitted by everyone, but
they are not removed or even palliated by
merely yelling about them or treating them
as isolated phenomena. To a large extent
the problems raised by them are the prob-
lems of the slum, of the industrial system,
of education and of heredity. And who is
there can justly say that in Australia the
struggle to raise the standard of living, of
thought and of conduct has been carried on
with less enthusiasm than elsewhere? To
say that “selfishness” is lurking about the
premises is about as helpful as to remark
that the summer frocks are suggestive of
feminine vanity. Of course there is selfish-
ness everywhere. The social conditions we
live among are based on selfishness. They
have their roots in a system which recog-
nised that the first man to grab the bone
was the one to gnaw the meat off. But in no
comparative sense is selfishness an Aus-
tralian characteristic. No people give more
generously. The jaundiced looker-on may
see indications of selfish characteristics in
the Conscription vote. But can any be seen
in the spirit which brought the Australian
army into being? Can any be seen in the
army itself? Nowhere in the world is there
finer' matesliip; and at rockbottom mate-
ship is unselfishness.
