The German’s Punch and the Allies’
Pantry.
Now which is more likely to be correct —a
statement made the other day by British
Ambassador Buchanan or one attributed to
the Russian Ambassador at Paris? Accord-
ing to Buchanan, Russia has finished as an
active power in this war, whereas Mackla-
koff asserts that “Germany is retaining the
greater part of her army on the Eastern
Front.” In spite of all the confused evidence
concerning Ivan, this paper is more in-
clined to back the Englishman’s yarn than the
other fellow’s. There seems no reason for
even hoping that Russia may again become
a serious factor in the disturbance, nor any
for imagining that the German view differs
from Buchanan’s. A section of the popula-
tion may fight, after a fashion, to defend the
Revolution. Also annexation may provoke bitter
resentment, though, as the territories which
Germany desires have been thoroughly Teu-
tonised, there is a good deal of doubt about
that. But Russia as a nation can hardly be
said to exist, and organisation for war now-
adays is something different from getting
ready for a football match. At any rate, the
story that the enemy is compelled to now
keep as many soldiers and guns in front of
the Russian as he had to when Brussiloff
persisted in making a real fight of it is
incredible. There are far bigger issues at
'stake on the Italian and French fronts and
scores of reasons why the German should
have as much of himself thereabouts as is
possible. That he will struggle to admini-
ter a heavy blow in both places at the earli-
est opportunity appears to be certain; and
while there is plenty of justification for ex-
pecting the Allies to resist defeat, there is
none for thinking that the job will be easy.
Meantime the figures relating to ships and
submarines and the details available about
the food supply in Britain are no more joy-
ful than before. An incidental detail is the
prevailing speculation as to whether J. Bull
will buy this season’s Australian wheat-cr p.
As John already owns many millions more
bushels of Australian wheat than he can
transport, and as he has just bought the
Argentine crop the prospects are not cheer-
ful. And for next season’s harvest they are
less cheerful still. Yes; the war is, after
all, quite a serious business. Australia may
even discover that it will stand some hard
thinking about.
The Vanishing Wheatfields.
A vanishing wlieatfield is one of the big-
gest problems that face Australian Govern-
ments. In N. S. Wales, for instance, after
the big spurt in 1915-16, the area shrank
some 25 per cent. This season it is esti-
mated officially that it has dropped another
15 per cent., but outside experts think the
reduction heavier. In Victoria the area this
year is 238,003 acres less. Hundreds more
cockies are now reckoning that if they cannot
get ss. a bushel guaranteed, they had
better throw the plough on the scrap-
heap ; and the general feeling is that the
man who nuts in a larger area than he can
handle without depending upon outside
labor is looking for trouble. Seemingly a
bait of some sort must be hung out to keep
the area from continued decrease. In sup-
port of a guarantee of ss. a bushel for the
next three years, the cockies point out that
in Maoriland the Government has guaranteed
its growers ss. lOd. a bushel; that U.S.A.
and Canada will give 2dol. 25 cents per
bushel, equal to over 9s. per bushel f.0.b.;
and that Britain has fixed a guarantee on a
sliding scale for the next six years, starting
this year with 725. per quarter (9s. a
bushel). But M.L. grows only about enough
for local requirements, the position in Great
Britain is obvious, while the U.S.A. has no
Avlmat to spare, and in any case it and
Canada are much closer to the big markets
than we are. Apart from the problem of
financing a future five-shilling guarantee,
what are we going to do with the wheat?
After this crop is taken off there will be
something like six million tons of grain piled
up in Australia, and most of it is likely to
stop here for years. The silos to be built
will hold only a handful of it. The wheat
cannot be held in stacks without deteriora-
tion. Is it a business proposition,_ then, to
guarantee sufficient to produce a big crop—
unless, of course, the British Government
wants us to grow and take a chance in
hoarding the wheat here, which means, of
course, that John Bull must find the
money. The horrible mistake we have made
is in devoting almost all our attention to
wood-and-water-joey jobs—including wool-
growing and wheat-growing—and letting
manufactures slide. To-day, because the
ocean is not open to us, we can’t get rid of
the wheat that we must sell 12,000 miles
away, and we can’t procure the manufac-
tures that we must mostly drag the same
distance. The foreign-traders and low-
tariffists have between them landed us in a
nice bog. Yet we still let them bluff us into
giving them the largest places of power and
authority. We deserve all the disaster that
comes to us.
Here is the sort of question that shouldn’t
need asking; and still The Bulletin can’t
answer it. The Chambers of Manufactures
ought to see to it promptly. The letter, by
the way, comes from Brisbane: —
In a month or two I intend setting up a dugout of
my own ; and as it is also my intention to put every-
thing in the place, as far as I am able, of Aus-
tralian manufacture (we are both Australians, and
yours truly has “done in” a leg over Australia
already, but doesn’t mind doing in a bit of cash
over her either), I am desirous of knowing if there
is any kind of book or pamphlet (Government or
otherwise) which will give a list of Australian firms
making any or every kind of article for domestic
use. In this town shop-people look blankly at you
if you ask for Australian goods. Hence my appeal
to you. If you can get the information for me I
don’t mind paying you for your trouble.
“ Don't mind paying yon for your trouble /”
This is from a man who has just cheerfully
lost a leg for Australia and now wants to
spend his money on Australian furnishings
for his Australian bride! And last week
there was a parson in Sydney raving about
the selfishness of Australians !
Why all this mystery about Hughes going
to London again? It has been known for
weeks past that representatives from over-
sea have been asked to attend another con-
ference with the Cold Country Government.
The Maoriland Government has made no
secret of it. Hughes is anxious to go and
will go—a circumstance which all along
made the fate of the pledge a certainty.
As things stand at the moment—they will
pretty certainly stand somehow quite dif-
ferently within a day or two —the Peake
Ministry in S. Aus. has to sit in judgment
on the land-deals of the Vaughan Ministry
which preceded it; and half of the Vaughan
co. forms half of the Peake coalition. Styles,
the ex-Minister chiefly blamed in the report
of the Commission, has resigned as a matter
of honor, though declaring fiercely and at
great length that the report is absurd and
ought to be reviewed by a real Judge, which
he does not allow Noel Webb (who was the
Commission) to be. The old original Labor
party sits back and asks why Blundell and
Jackson, the other two members of the pre-
sent Ministry who were also Vaughan’s
colleagues, do not resign as well. At the
very same unfortunate moment, Liberals
and “Nationals” are getting together to dis-
cuss some sort of coalition for the coming
elections, as well as for a mere Ministry. At
latest advices they were entirely unable to
agree, and will be probably running candi-
dates against each other, to the great joy
of Labor. An alternative scheme is to put
off the elections altogether for a time; but
that bit of treachery to the people would
take any quantity of engineering to put
through.
“Wart” : The B.M.A.’s boycott of the Tas-
manian hospitals is beginning to get in its
work. The Launceston Hospital, whose
usual staff consists of a surgeon-superin-
tendent, a house surgeon and an assistant,
with a few honoraries, is now down to sur-
geon-superintendent and one country doctor
who comes in for a couple of days a week.
In the first place the honoraries resigned,
and after them the house surgeons; then
the B.M.A. picketed the job, and there have
been no “loyalists” or “scabs” thus far, so
the institution has had to shut down
its out-patients’ department and restrict in-
patients to urgent cases. The chief cause
of the strike is that the hospital takes well-
to-do patients and charges them fees. But
why not? At the root of the matter is the
“charity” idea and the practice of accepting
the service of honorary doctors. If the
State paid every surgeon and physician for
their work, as it should, they would have. no
occasion to growl. A hospital is an institu-
tion organised for the treatment of disease ;
and to deny the moderately or even the im-
moderately well-to-do the right to the bene-
fits of that organisation is absurd.
The N. S. Wales Public Service Board,
put on to the job, finds that since the war
began £104,000 has been spent on monkey-
cages, etc., at Sydney’s new Zoo in defiance
of the law. Of course, nobody will be
punished. No politician will even lose his
job over it. Nothing will happen—except
that the same thing will happen again. The
misapplication of the money went on under
the nose of Parliament. But it was “part of
the game,” and no member took any notice.
The Bulletin called for an inquiry into
this disgraceful expenditure of money in
wartime nearly two years ago; and there
hasn't been one yet. For the Public Service
Board inquiry merely touches the surface of
the matter. The whole business stinks of
political bribery and corruption. The little
body which ran the old Zoo got hold of
Taronga Park and proceeded to make a new
Zoo in a modest, economical way. After it
had been at work for some months a number
of men were thrown out of their Govern-
ment jobs at the new abattoirs, etc. They
told the Holman Labor Government that it
would have to find work for them. They
were assured that there was work in the
country. “No good to us,” they said. “You’ll
have to find us work near town.” And the
Government, to retain the support of these
men, chased round to find something for
them to do. It jumped at the chance the
new Zoo offered—and that is, briefly, the
disgraceful story of how £104,000 (and well
on to another £lOO,OOO besides) came to be
spent at and in connection with the Zoo in
defiance of the law which says that works
estimated to cost over £20,000 must go to
the Public Works Committee for inquiry and
report.
“Croson”: While skirmishing round after
the odd ha’pennies that Peacock couldn’t
save, Premier Bowser was hit by the stag-
gering fact that Beecliworth Gaol, with a
governor, an assistant and five warders, was
being maintained for the —er —benefit of
one lonely prisoner. If Beecliworth doesn’t
misbehave more frequently he threatens to
close the institution and shift the staff —
and the prisoner—to Pentridge. But even
that would be only one small step. Private
Enterprise has never had a fair chance in
treating malefactors. I reckon that if I
had the services of a daily average of 300
prisoners in return for providing them
merely with plain food, coarse clothing, a
very simple bed and the guardianship of a
score or so of warders, and was allowed to
work them 12 hours a day or thereabouts, 1
would have to pay a heavy income tax. I
admit that before very long the supply of
“cheap labor” would probably fall to nearly
zero —but that is the main object of our
penal system, isn’t it?
Plaving received his instructions from the
Labor party, Lloyd George has cut out the
old “Never again” talk. The fact, of course,
is that the Allies are largely depending upon
something happening in Germany very like
Avhat has happened in Russia, and it would
be madness to expect a working-class cry for
peace in Germany if peace meant that the
Allies were going to starve Fritz. So, instead
of the old talk about “economic isolation,”
the new promise to the German working-man
is that there shall be, no economic war. Then
what about Australia? Obviously this makes
it more necessary than ever to get on at
once with tariff reform, if only to hold the
industries which the war has enabled us to
start. Hughes’s old talk about pulling up
upas trees and cutting out cancers goes for
nothing. “No economic war” —that means
“business as usual” ; and within a month of
peace it is just about as certain as anything
can be that Germany will be booking orders
for the old goods and offering them a bit
cheaper to get back its trade. Only one
thing will save our baby industries and
enable us to start new ones, and that is a
high tariff.
Sands’ Directory, the Sydney business-
man’s reliable old finger-post, makes its 48th
appearance with all the old features pre-
served when they haven’t been improved. It’s
a worrying time for Browne-Smythe, who
adheres to the principle that it is cheaper to
move than pay rent, when Sands’ Direc-
tory makes its appearance and tells his old
grocer and butcher where he is hiding.
From Gordon Gummow, gent, one: —
Re Metzler changing his name to Martin in accord-
ance with kingly precedent (B. 3/1/T8). It might
be inferred from the par. that he was convicted for
doing it. As a fact, he was discharged. It is a
weird tangle. Metzler’s father was born in what
was then French territory, and while he was doing
missionary work for Britain he begat at Jaffa a
son who was born to be the defendant. The son has
hit practically every country on earth, so much so
that a layman would shy at determining his nation-
ality. Two circumstances saved him the other day.
He was allowed to give evidence as to pedigree (one
of the few matters on which hearsay evidence is
admissible—usually without fear of contradiction)
and he proved he was a British subject. He had
been in England with his dad between the ages of
three and six, and his dad at some period or other
obtained a Certificate of Naturalisation in the U.K.
That was good enough for the Bench to decide that
he was a British subject, and therefore not guilty,
despite the fact that he had resided in Germany fox-
many years. But apart from that, the facts showed
that Metzler-Martin had been inventing all kinds
of horrible devices to defeat Germany, and placing
them before the Commonwealth Government.
The thin little excuse that was to give
N. S. Wales Attorney-General Hall a trip
to America melted through Canada decid-
ing for Conscription. It was just little
David’s luck to find fruit turned to ashes
that way. Something like this happened
last session. Jabez Wright, member for
Broken Hill, had struck an Abraham
Lincoln attitude and was talking at large.
“I consider,” says Jabez, “the Attorney-
General a necessity.” Here was a stun-
ning compliment from an unexpected quar-
ter. Then—“ Necessity knows no law,”
drawled Jabez.
The Earnest Believer is sometimes an
embarrassment to the leaders of a sect which
keeps one eye fixed on the next world and
one on this. The Sydney Methodies were
doing their bit on the Day of Prayer speci-
ally set apart for Winning the War. One
clerical gent, was speaking while two reli-
gious heavyweights, past presidents of the
Central Mission, beamed approvingly on the
platform; and a lady Methodist who doesn’t
approve of a profitable business arrange-
ment chose that moment of all others to
begin praying aloud for the fellow-religion-
ists who let the sainted Lyceum Hall, in
Pitt-street, to moving-picture people. The
speaker tried to go on ; but the lady had a
penetrating voice and refused to modulate
it. So a hymn was called for, and the
Voice of Conscience was drowned with
praises of the Lord.
The list of new names for places in S.
Aus. bearing German titles has been settled.
It differs widely from the suggestions made
by the committee of three (E. M. Smith,
Rodney Cockburn and Talbot) which was
instructed by the Vaughan Government to
substitute native names only. Peake has a
fancy for war names instead. The chief ex-
ception is Lobethal, known by its woollen
factory; this gets the sensible name of
Tweedvale. Petersburg becomes Peter-
borough, Grunthal is now Verdun, and Blum-
berg Birdwood. There are many other
changes among less well-known places; in
particular, the north and south Rhine be-
come the Somme and the Marne, ard the
way-back Kaiserstuhl is Mount Kitchener.
Further changes which will cause some re-
gret are those that wipe out the names of
good old State servants of Teutonic origin—
some of them still living. The “hundreds”
of Homburg, Schomburgk, Basedow, Krich-
auff and Von Doussa disappear in this way.
According to the latest communique from
the scene of action in Victoria, where the
lodge doctors are battling with the friendly
societies for a rise of a bob or two per
annum, seven applications for the job of per-
manent lodge doctor have been received by
those in charge of the societies’ arrangements
at Geelong, the first centre to make a start
with its own medical institute. The doctors
willing to embrace the friendly societies
without the blessing of holy 8.M.A.. are
located in five different States, only Tas-
mania being unrepresented in the list of
applicants. This looks like first blood to the
lodges—though, considering that it is the
wage-earning class which usually belongs to
such bodies, the latter are hardly to be con-
gratulated oh their success.
“S. Pallida”: Victoria has a Venereal
Diseases Act, but police magistrates are
powerless to apply its provisions. And,
apparently, nobody worries about such cases
as that which came before Notley Moore,
P.M., at Melbourne City Court, when a
waitress who arrived from Maoriland a
month ago was charged with vagrancy.
According to Policelady Connor, the
woman was venereally infected. She was
detained for medical treatment, of course?
Nothing of the sort —she was discharged,
and at this present is probably in a restau-
rant handing plates of variegated tucker to
unsuspecting customers ! “We cannot send
this woman to gaol,” said the beak. “There
is a Venereal Diseases Act, but no machin-
ery for carrying it out. We would like to
protect the public, but” — Here the police-
prosecutor chipped in with the remark that
persons suffering from defendant’s complaint
were, he understood, prohibited from work-
ing in places where food was sold. But the
beak explained that there were no means of
enforcing the prohibition. So the way to
Hell is still wide open and smoothly paved
with good intentions.
From the Gallery :
Of all the actors in the recent pantomime George
Wise, of Gippsland, remains most eminent in my
memory. Early in the proceedings he became
affected with an itch to worship W. M. Hughes, and
hastened to disclose his obsession in showers of
superlatives. In his view Hughes was a tempera-
mental haggis of Napoleon and Northcliffe, with a
dash of Martin Luther and Haroun al Raschid, and
in private or political quality combined the integrity
of a cash-register with the infallibility of the Most
High. He hurried about in the lobbies like a dis-
cordant blowfly, his small coat-tails flying and his
arms elaborating his stained-glass views of Mr.
Hughes. And the result of all his cheerful spruiking
was the honor of being sent for ! Yes, the Governor-
General, finding the Ship of State sinking and the
crew down with the mumps, plugged the leak with
one hand and frantically signalled for Pilot Wise
with the other. It was a great day for George—the
greatest day he will ever see. After a few minutes’
pow-wow with the G.-G., the former leader of a
party of one retired into his old niche. The G.-G.
had sent for him —but Hughes didn’t.
Handed a D.S.O. as a New Year's Gift.
Septimus Rowe, Major of Artillery and only
22 in years. He’s a son of the late Colonel
Rowe. A brother has been killed in action,
and another is giving a hand over there.

Palace in Bolls-Royce style last Saturday.

maker of the United States, is as trying as

** * *
The box-office at Sydney Majesty’s is feel-
ing like the best kid of the family. The
city has been stirred by the news that “The
Bing Boys Are Here,” and there are always
some people in the theatre who have paid to
stand. Nothing delights a showman more
than to see an honest armchair citizen try-
ing to do the flamingo trick; it is a sign
of good show weather when the human bird
stands on one leg for hours at a time.
** * *
The J.C.W. children’s panto., “The House
that Jack Built,” will come to an end at
the Royal (Sydney) on Saturday night, and
the cow with the crumpled horn will hang
herself up by the tail in the property-room
till next Christmas.
** * *
Peter Pan Macdonell will cease from
pirating on Friday at the Criterion (Syd-
ney), and on the following night the popu-
lar actress will produce “Outcast,” playing
the part of Miriam —one of her best.
Robinson Crusoe continues to do so with
much success at the Grand Opera House
(Sydney), and by all appearances Man
Friday has got a long job in front of him at
union wages.

The early Greeks and Romans made use
of beans in taking the votes of the people.
So does Bilanderson at Sydney Hippo-
drome, where he grows his own beanstalk
and has the assistance of Jack as returning-
officer. The questions put to the populace
were (1) Is this a bright and merry panto?
(2) Do you think the principal boy, Mabelle
Morgan, straighter than Billy Hughes? So
far, the Yessers are leading by 21 pecks one
pint.

Ada Reeve ceases fire at Sydney Tivoli
on Friday in “Winnie Brooke, Widow.” On
Saturday the bill will present a new mixed
front, with baritone Peter Dawson’s name
most prominent. Soprano Anette George
and the Creole Fashion Plate (a somewhat
tiresome mystery this) supply the other
turns. The rest of the evening will be
taken up by Bob Greig and Beatrice
Holloway’s acrobatic three-act “Baby Mine.”
Wallace Held and Eugene Pallette hold
the screen in “The World Apart” showing at
Hoyt’s Sydney house. It has a quick-action
plot, and includes all the thrills incident to
life in a “Western” mining town. At the same
address Pauline Frederick is putting fine
acting into “Double Crossed.” At the Cry-
stal Palace, that bit of quicksilver, Olive
Thomas, once more dances her way to
favor, making “Indiscreet Corinne” a
bewitching little vampire. The Lyceum fea-
ture, “The Woman Beneath,” is redeemed
only by Ethel Clayton’s brightness and
charming frocking; while across the Strand
sheet Balkan royalty plots and counter-
plots, and George Walsh scales the palace
walls and shows ’em “The Yankee Way.”

“The Story of the nosary” has brought
another week of war to Melbourne Prin-
cess's, but the venue has changed to Strel-
sau, where excited armies,‘about 15 strong,
strew the landscape with their rainbow
dead. Colonel Bob Inman heads Strelsau’s
rush on the enemy entanglements, anu am
voice of command assays a pound of molasses
to every word. Of the junior officers Cap-
tain Vivian Edwards keeps his nose nearest
to the barrage; he is then mislaid for about
two acts, while his sudden midnight bride con-
tributes a rainstorm to the general gloom.
Vivian’s wife this trip is Miss Marie Ney,
a tall and graceful novice who has
a sweet disposition, but needs to double her
output of soprano. The young lady is
hurried into matrimony by Arthur Styan,
who is fastened to the role of Father
Theodore and visibly frets at his moorings.
The same old Peterkin (Henri Dore) has
his former supporter (Elwyn Harvey) in a
frontal attack on the fortune of Nicodemus
Winkle, who is now a drivelling ancient
from comedian George Scully’s gallery. Allan
Wilkie again plays the reformed boozer, Karl
Larose, under the pseudonym of McHaggis,
so to speak, whilst the previous villain,
Walter Hunt, butts into the gamble for the
big feminine prize like one who holds a
royal flush. The show apparently finishes
to-morrow, and as the advt. is silent about
anything to follow, the presumption is that
the Princess’s will shut up for a while.
** * *
At Melbourne Majesty’s the career of
“Whittington” knows no checks, save those
for passing to and fro at squash-time. A
warm summer—the first the city has experi-
enced for several years—attempted to get
the local pantos, down, but they beat the
temperature. In fact, J.C.W.’s show im-
proves in the running. One doesn’t as a
rule think of the panto, stage as a promising
field for musical and histrionic culture; yet
there has been a considerable artistic
development in “Dick Whittington.” For
instance, the later scenes in Act 1. The
poor stuff that Bantock and Langford have
to talk, whilst stage-hands are busy behind
the front cloth, is treated as humorously as
could be; and the duet between Vera Pearce
and Pauline Bindley—“By and bye you will
miss me”—has been toned and softened.
Perhaps the heat and burden of a summer
panto, season is a healthful thing for the
art of the girl performers: they gain grace
as they lose flesh. Certainly it is when
Maggie Dickinson’s figure is slimmest that
her beautiful performance carries most
weight.

The tale of The Bird of Paradise con-
tinues to wag at Melbourne Royal, and Hula
Hula is still the cry. Every night, on the
right side of 11 p.m., the sacred mountain
gets its steam up and Muriel Starr is under-
stood to plunge into the crater. After which
a plump pagan (James Hughes) peers into
the scenic perturbation, like one who has
lost his hat. in order to persuade the audi-
ence that Muriel has made away with her-
self. The audience, being hardly convinced
on the. point, reckons that if Hughes really
wanted to win the war he would jump into
the noisy volcano himself. His conduct in
refraining from sacrificial suicide seems most
ungentlemanly urd" 1 ’ the circumstances.

** ♦ #
The leading lady at Hoyt's (Melbourne)
this week is beauteous Pauline Frederick,
whose speaking likeness dominates the
silent drama of “Double Crossed.” Pauline
on the film has the same fault as the ordi-
nary melodrama heroine on the stage. She
circumvents the villain in a terribly round-
about way, at awful risk to her sweet self,
whereas a few well-chosen remarks to an
influential capitalist would have saved her-
self and her husband all the anxiety. In
which case no drama to speak of would have
been available for the film. The picture-
story at Hoyt’s is full of interest, and
Pauline’s manner of disposing of the boss
grafter who means no good is a fine piece of
acting. The other principal picture shows
Wallace Reid in a pugilistic capacity. Reid
is an almost incredibly picturesque hero
when he is making love or proving his
innocence, and when he puts up his hands
to fight he is first-cousin to the god of stousli.
sj: * ❖ *
Dick Rosson, a new movie celebrity, is
finishing a busy week at Melbourne Majestic.
He is the hero the camera loves to linger
upon in the brisk Triangle story of Cassidy.
Cassidy is the “super” in the Order of
Supercrooks, but in gratitude one evening
he allows himself to be kind to a very nice
girl. The intelligent film-fancier can guess
the rest—can visualise a high-colored
Cassidy playing corner-man in the Band of
Hope. The features of Frederick Ward are
lit up in the second Majestic feature, “The
Heart of Ezra Greer.” Frederick appears
as Ezra, a trembling patriarch who uses up
a mile of film in a hunt for a vanished
daughter.

“sob sis.er,” and Fitzherbert (of course) as
the irresistible seductor.
The Byng adventure that premature halle-
lujahs were sung about must have been
pretty disastrous. Less than three months
ago it was claimed for the British armies in
France that they had lost only a few
thousand prisoners and no guns during the
year. Now it is announced that the British
losses on the Western Front were 27,000
prisoners and 166 guns for 1917. The
previous fine average of losses versus
gains must have been simply knocked
to pieces in the early-winter battle.
Byng! Ominous name. It was an Admiral
Byng who was court-martialled and shot in
1757 for having failed to attempt the recap-
ture of Minorca from the French. The
French cynics of the time said that the Eng-
lish shot Byng pour encourager les autres.
“K.”: Charles Gregory Wade having been
knighted, it is now time to tell the story of
how he tried to get away with the stained and
battered straw hat which I had christened in
self-defence “Old Sydney.” The then leader
of N.S.W. Opposition absent-mindedly took
the pathetic ruin from the rack of a
crowded restaurant and strolled towards the
pay-desk. Some of my friends raised the
alarm, and I rose and went after the pirate.
He returned it with a graceful apology and
came back with me to get his own lid—
which was just one shade worse than mine.
“I didn’t think the Liberal party had come
down to hat-stealing, Mr. Wade,” said I.
“Well.” laughed Gregory, “ collectively it
may, but individually it hasn’t.”
Old parson Jefferis, just dead in Adelaide,
used to say that he owed his degree of
LL.D. to some Sydney boys who stole his
apples. He chased the marauders and fell
and broke his leg; and when he found him-
self booked for some weeks on his back he
made up his mind that the time should not
be wasted, and started to read for the degree.
“Tea is 6s a 1b.,” says an Australian in
London, “and very scarce at that. The
oueue at one big tea place was fortified by a
few A.I.F. soldiers. When they gradually
edged in turn to the door of the promised
land, the man inside looked at them. “ You
don’t want tea, surely?” said he. “Good
Lord, no! We thought it was a picture-
show !” laughed Billjim.
When the Outburst happened in Europe
Esk Sandford, youngest son of thp ex-iron-
master, went off in khaki to Rabaul, and was
afterwards invalided and discharged. Inac-
tion didn’t suit the youngster, so he made off
to Blighty, and in no time was piloting a
’plane in the Naval Air Service. Next, he
was given the ticklish job of tester, and
finally was transferred to the Royal Flying
Corps, where he soon become a flight-com-
mander. Last mail announced that the Syd-
ney boy had achieved the rank of squadron-
commander, which leaves him little further
to go. .
Father Clune, a bearded stalwart who is
two years the right side of 50, has just won
the M.C. in recognition of his having
marched in six giants of the Prussian Guard
amid a Billjim cheer that split the sky.
He is with a Cornstalk battalion, and his
habit is to haunt the front trenches under
pretence of carrying important letters. It
was on one of these excursions that he col-
lected the Fritzes. He’s from County Clare,
and as Passionist missionary is known in
every State, for he has been 25 years in
Australia. He was the first R.C. padre to
enter Liverpool camp, and the last to leave
Gallipoli; he has been wounded and men-
tioned in despatches; and his brother is
Archbishop of Perth.

“Peter’’: Those good friends, Billjim and
Sandy, were at an estaminet chaffing one
another as usual. Sandy belonged to a
famous Scottish regiment that has the proud
record of ntrver having lost a trench to the
Huns. “I hear the Fritzes got into your
trench last night, Sandy,” said Bill. “Ye’re
a liar.” said Sandy. “Yes, they got in,” con-
tinued the Australian, “an’ you never saw
’em cornin’.” Anticipating an offensive he
moved out of range and then chuckled:
“They jus’ walked in disguised as a joke!”
* * * *
“Karee” : The decoction which the highly-
imaginative Gippo calls whisky was abso-
lutely taboo within the sacred precincts of
No. 1 A.G.H. Every trick was tried, but
defeated, after a more or less prolonged run.
Things were looking serious for the great
Australian thirst, when Billjim developed a
sudden fondness for the zepp-shaped yellow
melon, yclept bassoos, which showed inebri-
ating qualities hitherto unsuspected. A
rougly-handled drunk dropped his melon one
night, whereupon the sagacious vegetable
gave birth to a full-grown bottle of the best
procurable. Melons being rigidly censored
henceforth, Billjim suddenly waxed senti-
narental, and for a while successfully passed
the guard with a bouquet intended for the
ward sister, but chastely sheltering a hair of
the perennial dog. I can’t say what took
the place of that poetic dodge, as I resumed
chasing Jacko after its ignominious exposure.
Chaplain Stacey Waddy, once headmaster
of King’s School, Parramatta, is having his
grins, too. One night the battalion he was
with was returning from the trenches and
had to pass through a sea of -mud. Shells
were falling all around, so the chaps were
being pushed on. Stumbling along one
unlucky beggar got bogged up to the waist
and could not be got out. Finally his mates
told him they could do nothing more—he
would have to stay. The padre was
near, and the poor beggar in his agony at
the thought of being blown to bits or
drowned in the mud, burst out: “Damn it
all, Padre, you might try a prayer;
that’s what you’re here for!” (P.S. —They
came back after dark and extracted him with
a mule.)

Mostly concerning revolutions:—
There are many things that the community would
like to know about the present whirly condition of
Russia. To begin with, it wants to know the correct
spelling and pronunciation of “Bolshevik,” and the
reason why a Bolshevik is one. Also, who and when
and where was the first Bolshevik, and are there
honorary and stipendiary Bolsheviks, and appren-
tice Bolsheviks who study the business at a techni-
cal college? Does the whole affair consist of a few
leaders Who get very little chance to lead and a vast
indefinite mass of followers who include everybody
that wears a wild expression in the eye and untidy
hair? It is commonly understood that a man may
be a Bolshevik to-day and anything else to-morrow
or yesterday, and, to put it shortly, that anybody
is or isn’t a Bolshevik who looks like one or
otherwise.
The learned and dreamy leaders of the movement
saw that things were wrong, and yearned to put them
right. The extreme and ill-regulated tail which had
broken out of gaol before the completion of its sen-
tence saw that things were wrong and hoped to make
them worse by profane language and burglary. This
happens in all revolutions. The great essential is
that the head of the movement may be strong enough
to hang or guillotine the tail in the interests of
AN EFFORT OF DISREMEMBERING.
THE "BOY”: “You know, when I get into these clothes it’s hard for me to remember that
I’m twenty-two.”
THE OTHER: “Yes, it must be hard to forget the other ten.”
order. A revolution wants order even more urgently
than a despotism.
The old rulers of Russia were a hard lot, a bad lot
and a not. very capable lot. Yet in their time the
country held together in a sort of way, and people
walked on the right side of the pavement where
there was one, and three square meals’ a day were
not uncommon. Now there is famine with the best
intentions, and people walk on any side of the pave-
ment.
Revolution is a fine art when applied to an
ignorant folk who have had little chance to learn
self-government. The men who upset the Bourbons
in Prance were artists after a fashion. They beheaded
all the petty tyrants who didn’t get away in time
and confiscated their ill-gotten wealth. At the same
time they made the revolution keep step. They said
that one revolution at a time was enough. Every-
body had to revolushe at fixed hours.
An old, mouldy, brutish autocracy is an incurable
animal, and requires to be utterly wiped out as soon
as the right men are found for the job. On the
other hand a young revolution is apt to be frisky
and fll-regulated. It requires careful steering.
Many a promising revolution has broken a heap of
valuable property, and finally its own neck, because
the honest but uncertificated mariners in charge
didn’t know ar.y better. Then it was “buried with
the burial of an ass,” like Jehoiakim, King of Judah.

“Petifi”: I met the Sentimental Bloke’s
cousin in a little N. S. Wales mining town-
ship lately. He was clobbered obviously in
his Sunday tailorings, his hard-bitten face
shone with soap, and when he entered
the bar he tenderly placed a small
blue coffin he had been carrying under
his arm on the end of the counter and
softly ordered a beer. The other
gentlemen present watched him imbibe
in respectful silence. Then the bar-
lady voiced the genera l sympathy.
“So, Geoi’ge, you have lost your little
one.’’ “Yes, missus,” replied George,
dejectedly, “the poor little ’s snuffed
out!”

Lancashire a medical man was summoned
for assault. A 10-year-old boy was taking

rented the paddock, carried him into the
house to give him a hiding for his trespasses.
The boy screamed for mercy and attracted

gave him a bit of the whip, too. And
what happened to this landholder in good
old Tory England? We know what usually
doesn’t happen to a medical man who com-
mits a breach of the law in democratic
Australia. In fact, the wind of retribution
is tempered to any professional man of
means. But in Newest England! Well,
the doctor was sent to gaol for six weeks,
with hard labor. One of the “push” might
knock you down and jump on you in Aus-
tralia for less than that,

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]

The Great One sat in his easy chair, with languor in
every limb,
And raised his glass with a lordly air to sip at its
beaded brim ;
Placidly prim, in a splendid calm he wielded his
palm-leaf fan,
The while, on a number of pleasing plans, his
thoughts serenely ran ;
And all the time, in a careless way that really was
rather rum,
He rested his boots on the furniture and waited for,
what should come.
And, as he nibbled a biscuit there, his sizzly sips
between,
A menial entered and tiptoed up with a carefully
gentle mien,
Bearing upon his salver bright a fearfhlly fateful
sheet,
The which he gave to his Ex’lency and vanished on
fairy feet ;
And his Ex’lencj’ put down his fan and summoned his
wandering wits
To read the terrible words that soon should shatter
his calm to bits.
One glance he gave at the dreadful lines, then
bellowed, “Oh, gracious me t”
And hastily rose to scatter the crumbs that chatted
his noble knee ;
He pulled his beautiful waistcoat down and fingered
his chic cravat,
And, carefully smoothing his parted hair, he reached
for his outsize hat
And galloped out of that restful place, in the manner
of one who must
Be up and doing to Save the Throne from Crumbling
into Dust.
DEEDS OF VALOR.—NO. 1: CUTTING
THE LINES OF COMMUNICATION.
“What did the Colonel say when you asked
for Mabel's hand?”
“Swore dreadfully at me, and threatened to
horsewhip me.”
“Good gracious! And what did you do?”
“By Gad, y’know, that's more than a man
can stand, so I — er—rang off in his
bally ear.”
And then a clatter and hum arose in the halls of the
Truly Great,
And the Wheels of Fate went whirling round at a
simply amazing rate,
With the patter of nervous feet that rang down
corridors cool and grey,
As jabbering citizens came and went in quite an
excited way,
With the root-toot-toot of a swarm of cars that
bustled from here to yon,
While high, pink foreheads unused to stress with
perspiration shone.
Both far and near strange noises woke —the drum-
ming of angry drones,
The cackle of heated speech and “Blazes!” muttered
in undertones ;
The solemn cackle of earnest folk that waited around
in knots;
The distant snarls of the Tiger, vexed with his own
confounded spots ;
The painful snorting of messengers, arriving with
bags of mail,
And the sudden yelping of some stray Rover, cursed
with a tardy tail.
The creaking of crammed wastebaskets, filled with
the wreckage of busted plans;
The jangle of frantic ’phones, the rumbling roar of
electric-fans ;
The hurried swing of his Ex.’s door and a grumble
of “Well, what now?”
As the press poked in, persistently, with a hand to
its baking brow.
Grizzle and yammer and peck and push, squabble
and whine and biff—
The sort of racket one might expect at the birth of
a hippogriff!
Placidly prim, the Great One sat once more in his
easy-chair,
Doing his dutiful best with the terrible burden he
had to bear;
And, all the time, as he sucked a straw, with a
drink on the end of it,
From beautiful plot to fragrant plan he let his
fancies flit;
And all the time, as thus he helped the Empire to
endure,
He rested both his poor eld feet on the office
furniture.

Things have become rather strenuous for
those volunteer helpers who are trying to
keep Bilijim’s unfortunate or incapable wife
fed, clothed, sheltered, amused and out of
debt during hubby’s absence. Owing to the
extravagant conduct, here and there, of Bili-
jim’s grass widow, it is now a solemn fact
that a decent, way-paying soldier’s-wife often
finds that she is given cold pig when she
goes house-hunting, or gets the glassy eye
when she desires the convenience of a fort-
nightly account with the butcher. Bangs,
J.P., of Prahran (Vic.), in giving a 30-days
ejectment-order (no rent was asked for)
against a woman who had allegedly let her
rent run unpaid since last August, "While
drawing her allowance without intermission,
put the case plainly enough :
If soldiers’ wives refuse to pay rent it will be
hard on those soldiers’ wives who desire to pay their
way, as agents will refuse to let them houses.
It will be hard —and it is ; and the agents
will—and they do. Australia's soldier-wife
wants putting on a strict business basis for
the good of all parties ; and a step in that
direction would be the raising of the 44d.
children’s allowance to something adequate,
so as to remove the last reasonable excuse of
the slie-loafer.
“F.N.T.”: Premier Bowser is having his
duty pointed out to him by the Moving
Finger, which has written “D” opposite
rather more than the usual number of in-
fants’ names this summer in Melbourne. A
recent discussion scared up some facts about
the city’s chief milk-charity, the Talbot In-
stitute. It appears that (1) Talbot milk
co,sts lOd. a quart to customers; (2) it is
only supplied free, in
approved cases, during
the summer; (3) it
isn’t delivered free,
even in the most
necessitous cases, un-
less the Government is
in a kindly mood and
provides enough cash
to pay for a free sup-
ply; (4) the Govern-
ment isn’t always in a
kindly mood, and
hasn’t been so gener-
ous this summer as ic
should have been.
None of these facts,
however, are to the
discredit of the Talbot
concern. When things
are good the Institute
gives frep milk and
lends ice-chests to poor mothers,
and shows them how to manage
the supply properly ; but as it has
only a limited income, its posi-
tion is generally painful. Bowser
ought to do two things: (1)
Spring after the frequent milk-
man who sells old, rotten or
skimmed milk ; (2) see that Little
Jim gets at least the bare neces-
sities of life, whether mummer
can afford to pay for them at
lOd. a quart or not. Incidentally,
it is time the Commonwealth re-
considered the £5 baby bonus.
Inquiry in a hundred directions
shows that it rarely does what it
was supposed to do. It was to
be a “maternity allowance” —
something to make matters more
comfortable for mother and safer
for little Jim. It really is a
baby bonus, mostly. The money
could be better spent in baby’s
interest in a dozen ways—the maintenance
of a pure-milk supply throughout summer is
one of them. And baby’s interest comes
first.

From the local society gossip :
A unique wedding took place at North Heeiriskirk,
in this tangled wilderness of West Tasmania, last
week, when our best pick-and-shovel woman, who
is also a tin-miner, was married to a member of
the same profession. The parson had to take a
day’s journey to reach his job, which was performed
in a disused hut. In lieu of a white satin cushion
the bride knelt on an overcoat, and all the scratchers,
fcssickers and tin miners for miles around stood by
in their shirt sleeves encouraging the pair.
Well, good-luck. Bill!

The case of the damsel with a shocking
story which collapses under cross-examina-
tion has taken quite a new form in Adelaide.
She was 70, and the accused was 24! Two
charges were dismissed or withdrawn, on
account of the time lost—over two months—
in bringing them. There remained a similar
charge only 10 days old; but in this case
the old lady’s memory and self-possession
went all to pieces in the witness-box, and so
did the charge.
“Midget”: I’ve a wholesome dread of Ger-
man espionage. The —th Battalion, which
had been in France only a few hours, got
its first taste of trench life by relieving some
Maorilanders. A dark night was selected
for the change, and the handing-over took
place about 11 o’clock, the greatest secrecy
being observed. Next morning the new Bill-
jims were astounded at their welcome—a
board was reared above Fritz’s trench, show-
ing the colors of the new battalion!

The city sniggers at Wirewhiskers when
he comes down to the Show, with or without
the missus and the girls, and gets a lot of
fun out of their weird millinery; and it
never thinks that the Wirewhiskers family is
forming its own opinions, too. Wherefore
what “Bullant” Mclntyre, of the Southern
Cross (W.A.) Times, had to say when he
got back home from Melbourne is calculated
to do as good :
The “Block” is the strutting-ground on which may
be seen the arrogance of wealth, the ostentation of
brail.less females, and the price of unsexed females
who are as strange to virtue as they are to the touch
of an infant. Upon their bosoms may be seen pearls,
but never babies; in their hearts may be seen
vanity, but never love of motherhood that makes a
nation great.
I never walked the beastly “Block” without pray-
ing to Gcd to spare me the humiliation of meeting
“rnv ain” folk there, or those whom I was acquainted
with ; for that is the detestable abode of vanity, vice
and villainy.
What a contrast to the wholly-abominable “Block”
is to be seen in the Botanical Gardens. Here the
true mothers of a nation are to be seen ; not rigged
fore ar.d aft with birds’ tails, or other tails that
spell the price of the wearer, but robed like natural
mothers or maidenly lovers.
In the Gardens man has given his labor and artists
have given their art, in order that Nature, already
beautiful, may be so embellished that young humanity
may be nursed into manhood and womanhood there,
away from and untainted by greed or vanity, away
from the shameless female pariahs who strut and
pose on the “Block,” in robes that are as out-
rageous as they are indecent.
The Botanical Gardens are unspeakably glorious,
and when the mothers who know how to dress add
the beauty of their presence and the splendor of
their homely attires to scenes already made noble
bv the Creator and the combined brains and hands
of his fellows, there is hope for our race and a
future for our great Commonwealth.
“Boyd Cable”: I could close my eyes and
fancy myself back in Australia —if it weren’t
for the cold everywhere except where my
shins toast at the big fat stove. I’m sitting
in the mess of an Australian Flying Squad-
ron, and I hear snatches of Australian slaug
and a dropping fire of references to Condobo-
lin and ’Coota and Iviama and Coogee. The
papers in sight are the old Bully, the Mail,
Australasian, Herald and Sunday Sun.
The news is mighty stale because, although
this is November, the papers are only dated
xYugust (a mail has just arrived). These
papers are full of the Sydney strike, and the
mess is very full of curses. This afternoon
the squadron played a football match (Aus-
tralian game)with an A.I.F. battalion. Some
of them were in to tea, and again the talk
was boiling. I wish some of the strikers
could hear a few, even an expurgated few, of
.£he remarks of their scrapping men about
strikers and strikes at a time like
this. The iniquity of holding up ship-
ping while people in the Old Country
were going mighty short on food and
Australia is bulging with stores of grain
hung up for want of transport brought blis-
tering remarks. But what rankled most of
all was the claim of an occasional strike boss
that they were “protecting the rights” of the
men here! Over that magnificent bit of
hypocrisy the Australian adjective was
nearly worn out in indignant expletives.

From an Australian in Chelsea: —
George Reid is never seen in London now, and
rarely heard of even by Australians. The fact is he
has gone out of fashidii. This is not because of any
psychological discrepancy ; it is due purely to physi-
cal phenomena. You out there can hardly conceive
the antipathy against fat men that has taken hold of
us. We do not give loud expression to our hatred
of men of conspicuous girth, but the feeling is in-
stinctive, silent and morose, and however unjust is
based upon an almost universal emotion. Big eaters
are held despicable, and the crowd does not stop to
consider that the eater who is big is not necessarily
a big eater. Stomachs have “gone out” in London,
and for that reason public men are doing their
utmost to draw them in. Every day some personage
makes joyful announcement through the papers of
his loss of a further five pounds, or five ounces, as the
case may be. George Reid at large is a spectacle to
provoke anguish and bitterness in people with small
stomachs and little in them. So, in the interests of
public order, he remains perdu, possibly in compli-
ance with a polite request from the Lord High
Comptroller of Grub.
It comes in all sorts of ways. When
Henry Allen was climbing into a lorry at
Home Rule (N.S.W.) the other day, he
noticed that the family cat was having a
doze between the horses’ legs. Allen threw
a handful of sand at the cat to scare it out
of danger. Puss was scared all right, and
fled to safety; but the horses took fright
also, and in the ensuing bolt Allen was flung
from the lorry and killed.

6.30.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The hilltop street lamp, suddenly in jest
Flicks out the pale flame of the climbing moon,
And Twilight, dropping from a cloud balloon,
Sets off as postman with a bag of rest.
And I'm on the ferry where neighborly fun
Is sign that the day’s drab flurry is done.
The hill-dames round the harbor sit and smile,
Green gfannyhoods with earth-glee quivering;
They gossip slyly on the one droll thing—
That men must leave their ledgers for a while.
And both ferry funnels leave fat plumes of smoke,
To show they have fathomed the hills’ fine joke.
The fond bay stretches out his warm brown arms
And from them slips that ready flirt, the pier.
Coquettishly, as we draw slowly near,
She stirs to make parade of all-her charms.
And home thoughts like stars dance in everyone’s face
As steamer and pier hug in shameless embrace.
Soon all the hill's awink with window eyes,
Warm eyes that watch beside the waiting doors,
That hide joy-brimmed, ecstatic pinafores.
And so I’m singing, swinging up the rise 1
I’ll soon have one knuckle delightfully in
The tiny cleft curve of my baby’s chin.
MAORIIAND.
O. N. GILLESPIE.

Dear Bulletin,— l noticed a presenta-
tion of my classic lineaments in a recent
number. But you’ve manufactured a genea-
logy for me. The Portuguese are an excel-
lent _ people in many ways, and I have
received much kindness from them in
several countries; but I am not anxious to
trace my parentage to their race. My
father—not my stepfather—was a Chinese.
Hence my name. My mother is an Irish-
woman. All of which is good enough for
me. As for the rest, “I am my own ances-
tor.” —Yours, Thomas J. Iv. Bakhap.
There are still several persons living who
came to S. A us. with Gov. Hindmarsh in
his ship Buffalo in 1836, and one of them,
Mrs. McMann, actually attended at Glenelg
on “the twenty-eighth” and signed the year’s
roll. As for “pioneers”—any born or arriv-
ing not later than 1840 get a special wel-
come at the annual demonstration —nearly
a hundred are still active enough to attend.
The first Melbourne jeweller to make
a public confession that his girl is a great
help to him in handling his gauds is George
Palfrey, of Little Collins-street, the firm-
name having become Palfrey and Daughter.
There is more than one Melbourne butcher
whose female relatives do most of the chop-
ping-up and serving-out, and The Bulletin
still bones to see “Smith and Mother” on
the billheads.

Major H. W. 11. Seager, just returned
aft r service in Eg.vnt. Gallipoli, France and
Flanders, br’ngs a Military Cross with him.
He enlisted with his two brothers in 1914,
and won his decoration foi the dash with
which he led a rescue company against the
Hun. His gran’clad, Lieut.-General Seager,
C. 8.. took Tvnri as a lieutenant in the charge

The tragedy of war came 12,000 miles
closer the other day when Brisbane took off
its hat to the passing of Major W. O.
Hodgkin son. A son of the explorer and poli-
tician after whom a river and a goldfield
were named, he carried three stars on his
shoulder-strap in S’Africa. He was in camp
a month after the Kaiser set the world on
fire, and left as second in command of the
11th L.II. For his work in Egypt he re-
ceived special mention. Afterwards he put
in over 12 months’ strenuous service in
France and gained his majority. Illness
invalided him to England, and in August
last he was brought home to the four little
Australians who had lost their mother while
their father was in the firing-line. But he
never recovered sufficiently to leave Kangaroo
Point Military Hospital.
Signaller Tom Skeyhill, blinded at Gal-
lipoli, is off to America on a lecturing tour.
Not only have his wanderings in Austral’a
brought him more than a decent living, but
he has raised over £15,000 for patriotic
funds—a remarkable achievement for a man
who, when he was led off the boat on his
return to Australia, had only one grief, that
he would be a burden to his friends. His
father, J. P. Skeyhill, acts as Signaller Tom’s
manager nowadays, and will go with him on
his big tour.
Hughes never touches an omelet these
days.
The Hun robbed Australia of a promising
musician when he closed the score of Private
George Hurst last month. Born 26 years
ago, Hurst had already made good among
Sydney and Melbourne musical folk before
he went to Germany to study in 1912. Just
before he sailed one of his compositions—an
orchestral intermezzo—was produced at a
Marshall Hall concert. At its conclusion
vociferous calls for the composer failed to
produce young Hurst (as a matter of fact,
he had fled) ; whereupon Marshall Hall
called: “We can’t find the composer, but we
will repeat his composition.” Which, com-
ing from Hall, was no small compliment.
Hurst escaped from the Hun when the storm
broke, reached England and enlisted with
the Royal Fusiliers.
Dead at 65, after a very short illness,
E. W. Van Senden. He was born of a
Dutch family in England, and made wool
the business of his life. From 1881 he was
a S. Australian, but soon became known
almost as well in Maoriland and anywhere
else where there was wool to be bought or
sold. At the end of the century Dalgety’s
took over his business, and he became their
Adelaide manager. Public life did not
attract him, but he did good work for the
School of Mines and Chamber of Commerce.
Personally, he was the most genial and
kindly of men. Both of his grown-up sons
are in France.
The Australian record for number of
times enlisted probably belongs to James
Marquis Cairns, 25, who first joined the
colors on August 24, 1914, and was with
S. Australia’s famous Tenth on the Penin-
sula. Shot through chest and arm, he came
home, recovered, and joined the 50th, the
Tenth’s younger brother, resuming business
Egypt on March 21, ’l6. Wounded at
Fleurbaix, France, he was discharged in
Australia 10 months later. Just- a rear
after that (9/1/T.B) he was sworn in at
Melbourne for his third round. In peace-
time he is one of the messengers at Parlia-
ment House.
Concerning two inventors: —
Two useful Australians are Lieut. W. 11. Geake
and Alfred Salenger, who lately got the Order of
the British Empire “for courage and self-sacrifice in
volunteering to carry out dangerous experiments in
making munitions.” Geake (37) formerly hung out
his consulting-engineer’s shingle in Sydney, and
Salenger (36) is a partner in a Sydney firm of whole-
sale jewellers. Early in his career as a Billjint
Geake struck an idea for an improved bomb, and
Salenger helped him to perfect it. About a year
ago the Defence Department got an unusual hustle
on, and sent the twq inventors to England to submit
their ironmongery to the War Office. Soon after
their arrival a batch of the new missies were sent to
the trenches and proved themselves first-chop. It
was while making further experiments for the War
Office that the cobbers earned the 0.8. E. During
an explosion Geake rushed into a room, rescued one
of his men and dashed back again, thinking there
was another man there. Seemingly there wasn’t,
and the mistake cost Geake a broken leg, a right
hand, and divers other things. Details of Salenger’s
trouble are not yet available, but his little personal
lot of damages includes a fingerless left hand and a
badly-damaged left arm. Both men are now out of
hospital.
Bananaland lost one of its oldest inhabi-
tants when John Warren White passed out
at the age of 91. He came from County
Clare, Ireland, and had withstood 60 years
of the Northern climate. Major-General
C. B. White (trebly decorated) and Colonel
D. P. White are sons and are still on the Big
Job. His only three grandsons are at the
same address.
With a John L. Sullivan jaw and a voice
like the bull of Bashan’s, the Rev. Herbert
E Hobdaj- has been making more stir in
the Speck than the referendum and apple-
crop troubles combined. He is going to
reform everything from drunkards down to
politicians—from pubs to Parliament. Until
he “does his block” with enthusiasm he is a
finished elocutionist; then he roars and per-
forms acrobatic
feats that any
movie - producer
would pay him big
money for. His
only debauchery is
bowls.

Adelaide had word at Christmas of little
li. - i. 'lariton, who. after quitting tne Church
half a century ago, became a man of note in
S. Australia’s trade and politics. Nearly
smothered under a bank smash, - he went to
S’Africa, and, though he will be 90 in April,

young cedar, fresh and green.”
Arthur H. Moseley, one of the latest
batch of D.S.O.’s, is an old boy of North
Sydney Grammar, and took his medical
degree at Sydney Uni. in 1909. He served
with the Australian A.M.C. in Gallipoli and
Egypt, and since June, 1916, has been lieut.-
colonel in charge of a field-ambulance in
France. Father is W. 11. Moseley, who for
many years pursued costs in partnership
with W. P. Manning in Sydney, arid before
that was a captain in the British Army.

Now in Sydney, Ernest Darling, “The
Nature Man” of Tahiti, wearing a little
more than when Jack London introduced
him in “The Cruise of the Snark.” Sydney
in summer should be a good place to preach
the joy of throwing off clothes and madden-
ing the tailors ; but the nuts and cocoanut-
milk idea won’t catch on. Darling is a fine
figure of a man in an abbreviated khaki
suit, a fishing-net shirt, and no socks or hat.
He’s Amurrikan, of course.
“J.F.11.” looks backward through the fog:
Thirty-two years ago a quartette of Socialists,
John Burns, H. M. Hyndman, H. H. Champion and
John Williams, stood in the dock of the Old Bailey
charged with having seditiously raised a riot in
Trafalgar Square and the West End. They were
acquitted, but a few years later John Burns got
three months for creating a disturbance in the same
square. It proved a good advertisement. He
became an M.P. and Minister of the Crown with
£5OOO a year, which he threw up at the outset of
this war. Hyndman is still a Socialist leader, but
has repeatedly failed to get into Parliament. Cham-
pion has lived in Melbourne for many years and
treads other paths. Williams, the fourth of the
batch, has just died in London. He was one of the
founders of the Social Democratic Federation and
was sentenced to a month’s imprisonment in 1884 for
some illegal remarks at a meeting. At a more recent
date he got into trouble for making what the Lord
Chief Justice called “disgraceful suggestions against
a friendly Sovereign and relation of our own
beloved King.” That “friendly sovereign and rela-
tion of our own beloved King” was—Kaiser Bill.
Major Charlie Cunningham, who nas
been made secretary of the Vic. State \\'ar
Council, is a brother of Argus editor
Cunningham. As official shorthand-writer
at the Law Courts, he was frequently a tar-
get for amiable eulogy from C.-J. Madden
and other dignitaries. He enlisted in the
early days of 4*he war, got a commission and
finally was made a major and placed in
charge of the Australian military post office
in London.
By way of consoling old Frank Madden
for his kick-out from Parliament, the Vic.
Executive Council has appointed him a
trustee of the Public Library and National
Gallery, in place of Edward Carlile, de-
ceased. Frank Madden’s claim to anv sort
of connection with literature and art
amounts to nil. But his many displays of
arrogance and stupidity in public capacities
marked him as the sort of man whom
Executive Councils delight to honor in
ways like this. He was in the swim—
swimming in Fat, so to speak.
“HELLO, SOLDIER!”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Back once more on Moley’s corner, loafin’ like a
(look ;
Back on Bourlce, me livin’ image, not a slinkin’
spook ;
Solid ez the clay I started, medals on me chest,
Switchin’ with me pert malacca, swankin’ with the
best
Where the little wimmen’s flowin’,
With their veils ’n’ ribbons blowin’—
See their eyes of bloo ’n’ brown
Eutterflyin’ ’bout the town !
Back at ’orae —oh, ’struth, it’s good !
Long, cold lagers from the wood,
Ev’ry cobber jumpin’ at you,
Strangers duckin’ in to bat you—-
“Good ole Jumbo, liow’re you?”
“’Elio, soldier, howja do?”
Back at Grillo’s where the nigger googs his whiter
eyes,
Plucks his black ole greasy banjo while the cod-
steak fries ;
Fish ’n’ chips, a pint iv local, and the tidy girl
Dancin’ glad attendance on veh ’zif yeh was an earl;
Trailin’ round the blazin’ city,
Feelin’ all content ’n’ pretty,
Where the smart procession goes,
Prinked ’n’ polished to the shows,
One among the happy drive—
’S worth the world to be alive!
Dames ez smilin’ ez a mother,
Ev’ry man yer fav’rit brother:
“ ’Elio, Jumbo, how is it?”
“Arr, there, soldier ! Good ’n’ fit ?”
Talcin’ hozone at St. Kilder’s good enough for me,
Seein’ Summer and the star-blink simmer in the sea;
Cantin’ rip me bloomin’ cady, toyin’ with a cig.,
Blowin’ out me pout a little, chattin’ wide ’n’ big
When there’s skirt around to slcite to —’
Say, ’oo‘has a better right to?
Done me bit ’n’ done it well,
Got the tag iv plate to tell;
Square Gallipoli surviver,
With a touch iv Colonel’s guyver—
“Sargin’ Jumbo, good ole son!”
“Soldier, soldier, you’re the one!”
Back agin, a wounded hero, moochin’ up ’n’ down,
Feelin’ ’sthough I’d got a fond arf-Nelson on the
town;
Never was so gay, so ’elp me, never felt so kind ;
Fresh from ’ell a paradise ain’t very ’ard to find.
After filth, ’n’ flies, ’n’ slaughter
Fat brown babies in the water,
Singin’ people on the sand
Makes a boshter Happy Land !
War what toughened bone ’n’ hide
Turned a feller soft inside !
Great it is, the ’earty. greetin’s,
Friendly digs, ’n’ cheerful meetin’s:
“’Elio, Jumbo, howia do?”
“Soldier, soldier, liow’re you?”
VICTORIA,
EDWARD DYSON.

Actually £25,000 was reckoned by the mana-
ger to be required to meet depreciation in
the last year; but towards this there was
£3BOO standing unappropriated in the De-
preciation Fund. The total amount pro-
vided for depreciation since the mine started
is £155,954. This has been actual money
received on the sale of coal and most of
it. can be traced: —Works, etc. (presumably
replacements), £57.559; cash, £40,989;
Government stock, £34,061. The sink-
ing fund contributions as shown above
total £83,485, which is accounted for
this way : Repaid to State, £24,101; Govern-
ment stock, £52,274; in the business, £7050.
So we get this aggregate result. In seven
years the mine has repaid the State £24,161
of the £266,509 of capital put into it; it has
kept up the payment of interest on the loan
liability; and it has accumulated in cash and
Government securities £133,924 towards the
replacement of machinery and the return ot
capital. In addition it has provided out of
revenue over £50,000 worth of plant, etc.;
but probably all or most of this was to re-
place worn-out or discarded machinery.
As the depreciation and sinking funds have
accumulated, interest has of course assisted
the earnings. Of last year’s gross profit
£3184 came from the mine; interest revenue
was £2971. This only makes the slump in
the gross profit more serious. So far as the
past year is concerned the slump made it
impossible to put anything into the
sinking fund. However, the contribu-
tions thus far have been very liberal.
The manager, after careful boring, puts
the reserves at 17.000,000 tons, after
eliminating the areas containing thin
and banded coal. Last year’s output was
372.007 tons; in the previous 12 months
452.798 tons ; the year before that, 567,056
tons. At 500,000 tons a year the reserves
would last 34 years. As a matter of fact,
the present sinking fund, improved at 4
p.c., and without any further contributions
of principal, would amount to £225,000 in
34 years, and the debt now owing is only
£17,000 more than that. This paper isn’t,
however, suggesting that sinking fund con-
tributions should be knocked off. It will
not call for much of an effort to wipe the
debt out in 17 years. A contribution of
£5750 a year will do it.
If the Wontliaggi coal-miners had been
political agents in the employ of the Em-
ployers’ Federation or some other violently
anti-Labor body, they couldn’t have given
it better service. Their life, especially dur-
ing the past three or four years, has been
spent in throwing scorn on the Labor plat-
form and mocking at Labor aspirations.
Instead of demonstrating that industrial rest
could be secured by exchanging public for
private enterprise, these men proceeded to
show that State employment gives the pub-
lic ho greater security than private employ-
ment. On the question of earnings the
manager says:—
Net average earnings ot contract miners, alter
deducting cost of explosives and lights: —
1913- . . 13s. 11.49 d. per shift.
1914- .. 13s. 11.64 d.
1915- . . 14s. 6.16 d.
1916- . . 16s. 2.10 d.
There was a strike in November. 1910. in
sympathy with one in N. 8. Wales. The
War Precautions Coal Board heavily bribed
the men to go back and give their promise to
stay in for three years. That opened their
eyes. Having tasted the fruits of blackmail,
and found out how easy it was, they put the
screw on again, and went out once more in
August and September last year, without,
as they confessed, having any local griev-
ance.
One of the grievances of those who have
wanted to see public enterprise given a
chance was that the Leg. Council hamstrung
this undertaking by refusing to it the right to
sell coal to the public. All the screened coal
went to the railways and other public depart-
ments; only the slack could be offered to the
people, the owners of the mine. However —
The Government recently provided for the sale to
the public of the coal obtained from the thin seams
which could not be economically worked at the price
obtained from the Kailway Department for screened-
coal, the alternative being the abandonment of these
seams. Shortly after this decision to sell coal from
these seams to the public was arrived at, the War
Precautions Coal Board gave an unexpected addi-
tional payment to the miners working these low-
banded seams, although the men were previously
making good wages ; and as this decision will increase
costs, there is little possibility of making this coal
pay.
Most convenient thing, the War Precautions
Act!

Freight troubles are the chief worry of this
Melbourne co.. which carves forests in
Maoriland and. more recently. Westrnlia.
But scarcity of shipping has so curtailed
importation that the foreign stick is almost
unobtainable; wherefore, despite the hole
that lias been knocked in the building trade,
the co. shows no war-scars worth mention-
ing. Of course, like every other trader who
is opening liis mouth very wide, it talks
about the inconveniences it has to put up
with and the business it has to miss; but
when you remember that the bottom was
due to fall out of the borrowing and con-
struction boom in any case, there isn't any
need to condole with the co. Gross profits
at £92,425 for last year are only £l2OO
below those of live years ago, when the
boomsters were feeling very fit. Expenses,
certainly, have soared: management exes,
and taxes now take a cut of £43,130 (in-
cluding £4286 which the co. is allowing its
employees on active service) instead of
£24,906 in 1912.
The kauri forests of M.L. are gradually
disappearing, so in 1914 the co. began mill-
ing on a property it had acquired in West-
ralia. Here are the consequent balance-
sheet movements: —
1911. 1914. 1916. 1917.

Precisely what is the value of its properties
the co. does not know, as both freeholds and
leaseholds include “standing timber there-
on,” an asset which varies according to
the state of the market. It is almost certain,
though, that the scarce kauri will get dearer
and dearer, and at the same time
Australian woods will come more into
favor; so this eo.’s forests should
depreciate but slowly. At one time
the co. reckoned its kauri areas were depre-
ciating pretty rapidly, and wrote down their
book value year by year as the getters put
the axe in. But a revaluation disclosed the
fact that its remaining forest giants were
enhanced beyond the value of the original
properties. Going back to the balance-sheet
figures, it will be seen that the co. has re-
duced its liabilities by £101.707 in three
years; meantime, however, the co.’s holding
of assets other than forest properties lias
decreased by £105,350. On the other hand,
apparently about £57,700 has been put into
plant and machinery. The formidable-look-
ing reserves of £187.512 are made up in
this way: Fire insurance fund. £50.000;
marine insurance. £18.562; dividend equali-
sation reserve, £34,993: log reserve. £9912 ;
and contingency fund, £74,045.

The £5703 includes £697 interest earned by

the business; so the net profit from trading

Allen Taylor’s have given special attention
to big sticks and heavy construction
material: and with the collapse of the
States’ borrowing boom one of the principal
sources of profit in that trade has run
pretty dry. Further, there is no shipping
available for the export trade in hardwoods,
in which there has also been good money in
normal times. However, the directors are
no doubt reckoning upon a brisk demand for
hardwood timber when Europe starts to
repair itself, and that and the steadily im-
proving local demand for liardwood to re-
place soft stuff in building has set the co.
to work increasing its equipment on the

this table the co. has £29,379 of fixed de-
posits, War Loan and other reserve fund
investments; so its financial position is still
easy, notwithstanding the outlay on country
mills and the increase of hardwood stocks.
It is quite likely that the immediate future
won’t be much better than the immediate
past; but further ahead there will probably
be good profits — assuming, of course, that
the co. continues to command a manage-
ment that knows its way about.

This co., which lias swept the remnants of
half a dozen other building societies into
its basket, has been going slow during the
past two or three years, not knowing what
is ahead. Therefore if the profits are not
what they were shareholders can get this
consolation; (1) there is still a wide
margin of profit heyond the 8 p.c.
dividend, and (2) the risks have been
reduced —on paper, at any rate. The fol-
lowing table sets out the principal balance-
sheet figures as at various dates, going back
to 1900, when the co. had just purchased
the bones of the Planet Society:—
1900. 1915. 1916. 1917.
Permanent capital £72,741 £115,046 £115,046 £115,046
Investing capital. . 28,003 1,989 1,548 1,274
Deposits, etc. . . 74,334 380,545 346,207 331,644
Advances, etc. . . 89,345 493,450 457,782 440,137
Foreclosed pro-
perties.. .. 49,935 1,584 863 15,426
Other freeholds.. 44,478 96,859 94,955 87,370
Cash, etc. . . 2,479 465 . 9,713 12,367
The second line among the advances sug-
gests the definite turn of the tide: after a
gradual reduction in the book value of the
foreclosed properties year after year, there
is a sudden increase to £15,426. Still,
unless something catastrophic happens, there
shouldn’t be very large losses. In spite of
the moratorium —a measure for which there
was very little justification, and which
ought to be overhauled at once —the mort-
gagee, and especially the mortgagee who has
made advances on building-society terms,
has suffered and is likely to suffer less from
the war than most people would have
believed possible. Most great wars have
been accompanied at home by horrible dis-
tress among the wage-earners, and especially
among the families of the fighting men. This
time credit has been so largely employed
that the wage-earner in Britain and Aus-
tralia has, on the whole, had more money
in his pocket than ever he had before ; and
if the building society hasn’t taken advan-
tage of that to get itself snug, then it
doesn’t know enough to take an umbrella
ivhen it looks like rain. How this society
will get on even with the umbrella nobody
knows, because nobody can be sure what the
storm will be like. If the markets for our
staple products fall to pieces, we are going
to suffer from a depression that will make
most of us take up a few holes in our belts.
And even if the markets keep pretty good
there is going to be such a bill to pay that
we shall feel very sick;, and the concern
with property to sell will have to wait for
a buyer. This co. has, however, so sub-
stantial a reserve, and its management exes,
are so very moderate, that it has a good
chance of maintaining a decent dividend.

This, is one of the unpretentious little up-
country mills whose object is to help the
farmer save a bit more from the middleman.
There’s not much in it, though, as that
bumpy profits column shows. In 10 years,
after paying a dividend averaging 5 p.c. p.a.
and maintaining its plant, etc., the co. has
actually reduced its net liquid and semi-
liquid assets from £BOOl to £OOl5. Never-
theless, it would be doing serviceable work
even if it only helped to keep the “offal” in
the country, where it is most wanted.
Capital is £11,389; liabilities, £OBl5. On
the other side: property and plant, £10,006;
stock, £5790; book debts, £5936; and sun-
dries, £1159.

The Bulletin will not swear that all the
£134,071 was made in the past year. As to
£50,000 the directors explain that “during the
year certain lands which have been in the
possession of the co. for many years and
gradually improved, till they were suitable
for closer settlement have been sold for that
purpose, and the capital surplus realised
enabled the directors to restore £50,000 to the
General Reserve.” This is an interesting
comment on the fears which once filled the
co. that the Labor party’s Federal land tax
would mean blue ruin. So terrified were the
directors that they launched out in S.
America, to find, of course, that the distant
field isn’t so green as it looked. As a matter
of fact, the Australian tax hasn’t visibly
reduced the value of land in small farms, for
the simple reason that those farms are
not taxable. Hence the fine profits which
sales have shown, as reflected in the first
column in the table. This co. has, indeed,
benefited in two ways. While big prices
have been got for land suitable (or alleged to
be suitable) for close settlement, the vendors
have pushed further out, and this and the
high prices of stock and wool have put up
the values of grazing country in the dry
areas in which also this co. was very largely
interested. There isn’t a doubt that in addi-
tion to the profits disclosed the co. has
thereby made a good deal of money which has
been applied to internal reserves—-probably
to write down advances and properties still
held. Had the directors been told when they
were shivering about the probable effect of
the Australian land tax that their Australian
properties were a better security than their
gilt-edged British investments, they would
certainly have suspected that a lunatic was
talking to them. Yet in the past three years
they have found it necessary to provide over
£150,000 as a reserve on account of invest-
ments and for contingencies.
The present co. (though it has made a
small change in its name, substituting “Mer-
cantile” for “Mortgage”) is 53 years old,
and, so far as this paper can trace it, in
only two years have shareholders not got a
dividend. For a great part of the time they
had 20 p.c. Early in the present century the
co. had a poor time; but its finances are on a
good basis, and so it was able to wait for the
inevitable recovery. Practically all of its
borrowed capital has been raised by the issue
of debenture stock, a little at 3 p.c., the bulk
(£1.900,000) at 4 p.c. It is therefore able
to nurse its lame ducks instead of throwing
them on the market; and the capacity of
Australian pastoral ducks for getting over their
lameness, if given time, is simply astonishing.
Of course it is possible to lose money even
then on this business: all depends upon
when a co. makes the advance —-whether at
the top of one of the recurring pastoral booms
or otherwise. The co. ought to be pretty
right now: for, though prices of wool and
stock would in ordinary circumstances have
produced a boom in station properties, there
have been steadying influences at work. Ad-
vances. generally, have been reduced ; and so
far as this co. is concerned one result is that
