HEAD OFFlCE—Equitable Building, Collins Street, Melbourne.
on June 30 last, while it owed £470,395 on
aL accounts other than debentures, it had no
less than £1,000,429 in cash, short loans and
gilt-edged investments written down below
market quotatioils. Other assets included
£2,107,278 of pastoral advances and proper-
ties, £203,035 in advances on wool and sun-
dries, and £185,245 in store and office
premises. Like other English cos., it is find-
ing that John Bull isn’t leaving all his war
b.'l for the future. In 1915-16 it paid
£46,183; last year £32.425. In 1910 it
groaned a bit at having to find £7534.
Instead of imposing a wartime-profits tax
which nobody understands and which would
have the effect of heavily penalising those
who show some enterprise in supplying the
country with the things it can’t now import,
while allowing those who do nothing at all
to escape, the Maoriland Government has
substantially increased the land and income
taxes. Under the new scale cos. and indi-
viduals with incomes of £6400 and upwards
will pay 7s. 6d. in the £, with a refund to
shareholders whose income from all sources
does not exceed £4OO per annum. Referring
to the new scale the chairman of the Bank
of New Zealand lately told shareholders: —
To give you an idea of what the increase in
income-tax means to us, I may mention that, on its
assessed income of last year, this bank will contri-
bute about £2X6,500, or an advance of £121,830 on
the amount we |>ajd last year. The former sum is
equivalent to 12.37 per cent, of the total paid-up
capital; in other words, if the present rate of taxa-
tion were to be continued, an amount equal to the
whole of the capital would, in eight years time, be paid
to the Government in the shape of income-tax. Then,
in addition, our land-tax will amount to about
£17,000, and pur contribution to the revenue in the
shape of note-tax will probably be considerably in
excess of £75,000 for the year.
But even so the chairman applauds the
prompt repeal of the excess profits tax after
12 months’ experience had proved it inequit-
able.
.T. E. Donoghue, g.m. of the Electric Light
and Power Supply Corp.—the growing con-
cern which was born in Balmain —is back
from U.S.A., where he went inquiring into
all things that might be useful to his co. One
of the matters lie probed was the practice
with regard to allowances for depreciation.
To this paper it has seemed that the co.
wasn’t writing off enough to cover wear and
tear. In view of the fact (which this paper
admits to be important) that, in addition.
the co. is paying for all repairs and renewals,
the g.m. concludes that the co. is doing more
than American authority declares necessary.
On that point, by the way, there was a
pretty obvious error in B. 10/1/’IS. The
depreciation mentioned there (£5100) was
for a half-year, not for a year.
While it is thinking about reaching out
across the water to Walsh Island and build-
ing real ships there, the Broken Hill Prop,
is pushing on with a little new industry on
its own side of the water. So far it has
bought from outside Australia the ferro-
manganese compound used in turning pig-
iron into steel, but now it is putting in a
plant to make its own. As there’s a good
deal of manganese in Australia there’s no
reason why it shouldn’t.
Mount Booralong, Armidale (N.S.W.), is
opening up its deposit of molybdenite ore by
sinking a shaft 50ft. below the bottom of
No. 1 open cut. The manager reports that
the bottom of the shaft is showing “good
payable ore,” and mentions that a porphyry
formation 200 ft. wide has been proved to
contain molybdenite. There is evidently a
large deposit; but as usual the mineral is
disseminated through the rock (in this ease
porphyry), and it remains for the minei-al
separation process to determine values. It
is all very well to report that the formation
carries 2-J p.c. metal. Just as wolfram con-
centrates are valued on the basis of tungstic
acid, so molybdic acid is the basis for valu-
ing molybdenite concenti’ates.
The N. S, Wales gold-miner is finding
that it isn’t always an advantage to handle
the only stuff that lias a fixed value. The
Mint gives the same for gold no matter what
happens; but the sovereign won’t buy wnat
it used to, in either labor or stores. And
as the margin of profit in N. S. Wales gold-
mining has for many years been very small,
there was a slump last year in the produc-
tion to 85.9540 z. crude, equal to 82,1710 z.
fine, valued at £349,038. The previous year
the yield was worth £459,370. There has
been a steady fall ever since 1905, when
£1,165,013 was reached. With a single ex-
ception (1888) the past year’s figures are
the poorest since the goldfields were dis-
covered in 1850.
In urging shareholders to vote Yes at the
Boulder Perseverance meeting, chairman
Bright, who submitted the liquidation resolu-
tion, said that although the war had greatly
hastened the end he doubted if the outlook
in their case would be muqh improved if
hostilities ended to-morrow. Instructions
had been given to take out all the rich ore
that was left, and when that was done the
plant would be dismantled and put up for
sale. There was still a lot of gold in the
mine, but, like the hundreds and thousands
of tons in the sea, it could not be extracted
without loss. The winding-up motion was
agreed to unanimously.
Kalgoorlie is depressed over the Perse-
verance liquidation, and the local paper dole-
fully declares that the co. ought to have had
more consideration for the town and kept
going on a reduced scale until the war ended.
However, the Board considered that the cost
of looking after the property and plant,
together with a little rooting here and there,
would be too much, and decided to give up
right away. When work became unprofit-
able, orders were given to break out all the
rich ore before closing down. But as the
shrinkage-stope system made it impossible at
times to separate the good from the poor ore,
the co. was unable to do this, either. At
the beginning of 1917 cash, bullion and
investments totalled £26,767 and liabilities
£8155; but losses incurred since then must
have considerably weakened the co.’s financial
position.
Several English shareholders in Oroya
Links (W.A.) are questioning the advi-
sability of the Board’s policy of letting only
the poorer parts of the mine on tribute, and
advocate handing over the entire show to
tributors. Failing that, they suggest early
liquidation, thus assuring a return of about
2s. per share which the co. holds in more or
less liquid assets: The co.’s ss. shares are
worth only a few pence in the market, and
no div. lias been paid since December, 1934,
when there was a threepenny.
Westralia's gold yield for 1917 was
88,000 oz. less in quantity and roughly
£370,000 less in value than for 1916, and little
more than one-third of what it was in the
early ’nineties. Several mines, including two
or three bis shows, closed down during the
year, and as others are likely to follow suit
the prospect of arresting the decline during
the ensuing 12 months is not hopeful.
Some time ago, when interested people
were booming Black Range Pinnacles
(W.A.), there was said to be heaps of gold
to be taken out, and at anything under £2
(they were then about 175.) the shares were
declared to be dirt cheap. Now operations
have ceased, and shareholders have been in-
formed that “in view of the abnormal in-
crease in the cost of mining supplies and
lack of efficient labor it would be suicidal
to carry on any longer.” Not a word about
the refractory and impoverished condition of
the ore at depth, which had more to do with
the co. giving up than anything else; and
shareholders ought to have been tola this
officially when the workings got below water
level.
Tn some notes accompanying the latest
annual report. J. A. Agnew, formerly man-
ager for 8., M. & Co. in Westralia and now
a director of the Lake View and Oroya, has
a word or two to say about what is left of
the co.’s interests in that State. It has, he
declares, been impossible to continue work-
ing without loss at the Lake View and Star,
and in order to keep going the Board
recently decided to restrict milling opera-
tions to a limited tonnage from the higher
grade reserves. This, of course, involves an
increased working cost, which Agnew
reckons is more than counterbalanced by in-
creased recovery, and he expects small profits
to be earned for some months to come. What
effect this cream-skimming will have on the
mine in the long run is not stated, but the
hope is expressed that it will “not seriously
damage the co.’s interests.” Yuanmi, the
only other live holding which the co.
now has in the West, is said to have shown
a gain in ore reserves during the past year,
but like all other shows it has suffered
severely from labor troubles. Yuanmi is one
of the shows 11. C. Hoover used to have an
exalted opinion about. He spent his spare
time calculating what shareholders would get
in divs. if the shoot, which was such and
such a length and such and such a value at
the No. 1 level, lived down a few hundred
feet. It paid 17 £ p.c. while he was doing
this, but nothing since.
Edna May Junction (W.A.). having
acquired a block on Consolidated’s eastern
boundary, has decided to bury the past and
order a new door-plate. In future the co.
will be called the Edna May Consolidated
Extended. Manager W. 11. Collins is put-
ting down a mainshaft on the new property,
and hopes soon to find out the strength of
the show.

If Stuckey’s boring programme at Edna
Deeps (W.A.) is carried out with his usual
luck, he will again put a bloom on the
Westonia rye. Duff has long hankered to
go after the low-grade formation to the west
of the shaft, where he hammered out a
living till the market was readv to swallow
the claim per Alec Peacock’s office. So if
the drill hits a decent prospect out that
way nothing will hold the Melbourne bull
crowd. One of the other bores contem-
plated will be passed down from the bottom
level to where it should catch the point of
the rich shoot on its pitch to the nortn.
This will be a critical drill. The third will
help to define the northern limits of the gold
in the whole shoot. The market approves of
the scheme, because it wants to know what
is underfoot at the Deeps and because it
sighs for a new stimulant to set' the merry
punter jigging once again.
Commodore, Meekatharra (W.A.), called
up all its cash to give Kearn’s neai-oy
Macquarrie lease a go. Assays never
materialised in the battery and the vats, so
it looks as if the eo. got on to another
stringer on that deplorable field. As good
stone has been got close to the boundary in
the lease to the south of the old mine, the
co. will go back there, provided shareholders
agree to a reconstruction to provide the
funds. The job won’t be costly, and it is
not a wise thing in these days to livhtly wind
up a co.
Mount Elliott (Q.) is installing a new
converter to handle matte from Selwyn.
Every preparation has been made lor another
campaign at these works. As a result of
an overhaul of five properties, probable re-
serves have been increased to 040,000 tons
—Mt. Oxide, 250.000; Consols, 400,000;
Great Australia. 180.000; Dobbin, 80,000;
Lady, 30,000. This large tonnage is, how-
ever, calculated on a low basis of value.
For instance, the 180,000 in the Great Aus-
tralia is mostly calcite flux worth from 1 p.c.
to 2 pc. copper. The Dobbin and Lady are
averaged at 5 p.c., and Mt. Oxide gets larger
reserves by the simple process of calculating
tonnage on a 10 p.c. basis.
“J.ILK.”: There is an atmosphere of
stinking fish about “Platman’s” yarn (B.
27/12/T7 ) re the Chillagoe field having-
been proved a blank. There are other dis-
tricts that were “proved” the same way.
For instance, llerberton, one time a tin
centre, now, as a result of systematic pros-
pecting, a copper producer. With few ex-
ceptions nothing was found here at the grass
roots; but sinking on a soft gossany forma-
tion generally unearthed something pay-
able. The Empress, the star show of the field,
has given a net profit of £OO,OOO, and has
a like amount in sight. Nothing was found
in this show above 80ft. My personal ex-
perience is that some of the finest copper
experts are the finest fools. Pick, Shovel
and Co. are an old-established firm and take
a lot of wiping out.
* * * *
Mt. Bisehoff (Tas.) has declared a two-
and-sixpenny, payable this month, The
new 20 head of heavy stamps are now at
work, and a return of 40 to 50 tons of con-
centrates per month is expected. The last
disclosed figures showed that the cost of pro-
ducing metallic tin was up to £l7O per tun.
So recently as the later half of 1010 Bisehoff
averaged only £175 os. 7d. for its metal; but
there is now a margin of over £lOO. As
there are only 12,0U0 shares, dividends
should be frequent while the metal market
holds up ; but on the foregoing figures it is
absurd to take much notice of present profits
in calculating the value of the mine,
* * * *
North Mount Farrell (Tas,) closed its
half-year with a credit balance of £4301. It
sells its picked crudes and concentrates to
the Sulphide Corporation, but the difficulty
of shipping bullion has caused a restricted
demand for ore and concentrates. It was
only by careful management and a curtail-
ment of development that working costs
were made to average £1 11s. lOd. per ton of
ore compared with £1 Ss. fid. in the previous
term. The North Farrell has been a good
little mine: it has produced 55.741 tons ore
containing 22,401 tons lead and 2,221,5100 z.
silver. Costs certainly are high, but with
silver and had at present prices it can do
all right.
John Brandon. Dorry Doolette and their
friends have gor firm hold of the Ivingsgate
molybdenite mine: they have collared the
whole issue, not letting the gentle public
have a look-in. The claim was worked in
pre-war days for bismuth, but since molyb-
denite has been so in demand the owner has
had two plants at work to save that mineral.
Brandon's option was at over £20,000 cash,
and as that is provided, war conditions
haven’t emptied the pockets of all the Queen-
street gang. What switched Brandon on to
molybdenite was the success of Alec
Campbell’s King Island scheelite mine.
With that co. making profits Campbell looks
well in the present float, and of course will
be put on to the Board. What hasn’t ap-
peared yet is what water the promoter
squeezes into the float.
* !» *
Asain Kumbang, the new tin-dredging co.
in the Freeman-Austral Malay stable, has
apparently given up hope of buying any
Malayan lame duck’s dredge ; but “after very
great delay and much difficult negotiating” it
has arranged to get all the materials for a
dredge, and expects that the machine will be
shipped from Australia by August. But
production won’t start till close-up six
months later.
♦ * * *
Deloraine is the only gold mine ’n 8. Aus.
on the dividend list, The fourth, of Is. per
share, was paid Lu December, and a clean-up
in that month, worth £lOO9 from 300 tons,
was close un to the average. The directors
appear to depend upon lateral development
lor a future supply of crushing stuff,
although the deepest level is only 293 ft.
down.

_ Although Westport (M.L.) sells over
300,000 tons of coal a year, it is a tight
squeeze these times to hand out the usual
10 pc. (£45,000).
The Broken Hill Prop. Co. had an instance
of the prophet’s honor in his own country
when there was talk of making munitions in
Australia. It set in to turn out steel, but
the- local inspectors condemned the stuff
because of alleged defects in the shape of
ghost-lines. Then the co. shipped the stuff,
defects and all, to British munition-plants
and they simply ate it up and asked for
more. A lot has been shipped and there is;
more to follow.

HOW TO MAKE YOUR OWN HAIR
TONIC.
A REMARKABLE RECIPE.
Every trace of scurf and dandruff was
banished —hair that had long been dull and
thin became thick, bright and beautiful,
and, more remarkable still, new hair, plainly
visible to the naked eye, actually grew upon
the patches that had been bald for years.
These are the results a reader is said to
have obtained by simply brushing the hair
gently and regularly, and applving to the
scalp a stimulating tonic composed of 3oz.
Bay Rum, loz. Lavona de Composee, and
i drachm of Menthol Crystals to which was
added a little perfume. Most high-class
chemists can supply these ingredients at
small cost, and the tonic can be quite easily
made up at home. It should be well rubbed
into the scalp night and morning, and the
hair should then be gently brushed for a few
moments. Results are quickly noticeable.
Burning, itching scalp is soothed; hair stops
falling out. New hair begins to grow, and
it becomes bright, soft and luxurious. Such
a tonic, so easily prepared, is certainly
worthy of a trial by every reader who is
suffering from hair or scalp trouble, or
whose hair is turning prematurely grey.—
Advt.

A WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, January 14, 191 S.
My Dear Akim, —
You can find these Australians anywhere.
The other day The Bulletin discovered one
of them, a Sydney medical student named
Keith Barry, in Baluchistan, helping to
guard the wild nor’-west frontier of India.
He hadn’t heard of another Australian there-
about. But there is one. Dr. Chisholm
Boss, of Sydney, has three sons doing more
than a bit. One of them was in Assam, tea-
planting, when the Big Trouble started. He
I stands behind a gun in Baluchistan now.
Gifford Macarthur King, Lieut.-Colonel,
is among the recent D.S.O.’s. He left here
at the end of 1914 as a major in the Field
Artillery, went through Gallipoli and got
a C.M.G. and a mention in despatches, then
lon to France. He is one of the Macarthur
Onslow clan, and the only son of his mother.
She is Mrs. Agnes King, of Woollalira,
widow of the late Gifford King.
The late John Hurley’s son Lionel (father
used to be member for Hartley), formerly
tin the N.S.W. Agent-General’s office in Lon-
don, caught a small C.M.G. in the New Year
shower. Hurley, now a Lieut.-Colonel, went
to France with the first ambulances given by
this Government. But mostly his job has
been in England, where lie is a deputy-
assistant director of something or other at
Tidworth.
News comes of the solid usefulness of
several Australians now serving their coun-
try in ‘'occupied” territory in the Pacific.
Captain Leach, formerly a Brisbane jour-
nalist, is district-officer in the southern por-
tion of New Ireland, where his tactful man-
agement of Fritz and Fuzzywuz is ironing
out awkward creases. New Ireland is the
place where the ill-fated Marquis du Ray
expedition hoped to found a lesser paradise
on earth.
And then there’s Captain Hunter, district-
officer in charge of the German Solomons.
This Adelaide man is just now on furlough
in his native State after over 12 months’
graft in the sun-kissed isles. Kieta, his
headquarters, is one of the beauty spots of
the Pacific. A lean, wiry Australian, Hunter
goes anywhere and everywhere armed with
a walking-stick. “Terrific heat seems to
glance off his lithe, greyhound frame,” says a
recently-returned Australian, “while his
method of handling both black and white
fills one with admiration. Before the war he
was an accountant in Adelaide. Opportunity
came and found the man. There isn’t a
more respected official m a difficult position
in any enemy colony now occupied by Aus-
tralians. He upholds the white man’s high-
est tradition that his word is his bond.”
Mrs. Martin Egan, who used to write as
Elinor Franklin, is filling her fountain-pen.
! She is on a globe-trotting mission for U.S.A.
papers, and comes this way from Manila.
! Possibly she hasn't seen Martin for five
minutes in the last 12 months. He used to
scour the Seven Seas for copy for Sammy’s
Associated Press. That was in the days of
the Russo-Jap war, when he put in some
time at Tokio and left the impression that
he could squeeze information out of a dead
mandarin. After much running up and
down the earth he has been of late reposing
on velvet as publicity-agent for the million-
aire firm of J. P. Morgan and Co.
The old village of Smithfield, under the
shadow of Prospect Hill, stirred in its sleep
the other day when the local coach unloaded
a cargo of Sydney visitors to wish many
happy returns of their golden wedding to
William Shelley and his wife. Eldest
daughter Amy Croft, whose husband super-
intends the mails for this State, poured out
the tea. The Shelleys are the oldest inhabi-
tants of Tumut, where the original Shelley
pitched his humpy on the best grazing land
in the district, at Tumut Plains, when last
century was still in pinafores. Later on, his
! descendants discovered that the land would
grow wheat also. His wife —Sara Martyr
until 50 years ago—was born on old Rich-
lands station, out behind Taralga, in the
Abercrombie ranges, where her father man-
aged in the long ago for William Macarthur,
the wool-growing knight who owned Camden
Park and other desirable allotments. Her
mother was a Pentland, one of an Irish
family ; another member of it, John Pentland
Mahaffy, is provost of Trinity, Dublin, and
a member of the Irish Convention now try-
ing to soothe the sorrows of the disthressful
counthry. His son, Arthur Mahaffy, who
married a Melbourne girl, Enid Boyd, was
chief sec. in Fiji and deputy-commissioner
for the W. Pacific a few years ago.
The Women’s Hospital (Crown-street) is
to get the collection from a matinee de luxe
on January 29. Hon. organisers Nellie
Leach and E. Card Trouton have stirred our
most exclusive suburbs to unload. 10s. and a
guinea a seat. In the fashion parade the
visions will be worth it. The Rajah’s lady,
Mrs. John Osborne, the Gordon girls and
Barbara Bowker are to flit across the stage
in the latest finery; Olive King has con-
sented to appear in pyjamas; and there’ll be
an Oriental spasm from “The Persian Gar-
den” by Nellie Leach and Goossens. All at
the Tivoli.
A son of the late Scotchbyterian parson
Dill Macky was wed in dad’s old kirk the
other evening. Dick Dill Macky chose a
Strathfield meenister’s lass, Olwen Moi’gan,
for bis bride. The Rev. Tom Tait, for-
merly of Melbourne, tied the knot, and the
wedding party left the ancient Scots’ Church
to step next door and crumble cake at the
Wentworth.
“Do you think we
could sit back?” asks
Mrs. Hugh Ward.
“The America n
woman wasted no
time in organising
her share in civili-
sation’s task. Al-
ready every little
hamlet has turned
its women’s club
into a Red Cross and
war-fund centre.
We’re keen on
organisation at any
time. When the war
came the material
was there—it had
only to be mobilised.
Before the Conscrip-
tion Bill passed, the
pacifists and all the mixed following behind
them promised us a revolution. I was in a
New York shop the day before the first draft
was to be called up, and was seriously
warned to keep indoors next day. ‘The
streets of New York will run red; the city
will be a shambles; thousands of dead and
dying will shed their blood on the pavements
in the fight against Conscription,’ said the
panic-mongers. Everywhere one heard the
same story—the biggest civil war in history
would destroy President Wilson’s attempts
to raise an army. Next day came. Many
women kept indoors, waiting breathlessly for
the sound of guns. In the afternoon I went
out. New York was just as usual—except
for the long queue of eligibles waiting to
register for service. Before the hill was
through there was a rush of volunteers, and
their names are enrolled in the public build-
ing of their town or village as the first
Americans to offer in the cause of the world’s
liberty.
“How does the American woman feel
about it all? Thirty thousand women
marched through N. York while I was there,
carrying banners expressing their joy when
President Wilson declared war on Germany.
The tears will come later, whdn loved ones
fall on foreign soil. But their womenkind
will not murmur. My sister’s son in Pitts-
burg was in the first rush of volunteers, and
gave up a good position to join the army
for France. ‘They’ve accepted me, mother,’
he said. ‘For what?’ said she. ‘l’ve been
up to the recruiting office to enlist.’ And
that American mother just answered quietly,
‘You couldn’t do better, my son!’ ” Here is
the photo, of the lady whose sweet musical
lilt is often a feature at concerts for patri-
otic or charity events. She is the wife, of
course, of Hugh Ward, of “the Firm.”
“Look at that view —right down to North
Head from this window,” she laughs, “and
then say you think I could leave Sydney to
live even in America.”
Another lately-returned American “just
loves little New York.” “Y’ know,” he said,
“this place does look a bit cheap after you’ve
been home. It’s the suburbs as much as any-
thing. Their untidy fences always hurt me.
Here you’ve got something pretty good : next
door there’s a dilapidated wreck of palings.
But it’s glorious out of doors. I just love
getting in the car and running out a few
miles and camping for lunch. Know I never
had a meal home except breakfast during
the holidays? Now, you can’t do that in
New York. There’s no place handy where
you can pull up and camp. Of course there
are plenty of beautiful spots, but they’re
private property. It’s the bush and the
quiet beaches right alongside Sydney that
have got me.”
You remember Dr. Saumarez Smith, my
dear—the kindly old prelate who wore the
first Anglican archgaiters in Sydney and
wouldn’t pay more than a shilling for his
cab fare to Macleay-street? His daughter
Ursula, who has been'teaching young Binghi
at the abo. mission station at Yarrabah
(N.Q.), is announced as engaged to parson
J. T. Perry, lately chaplain at the mission.
Her sister is married to W. H. Stevenson,
who expounds the Word in a Brisbane sub-
urb, and was once genthelp or something
to the late Archgaiters.
The Countess Morner, who was Mrs.
Henry White, left Sydney the other day for
the United States. Before she went she con-
fided the interesting fact that she was going
to be married over there. The gentleman
who has tempted her into a third matri-
monial venture is, she says, literary.
Thirty officers drew in their chairs at the
Wentworth Hotel on Friday to farewell
Colonel “Bob” Owen, retiring commandant of
the Menangle Light Horse Camp. Lieut.
“Dad” Drummond, D.C.M., mess president,
was in charge of the jolly assembly, which also
included Brig.-General George Lee, D.S.O.
(District Commandant) ; Brig.-General J.
M. Antill, the incoming camp commandant;
and Lieut.-Colonel R. W. Lenehan, V.D.
After the cigars and good stories had gone
round, a general advance was ordered on
one of the theatres.
Major Shillington has given up the heart-
breaking job of organising-secretary to the
State Recruiting Committee. Tue thorny
task of beating up unwilling eligibles is now
made harder by the shuffling of the Prime
Minister out of his pledges and the terrible
delay in practical repatriation work. Quiet
and tactful, Shillington (who has done his
bit and can’t get the doctors to pass him
again) had the regard of his colleagues in
the “Where-are-tlie-lads?” office. The women
officials added their token of goodwill to the
male staff’s farewell gifts.
As Sir Harry Barron was Cocked Hat at
Perth when the Sydney captured the Emden,
he was detailed to hand over the piece of
plate and medals which Westralia bought
with a shilling subscription for the cruiser’s
officers and men. Altogether £4OO was thrown
in the hat, and the proceeds were recently
delivered on the vessel’s deck under a foggy
English sky.
The Aubrey Murray family— lie's a
brother of the Papuan Excellency—are in
trouble about their second son, Gilbert. He
has been reported wounded and missing. The
young man was named after his learned
uncle, the Oxford don, whose wife. Lady
Mary Murray (a daughter of the Earl of
Carlisle), is hustling round looking for a
ti’ace of the Sydney lad.
It was Lady Mary Murray, by the way,
who classed ostentatious weddings in war-
days as “unthinkable.” So her daughter
astonished smart society by driving up with
father to a London registrar’s office and link-
ing her hand in her new husband’s without so
much as one fashionable bonnet in the offing.
Not so many years
ago two small heels
made a great clatter
under the Harry
Rickards manage-
ment. The orchestra
was often half a beat
behind. It wasn’t
young enough to
keep up with the
fairy patter of seven
or eight summers,
which dances like
the morning breeze,
because it must. At
seven Eileen Boyd
was doing a child
“turn” in the music-
halls of Maoriland,
with whistling and
clogs to back up her
lilt. Since then she has travelled far and
seen the wonders of the world. At 17 she
was a pupil of the kindly Steffani. who with
his wife pulled up the family sticks and left
for Paris, taking the Sydney girl under his
wing. Later she went to London to com-
plete her vocal gymnastics under Sir Henry
Wood. A luscious contralto is the result.
After eight years’ absence and a wide pro-
fessional experience—she was with the Butt-
Rumford Red Cross tour last year—Eileen
has come home to tell mother and daddy all
about it.
Though still a pretty little girl in appear-
ance, Eileen is a war-widow. Married to Cap-
tain Roberts, R.N.R., about three years ago,
her husband broke, down under the pressure
of taking troops to and from the Dardanelles.
“Just what that meant,’’ says the contralto,
with brimming eyes, “was this. My hus-
band, at any time a delicate man, given to
worry about his responsibility, was often
three days and nights on the bridge, watch-
ing for submarines, and the strain was
awful. On his last trip to England he was
promised a fortnight’s leave, but he couldn ? t
be spared. In days he was sent back
to duty and fell from the transport’s bridge.
‘Double paralysis due to overwork’ was the
verdict.
“On the voyage out we —my small son is
two years old—rammed a submarine and
travelled with a hole in our bow to St.
-John’s, Nova Scotia. We had 1700 passen-
gers on board, including 500 Canadian wives
and widows returning with their children.
But there was no panic or excitement: we
stood with our lifebelts on, waiting to take to
the boats, till that danger was past. Oh, it’s
good to be home —you just don’t know what
war means in this beautiful country,” sighs
the little widow. I send you a little picture
of her bonnie face.
Among the “Bing Boys” now grinning at
Her Majesty’s audiences, Minnie Love is the.
most expensive charmer. She finishes up, so
far as the boys are concerned, in a frock of
molten gold that appears to have been
poured on to a Chinese pagoda, set aside to
cool, then passed on with its peaked skirt
and gold topknot to Minnie; There’s an all-
feather frock in icecream pink for which the
ostrich has suffered heavy losses. A hat of
the same, only more so, sets quarter of an
acre of fluff waggling on the lady’s yellow
locks.
Visitors from Hay district, Mr. and Mrs.
Roy McLean, are housed in Sydney for a
few months, and then the bush will reclaim
them. Mrs. McLean was once a Dixie; she
grew up in the Fog and annexed her husband
in Belgium, whither he went in khaki and
she on a Red Cross job. The pair are re-
lated to the Edgecliff family of that ilk, one
of whom, Matron Hester McLean, of Maori-
land, recently got honors for pioneer war-
work in Egypt.
A line from Brisbane:—
Among the Year honors was a decoration for
war-work for George Frederick Davidson, who has
won distinction as an engineer in England. A Bris-
bane Grammar bov, lie passed on to Sydney Uni., and
won a travelling scholarship in engineering, which
took him to Cambridge about eight years ago. Since
then he has been at engineering works in Newcastle
until the outbreak of war, when he was put in
charge of a munition factory at Leeds. He is one of
the first members of the new British Empire Order.
When Mrs. Abbott came north to gather £6OOO
for the Scottish Women’s Hospital there was con-
siderable doubt as to her prospects of success. But
Caledonia knows how to give as well as make, and
thus far Queensland has put £7200 in the hat,
with an expense sheet of less than £420.
W. F. Dunn, M.L.A., the ex-school teacher
who recently returned from the Front —he
is still on the invalid list—was married in
Mudgee the other evening to Minnie
Durrington.
There’s to be the annual party —this year
makes the fourth —in St. Vincent’s Hospital
Nurses’ Home on January 23. It’s a gas-
light gathering, with progressive euchre and
music.
The Governor-General’s intelligence de-
partment will be henceforth in charge of
A.D.C. Clifford, who takes the Private Sec.’s
chair lately occupied by Francis Harman.
The latter hailed from Samoa and looked
after the Fairguson’s letters for about six
months.
Brisbane will supply a new curate to St.
James’s. His name is Collis, or something
(hat sounds like it. and he once saved souls
iii Borneo. Meanwhile the amiable James
Cheong charms even the stern verger with
his silky manners. He stepped into the
breach for love, as he’s on holiday from his
Molbouime tabernacle.
You can’t look at a Path 6 picture of an
honors-giving scene presided over by King
George without noticing that the royal left
knee twitches continuously. War nerves, no
doubt. A grey beard lias also been acquired
since the trouble started and thrones began
to tumble.
The Darling Point limb of the A.M.C. is
going to resume its knitting on January 17.
Yours affectionately.

Melbourne, January 14. 11)18.
My Dear Yarrie, —-
Lady Helen, attended by gentlielp Kerr-
Pearse, went to the Princess’s on Friday
night to see the last of Allan Wilkie in
“For the King.” It was a special military
performance in aid of the Red Cross Rest
Home, and was worked up by the nurses,
aided and abetted by Bishop, who is presi-
dent of the Rejected Volunteers’ Association.
The bar of the theatre (now cleared of liquid
refreshment) was occupied by nurses with
sweets and kewpies for sale.
Lady Helen sat in the centre of the dress
circle along with Matron Milne, of the Rest
Home, and Matron Anderson, of Caulfield.
The stalls were full of white-coiffed girls,
and lots of the darlings were dodging about
with baskets of dolls dressed in battalion
colors. Mrs. Billy Hughes sat in a box
with Billy’s secretary, Mr. Dean, and his
brand-new wife. John Forrest and his wife
were there. Sol Green lurked in another
box, where he was found and besieged.
Young Bishop aforesaid has a great
scheme in hand to put up a new Red Cross
Kitchen. He and other rejects intend to
make a one-day building job of it, and they
are rounding up carpenters and other crafts-
men. The organiser is a son of Archdeacon
Bishop, of Bendigo.
A new idea for Red Crossers. Mrs. Massey
is helping the Wellington (M.L.) lot make
a name-quilt similar to one she saw in an
English hospital. It had been produced in
Dunedin and was a patchwork of names
worked by people who had boys at the Front.
The wounded soldiers found great interest
in searching for names they knew. Welling-
ton is fairly on the job now, and doubtless
other towns' and districts will follow suit.
Word floats in from Colombo that Arthur
Harris (who married Carmen Pinschof) has
enlisted with an English regiment which he
will shortly join. When hubby leaves for
the trenches Carmen intends visiting Mel-
bourne.
Slender Mrs. Leroy Burnham, who in pre-
war days as Margaret Niall cheered many
a dull social function, has just sent a cable
to papa, re brother Arthur, who was posted
missing. He is in the hands of the Bodies,
.a prisoner, but well. Leroy Burnham was
aide to the Denmans and was winged during
the retreat from Mons.
The Frank Grey Smiths’ elder daughter,
Mona, has taken over Marian Campbell’s
job as hon. sec. to the Y.M.C.A. Soldiers’
Tub in St. Ivilda-road. It’s a real long-
('ljinnee job, lasting from 9 a.m. until 9
p m and needs a girl with a cartload of
energy . A ud R () od temper.

Archpush-up Mannix has retired to the
tree-fernery cloisters of Lome, the Western
District’s most exclusive sea-sidery, and pro-
mises to shut off steam for a month while
lie is dipping his toes in the ocean, so kindly
kept free of submarines by the brutal
British.
A Military Medal case that appeals speci-
ally to Melbourne softgoods, apart from per-
sonal friends of the medallist, is that of Pri-
vate A. B. Sanders, of the A.M.C. He is a
member of the old Leviathan clothing firm,
one of Melbourne’s gilded johnnies, and he
won his bauble by brave work in a service
where honor and glory don’t come much in
the workers’ way. Private A. B. Sanders
got his M.M. for gallantry in stretcher-
bearing.
Our late Ex. Carmichael lost the bulk of
his great collection of Australian black opals
in one of the torpedoed boats. One piece
remains safe in Australia, and that was a
wedding gift to Mrs. Harrison, wife of oUr
Flying major. It is a magnificent piece
with the Southern Cross set sheer into the
jewel in diamonds. Some nivstical diamond
letters are interwoven on another facet.
The Frank Mitchell daughter put her name
down for enlistment as probationary nurse,
and Auntie Melba gave her an encouraging
pat on the hack. But the young enthusiast
felt rather injured when Melbourne Hospital
called her up on Christmas Eve.
Sister Constance Murray, a sister of our
Mrs. Clyde Norton, of Toorak. was lately
transferred to England, after many months’
service in France, and is now at the new
Hospital for Nurses at Soiithwall Terrace,
London. The Nightingales call it "The
Nunnery,” as it is staffed by women, and is
for women only. But as Dr. Henry Maudsley
is in charge, male wags call it, in the Cockney
tongue, “Henery’s Hennery.” The hospital,
given by a rich Australian, Mrs. Hall, was
opened by Andrew Fisher some months ago.
It is fitted to perfection.
The ordination of H. It. Potter as an
Anglican priest by ArchgaiteSs Clarke the
other day establishes an Australian record.
Potter’s father, who is an archdeacon at
Wangaratta, was ordained in 1886 at Bal-
larat by Bishop Thornton; and his father.
Canon Potter, now deceased, got his “orders”
in 1859 from Dr. Perry, who then bished
at Melbourne. There is no other instance of
a third generation of Anglican priests, all
ordained in this country. The call of the
collection plate runs in the family; old
Canon Potter’s father looked after one in
Ireland, and his brother was the first Angli-
can archdeacon in Ballarat.
Miss Adelaide’s evidence :
Josiah Sytnon has passed over to the Art Gallery
Frederick Chester’s painting, “Making Hay While
the Sun Shines,” which came to Australia for the
Sydney Jubilee Exhibition. The canvas was bought
there by the late Alexander Hay, but the K.C. has
owned it for some years. The Symons’ eldest son, by
the way, has won the M.C. He is an adjutant in the
Coldstream Guards.
Birkenhead, which is more or less part of Port
Adelaide, is a cheerful spot to inhabit these days.
The wheat-stacks along the wharves are swarming
with weevils, which are overflowing to the houses and
shops and crowding out the adjacent resident. The
insects are literally in masses, smothering any food
that is left uncovered, dropping from the ceilings
and attaching themselves firmly and impartially to
saint and sinner. They have to take in the saints
because there are not enough sinners to go round. One
desperate shopman painted his building with sheep-
dip, but that gave only temporary peace.
The Wattle Day League’s final count of its Button
Day takings showed that the effort netted over
£2OOO towards the hospital wing which the League
hopes to build and equip at Keswick.
Two S. Australians, Surgeon-Colonel Hayward and
Major E. J. Sexton, figure in the list of the new
Order of the British Empire.
Died at Norwood on Sunday, Mrs. A. A. Allen,
well known in musical circles as Miss Murielle
Woods. She was married only a short time ago.
The U.S.A. gave a healthy account of mem-
bership: and finance at its annual meeting. The
number on the roll is 3000, and the assets are over
£l6OO. There is a heap of work for the assoc, to
do, if only in the direction of galvanising the
authorities into some practical effort to deal with
the problem of returned men before the end of the
war unloads them on us in thousands. Captain
Blackburn, V.C., was re-elected president. An
effort is being made to give a fresh start to the
Returned Soldiers’ toy factory, which began here
under Government auspices, but did not flourish.
The Cruickshanks have had news of the death of
their only son Errol, who was' killed in action in
France on Christmas Day. He was in the Gallipoli
landing, and remained on the Peninsula till the
evacuation, coming through the fighting there and in
France safely till he joined, the Great Host. Lieut. R.
G. Raws, son of the Rev. John Raws, who has been
missing since July, 1916, has also been officially
reported killed.
We are to have another league—-the Liberty League
-—whose business it will be to oppose “intemperate
legislation” especially as it affects amusements,
sports and the rest. Bert Sayers is president, George
Auld and James Hall vice-presidents.
The S. 11. Officer widow, who was to have
gone to Tassy for son Cairns’s wedding with
Louise Sexton, called a halt in a private
hospital. Whilst hurrying down the Col-
lins-street incline she caught her foot on the
pavement and was upset. The result was
an injured hip, which will take some weeks
to recover.
Henry Cornish and his smart little
missus, back from a five months’ ramp
through Tahiti and Maoriland, have un-
packed in a South Yarra flat, and Mrs.
Henry is once more busy on Braille work
for blind Anzaes. In July the pair box up
for the Bermudas. They have got the travel
habit badly.
“Bandbox” Brown, of Hobart, has been
nibbling his daily bread at Menzies’ for the
past few weeks. He is the Wallington of
Tasmania, and jogged the elbow of more
Governors than you would think, for he is
a time-defying person with a deceptive
suggestion of youth about him.
Young Bob Brownless, of South Yarra,
has just returned from th<h wars. He is a
grandson of knight Anthony Brownless, a
whilom Chancellor of the University. His
grandmother was a niece of Catherine
Hayes, the singer of pleasant memory. Bob
is one of Daigety’s promising young men
and fought at Gallipoli, Pozieres and Gaude-
court, and in the last engagement had the
rough luck to lose his left leg.
The Crevellis have received news of the
marriage in France of son Louis, the lucky
mademoiselle being Yvonne Houet, of Paris.
The engagement was announced some time
ago, and the lady’s photograph is a charming-
recommendation. Louis is in the French
A.M.C.
Colonel Kent Hughes’s daughter was a
fifth-year med. at Melbourne Uni. when sen-
sational things happened, and she became a
wife and a widow within a few days. But
these things failed to interrupt the even
tenoh of her studies. She has duly
finished her course at the Shop and
has just established a record by be-
coming the first woman doctor to hold
a resident-surgeon’s job at the Geelong Hos-
pital. Dad himself, by the way, is nowa-
days doctoring soldiers at Petrograd, unless
since latest advices he has been Bolshed or
transferred to serener scenes.
The language of the battlefield is infec-
tious. Archie Whyte, Prime Minister
Hughes’s publicity agent, is about town
boasting that his wife captured an eight-
pounder in Christmas week. He has been
christened John Stirling Whyte.
Mrs. Tom Carrington, a sister on the inky
way, who was wielding a pen with ease and
dignity when many of us sported pigtails,
passed out last week. As “Queen Bee,” Mrs.
Carrington supplied the Australasian with
formal conversation concerning the doings of
the Very Best People for 30 years. Lately
she had been ailing, and was helped in her
work by her two accomplished daughters.
There was nothing of the Bohemian
in the dead penwoman; She had very
definite ideas about the dignity of
her calling, and it says much for
her personality that she forced the snob-
ocracy to accept her at her own valuation,
even in the days when journalists were
looked upon as quaite impossible. All who
know them feel much sympathy for “Queen
Bee’s” daughters and her widower, Tom
Carrington, who was one of Victoria’s earli-
est black-and-white artists, and has done the
theatrical first nights for the Argus for
many years,
None but his own family and the pr'nter
ever know the name of the printer’s devil.
When John Pascoe Fawkner, in ’3B, started
to rake Melbourne news together with a pen
( that was before he got the old press now in
Melbourne Public Library) he hired a boy
named John Brophy, and young John did his
job with pen and press and grew up and left
without ever getting his name on the early
journalistic records. His youngest daughter,
Catherine Frances Brophy, died in a Malvern
private hospital after operation last week.
Though she was no writer herself she had
a keen eye for character and a good story,
and was cheery to the last, nobody but herself
suspecting that the end of her own tale was
very near. Her nephew “Kodak” recollects
that she always disliked telling stories with
a tragic finish.
The McCubbins. mourning their genial
artist dad, are up at their house,
which hangs from a garden on the Woodend
side.
Last week-end the gentle and gracious
Dolores jaunted to Tasmania with profes-
sional and Cross ends in view. For the
first time since the Huns began to devastate
her native land the Dolores is using her
Voice for her own profit as well as the profit
of the Cause. Her first concert at Hobart
was billed for this Wednesday.; The trip
will last three weeks, and the songstress
thinks of it as a pleasant preliminary to
another tour.
The London Royal Academy of Music
examiner, V. Hathaway, has seen moi’e of
Australia than many Australians, for it is
his job to tour the country judging the scale
players and howling dervishes. He has just
been joined by his good-looking missus, who
has been nursing in a military hospital in
Rouen.
“The Navy Now,” sung in “Aladdin” by
Andrew Higginson, was written and com-
posed by Higgy all by himself. The once-
adored beauty mummer is not the merry
Andrew we knew in “Merry Widow” days.
He is a very subdued, domesticated person
and an industrious worker. Daisy Rivette,
who reveals a pleasing figure as Prince
Pekoe, is Mrs. Andrew.
The Sassiety regiment paraded at dearly
full strength in the front stalls to see the pet
musicians, Edward Cahill and George
Brookes, begin their vaudeville career at the
Tivoli. It was a crowded Saturday-night
house, and the fine musical turn went with
a roar. Two laurel wreaths were sent and
barrowloads of flowers; but the youths
wisely had them side-tracked to the stage-
door in dread of the howls of the gods.
Muriel Starr has perched herself on the
heights of Toorak in Huyton, St. George's-
road, the humpy of Mrs. Lyon. She has
already given the Toorakians something to
chatter about, as at Christmas time she was
serenaded by the Hawaiian Ukulele Band,
now appearing in “The Bird of Paradise.”
The Xaicest do so love a free show ! And
it delights the show people to give it to them
if it means a good ad.
The ever-moving Freddie Shipman pair
spent Christmas in Japan and sent a cooee
to Australia from Nagasaki.
Mallacoota Inlet, the faraway fishing
place 'which used to be sacred to the male
sport who left his women in softer places, is
in danger of becoming fashionable. This
year several women strapped their suit-cases
on to the motor and trundled there with the
men. Mrs. Felix Meyer, a delicately-fair
University girl, went with her doctor hus-
band in the wake of Allenby, a busy Roads
Boards man. Critchley Parker, of the
Mininci Standard, arrived the day after the
referendum, and his sporty wife went to
help untangle the fishing-lines. The widow
Horsfall also arrived surrounded by the halo
of her banking account. A plucky maiden
from Ivew, Miss Larkins, ran her own car
through the forests from Melbourne and then
carried her mother and three men-cousins on
to Sydney.

A SAFE PROTECTOR
ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Eracle” : Barcoo rot, veldt sore of South
Africa and septic trouble of Egypt are all due to
the same cause —a small bacillus. Prcf. Martin,
pathologist of the 3rd A.G. Hospital, studied it
when stationed in Abassia (Cairo). Some of our
soldiers developed sores which they called Barcoo
rot, and they were right. The important point to
remember in the treatment is that the responsible
little devils spread through the hairs of the skin;
sometimes they squeeze themselves into the hair-
roots. Prof. Martin discovered this and advised
A.M.C. doctors to pull off the hair about one inch
around the sore. We did that and painted the
skin with strong iodine; any ointment over the sore
would do. As a result no sores would develop and
the patient would be well in a few days.
“True Bill” : Talking of diseases peculiar
to the Outback reminds me of the blindness that
prevailed 30 years ago in the Never-Never of Western
Queensland, caused by flies from the filthy and
unholy blacks’ camps attached to the stations. It
wasn’t safe to allow a fly to get near the eye. The
few white babies there were had to have their heads
encased in mosquito-net during the daytime. The
saddest sight was that of strong, hefty men and bonnie
boys and girls, quite blind —nothing to be seen of
the eye but a white slit. The disease and scandal
became so great that the Government sent up experts
to take a hand. One of the first things ordered was
the dispersal of the nigger camps. I well remember
as though ’twere a nightmare a drive of 18 hours
in an open buckboard with an infant supposed to
have contracted the awful disease (there was yellow
mucus in the corner of the eye)—the blistering
sun, the swimming of the billabongs and creeks (it
was flood-time), the broken traces, the drowning
of the horses, the arrival at the doctor’s at 2 a.m. to
be soundly cursed for waking him out of his sleep,
and finally the verdict that the kid had a cold in
its head!
“Barcoo”; You’ll agree that it is impos-
sible to ; carry water in open petrol-tins or oil-
drums suspended from a vehicle. Right. Well,
here’s a simple remedy. Throw a couple of handfuls
of eucalyptus leaves on top of the water, and not
enough will escape to give a mosquito a decent
drink. Another inconvenience picnickers and mug
bushmen suffer from is water smoked when boiling
the billy. To obviate this get two green twigs,
trim them of leaves and lay them across the top of
the vessel. The water can then be boiled with
impunity and the most sodden twigs available.
“Captain”: When my New Guinea boy
whom I had sent with a note to a neighboring
planter reached the house' he could see no one
about, so he “chanced an eye” through a French
window. What he saw and heard rattled him
badly, and, not waiting to deliver the missive, he
broke all records for home. In a frenzy of excite-
ment he opened out: “Me go longa house belonga
other fella master. Me lookem. Missus belonga
other fella master stop longa house. Missus take
lik lik bokhus (little box). She put him
longa big fella bokkus (piano). She sit longa top
lik. lik bokkus. Missus open mouth (lid) belonga
big fella bokkus, fightem teeth (piano keys) plenty
strong too much. Big fella bokkus he cry too
much. Me fright. Me go.”
“Timberite”: The 70ft. ironbark pole
mentioned in a recent Bulletin by “Jackdaw”
would require a long ladder, to reach to the top of
the stick that raised the mill-funnel at Mount Rous,
Richmond River (N.S.W.), in September, 1898. Cut
at the first limb, this pin provided a 92ft. sheer-leg
to swing the 110 ft. tube on to its 12ft. high
pedestal. The stack weighed 20 tons and bent the
back of the straining ironbark, but up she went
without any trouble.
POOR SPORTS.
BUSHMAN : “ I’d like to see him chuck young
Bob!”
OLD BOB: “You loafer phella like it plurry
fun, you do! That yarraman break it
young Bob neck, baal you pay it
doctor.”
“Canine Lover”: This is not known to
everybody, but it ought to be. Five or six eggs
beaten up and poured into a dog that is strychnine-
poisoned will make him as good as new.
“S. S. Severn”: I think I found more
superstition in the quiet places in England than I
did in equal time in Papua. One day last spring
Margaret and I were mooning along Madeira Walk
in Falmouth, when I noticed a kind of little umbrella
fern twirling round and round amongst the under-
growth. It whirled at such a rate and in so strange
a fashion (really as though someone were rolling it
between the palms) that I stood watching it. And
suddenly it seemed as though a tiny man about a
foot high, dressed all in grass green, with big and
very bright eyes, and wearing a high green cap, were
standing beside the fern staring up at me. As I
watched he faded away. When we got home I told
our landlady about him, feeling that she would laugh
at my fancies. She did not. She said there must
be very few of the Little Green Men left now. She
had never seen one herself; but her grandmother
had, many years ago. And to be prepared for bad
news. Which came.
AN IMPOSITION.
COHEN (who has been asked for a subscription to the Disabled Firemen’s charity): “Vat!
Give you a thubthcription! V’y, you're the ruination of bithness.”
“Ritasun”: Now that the stacks put by
last harvest are being dismantled in Northern Vic.
thousands of mummified mice are being brought to
light. Every crevice is chock-a-block with them.
They evidently sought shelter from the cold, and,
having lived their fleeting period, died. Some of
the groups of corpses are in quaint formation, all
headed one way. Cocky in those parts reckons that
the pest is done with —anyhow, for a time. But is
it? Echuca way thousands of mice, alive and
hearty, are making themselves at home. Only one
thing is certain, and that is that the suicidal mad-
ness of exterminating the native birds, particularly
the night-owl, mopoke and curlew, is pi'oducing its
aftermath; and when the locust hordes come, and
the frogs, and the pumpkin beetles, and the green
grubs, the lesson will be driven home with the
mallet of remorse.
Mrs. Lance Rawson: In the late ’fifties
r small live tortoise was found embedded in coal
it Newcastle (N.S.W.) 400 ft. below surface. Mr.
Kean, the then Government Geologist, had the little
creature for many years in a globe of water in his
drawing-room till it grew too big and was passed
on to the museum. When first released from its
coal bed it measured a shade over an inch in
length, but in six weeks’ time it had grown to five
and a half inches. It took a very long time to
attain any considerable size, however.
* * * *
“Tongio”; A wayback from the Omeo
Plains made a fuss the other day over a trifle. He
was a passenger on the Melbourne train and fol-
lowed the rush into the refreshment bar. His por-
tion of steak and kidney pudding was rapidly vanish-
ing, when he went up-counter to hunt for butter.
Presently he returned and continued his feed. It
was when he looked for his tea that the trouble
started. The cup was empty, though he hadn’t drunk
any. It occurred to him at this point that on return-
ing with the butter he had taken on someone else’s
leavings. He was making his troubles heard when
an ancient and very insanitary Chow chipped in and
accused him of stealing his dinner. The next I
saw of the cocky he was on the platform looking
very unwell.
* * * *
“Ghooliman”: There are several other
howls that can be forwarded as far as “coo-ee.”
Place your hands to your mouth and try “koo-who-
whoo,” raising the voice upon each syllable. Years
ago Dick Bradley, whom I worked for out Cool-
gardie way, used to swell his neck like a gohanna
when you pelt a rock at him, and beef out “Yah-
hoo! ” The farthest man in the gang never missed it.
* » * *
“Machete”: Those Queensland kids who
yarded up a mob of snakes in a hollow log (B.
6/12/’l7) in all probability butted into the middle
of a courting bee. These gatherings are as char-
acteristic of snake society as they are of more
exalted circles, and in both cases the lady’s admirers
are liable to get into holts with each other in the
presence of the blushing and palpitating prize.
‘“Capital”: If Andy Fisher could see
Canberra now he would, as a true Scot, be proud of
his godchild. The wet season has suited the weeds
all to pieces on the flats along the Molonglo, and
there’s about the finest show of Scotch thistles, star
thistles and variegated thistles in Australia. The
Scots thistles are the bonniest of the lot, forests of
them 7ft. high, and so thick in places that a rabbit
has to back out if he wants to scratch himself.
Indeed, what with Bathurst burrs and about a thou-
sand other weeds as well as the thistles, the capital
site can show as representative a collection of Aus-
tralian vegetable pests as any goat could wish for.

BILLO'S POINT OF VIEW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By the rock that’s like a pillow
And the homes of business fleas
‘Came the bottle merchant Billo
With his old horse Socrates.
Down the steep hill to the ferry
Billo picked his zigzag way;
And I thought his face seemed very
Thoughtful—for a Saturday.
On the kerb his cartwheel grated
And the bottles grated too,
While he slewed his load and waited
As old friendship bade him do ;
And methought I heard him mutter
Kindly words of blasphemy.
And he spat into the gutter—
And I spat in sympathy.
Maybe, out amongst the wattles,
At a villa spick and span,
Where he went for empty bottles,
lie had met a wowser man.
Or a woman with a mission;
Or a long speech made last night
By a puppy politician
Had raked Billo up orlright.
But the strength he gave me of it,
With an elbow on the shaft;
And I listened, to my profit,
While the Harbor water laughed.
Billo blinked at three-legged Brunno —
Dog that everybody knew —
And he opened out with “Dunno
What the world is cornin’ to.
“Wot them wowser blokes is givin’
Us is more than I can tell.
We don’t wanter go ter Heaven,
We don’t wanter go ter Hell 1
I’m a fool, an’ you are clever;
Both is mugs, an’ get bn fine;
Bottles full is your endeavor,
Empty bottles is my line.
“I ain’t one as turns me nose up
At each blessed thing I sees;
When I dies and turns me toes up
I jest want a little ease ;
I jest only wanter getter
Where I’ll have a beer, an’ kiss,
In a world a little better
An’ a little worse than this.
“What the Union crowd is doin’
I am damned if I can say;
We don’t wanter go ter ruin,
We don’t want a quid a day ;
We just want a bit er clobber,
An’ when things ain’t goin’ right
We can always find a cobber
Who will buck us up to fight.
“This old strike will be forgotten,
Like a hundred strikes before,
And the War be dead an’ rotten
When we have another war ;
And you’ll see the landsharks swimmin’
On the outskirts of the fray,
While weak men an’ lyin’ wimmin
Muck things up the same old way.
“For the sum of all this scrappin’
When yer use yer comminsense,
Is that nothing that can happen
Never makes no difference.
So I give it to you, knowin’
Mates are mates, say what they please—
There’s the punt! I must be goin’.
So long! Geddup, Socrates!”
Then I dreamed a space, and started ;
For it seemed another day,
And we just had met an’ parted,
Casual, on the Appian Way.
Gown and wreath and mail and helmet
(Over Time victorious)
Just two ancient Romans well-met,
Billo and Lawsonius.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.

“Phil Anthus”: “Jimmie Pannikin’s”
observations of the brolgas’ cheery habit of playing
eatch-as-catch-can with sticks (B. 6/12/’l7) move
me to remark that the big wedge-tailed eagles
(usually slighted with the title of eaglehawk) have
been known to take on a similar game. But these
sports bring their wings into play instead of run-
ning about like the cranes. Grabbing a back-log
from the earth an eagle will take it aloft in it 3
claws, drop it from a big height, and hook the
timber again in masterful style before it has finished
its whirling career southward. Most birds play
some game or other. The shining starlings of North
Queensland often give delightful exhibitions of
tumbling in mid-air, and I’ve frequently seen the
yellow 7 -tufted honeyeaters of southern Australia in-
dulge in corroborees c-f the most hilarious and fan-
tastic kind.
* * * *
“Bung”: “V.D.’s” theory that westerly
gales divert the Australian southerly Pacific current
seawards is probably correct. It is a known fact,
however, that a constant northerly undercurrent of
cold water traverses the eastern coast. For months
after the steamboat Bega went under off Cuttagee
beach some years ago, boxes of butter were swept
ashore many miles to the northward, and coal from
a sunken collier near Montague Island is regularly
strewn along the beaches as far north as Ulladulla.
FORCE OF HABIT.
BILL THE SHEARER ( absent-mindedly after
wounding his man ): “Tar ’ere!”
“Murkah”: A recent cold snap pinched
a pair of swallows that were nesting with a brood
under a verandah at Rooty Hill (N.S.W.). The
task of feeding and keeping the infants warm was
beyond the capacity of the two small parents, and
apparently the S.O.S. call was sent out last night.
Anyhow, by the light of a candle seven tails were
counted projecting from the nest. To my mind this
indicates the power of communication, and it aston-
ished me. I’d be glad to know if “E. 5.5.,” “Leo
Lear” and the mob generally would have been equally
flabbergasted.
PONIES—OR THE OTHER SORT?
BROTHER SNIFFES: “ Friend, will you come to our meeting?”
SPORTING GENT: “Vot is it, registered or unregistered?”
“Copabella”: The city fathers were dis-
cussing shrubs for the new park. The curator had
recommended a few Grevillea Robusta for a certain
situation. “Look ’ere, Mr. Mayor,” protested Cr.
Malaprop, “I’m an Australian, an’ I’m against
gorilla-busters 1 What’s the matter with native
things, I’d like to know?” After some discussion
the recommendation was adopted with the under-
standing that the curator would see that the Grevil-
lea was quite free from foreign disease.
“Eddyson”: Talking of the loneliness of
the bush with an old bushman, he drew my attention
to a peculiar development. I was able to verify his
idea with many experiences of my own. Said the
bushman: “The bush isn’t so lonely that it drives
them livin’ in it to break their necks lookin’ for
company. 1 know of fam’ly after fam’ly in the most
desolatest places who never speak to each other from
yeaf’s end to year’s end. There be whole fam’lies of
seven or eight who ain’t playin’ speaks, and haven’t
been for years past, ’n’ most probable won’t be for
3-ears to come. They tell of a chap out of Wagga,
New South, that’s had tellvphone wires run along his
fences to a neighbor’s three miles off. He’s got
rheumatics so bad he often can’t stir, but he don’t
speak to his wife ’n’ won’t. One day the neighbor
had him yellin’ over the tellvphone, ‘Fer Gorsake,’
he said, ‘ring up my wife ’n’ tell her the
horses is in at the wheat!’”
“Pick”: When help is called from mates
in the bush the soldier-bird is no shirker. I was
attracted by violent language and ever-increasing
numbers to a big manna-tree on the Monaro
(N.S.W.). Reinforcements were streaming in from
all points, and as they whirled among the forest
of limbs I saw the soldiers were fighting a blue
falcon. Though a much larger bird, he was several
times beaten off his perch by the onslaughts. Then
I discovered that his apparent helplessness was owing
to his having a young bird in his claws, so I
shoved into the argument with a Winchester, and,
as the chicken-killer fell to the ground, many of
the enemy thr"'bed feathers out of him, while others
escorted away the little fcl' v.v that had caused all
the disturbance.
“Jim Carew”: A scrap between two
Papuan natives isn’t in it for stoush and humor
(for the spectator) compared with a dinkum go
between two Australian gins. The combatants, each
provided with a light club, take their place in the
centre of a ring formed by their expectant tribes-
folk, and commence operations. One gladiator bends
forward from the waist and sticks her head out, and
the other does her best to split it open with the
club. Then it is the other lady’s turn, and this
goes on, whack for whack, until one of the bellig-
erents is laid out. She is then dragged into the
shade and left to recover as best she can. I have
seen a fight like this last for 20 minutes, and it is
a never-ceasing wonder to me that some of the
women are not more seriously hurt. They always
seem to recover. I have never been able to discover
how it is determined which madam shall have first
wallop.
“Norbell” : Seems that voracious eels are
submarining the fledglings of the black duck in Bris-
bane Botanical Gardens. Do eels favor game when
it’s on the menu, or is this an exceptional display
of frightfulness ?
“Haka”: At Marlborough (M.L.) the
other day a cocky barbered his jumbucks with a
horse-elippi.’ig machine. While his missus turned
the handle he hoisted 800 through the porthole.
During smoke-ohs the pair piclced-up, rolled,
classed and baled the clip. Any complaints?
“Tom-Tit”: Is there any authentic record
of a pure white magpie? For three years or moie
there has been such a freak, so the station-hands
declare, flying about on Allanvale, near Great
Western (Vic.). Its form is exactly similar to
that of the ordinary mag., and it mingles freely,
they say, with the common kind.
“Melos”: It’s a wonder that the blacks
didn’t long ago pass out under the ravages of
venereal diseases. Maybe they had some remedies;
I know of one, at least. That was a plant on the
N. S. Wales North Coast—the gigul-gigul. Binghi
chewed the leaves and swallowed the juice.
* * * *
“S.S.”: When the White Horse started
its pumps it gradually reduced the water in the
shaft of the old Johnnie-Come-Lately. The Johnnie
had not been worked for close upon 15 years, and
during all that time Tim the Cheese had remained at
Piebald, sustaining himself precariously by fossicking
up and down the flat, and taking on any old job
that was available within a radius of about three
miles. You could not have tempted him to go
beyond that with an offer of the State Governor’s
position, it would seem. Tim’s life interests circu-
lated around the old Johnnie shaft, in which until
lately the water had remained within 30ft. of the
surface. But the White Horse, actively operating
12-inch lifts, gradually drained the country, and
slowly the waters in the Johnnie, a mile away,
receded until there was 150 ft. of the 200 ft. of shaft
quite dry. The interest Tim the Cheese manifested
in this development was covert but keen. Twice a
day for months he had dropped a plummet into
the deserted mine. Then, finding apparently that
the water was at last getting no lower, he commenced
a curious task. He ran a rough sapling ladder down
the shaft, constructing the lengths on the surface,
and lowering and securing them one after the other.
From the lowermost ladder he sank a hide bag into
the water, and then, at the age of 04, he began
experimenting as a diver. How many times he went
down nobody knows, but eventually he was missing.
A ’party that discovered two lines leading into
the black waters at the end of Tim’s improvised
ladders drew one up. Tim was on the end of it, and
distressingly dead. They drew up the other line and
recovered a hide bag in which was over lloz. of
coarse gold. Later something like 2500 z. of similar
gold was found in an old mercury bottle under Tim’s
bunk. Then a feverish desire to get the rest of the
water out of the Johnnie-Come-Lately seized a small
syndicate. It spent £4OO, and discovered that Tim
by his cheap and primitive method had recovered
ail • the gold worth worrying about —it* had
been hidden in the shaft in a recess in the reef
behind one of the slabs, evidently by gold-stealers,
of whom, no doubt, Tim was one, and probably the
sole survivor. The Johnnie-Come-Lately miners who
had been putting by a little gold as a provision for
their old age were cut off from access to it by the
sudden and dramatic flooding of the mine. It was
Tim’s rotten luck to be snagged under a displaced
“centre” after getting his hands on the last few
ounce 3.

A VICTIM OF DRINK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bill Byblow drank a lot of beer.
To drink all day he’d gladly, sit,
But never did a person hear
The dipso, honestly admit
He drank beer for the love of it.
He drank because the day was hot;
He drank because the day was not.
He took a drop the time to pass ;
He guzzled to impede its flight.
With company he took a glass,
Or drank when lonely; or he might
Take to it if his boots were tight.
He even drank to some extent
To prove a temperance argument.
To Phinn’s hotel one summer night
He went alone, and up a spout
He clambered to the roof with quite
Heroic effort. Tearing out
A skylight, though uncommon stout
He battled through. At a.m. four
They found him on the bar-room floor.
Byblow had drunk a giant’s share;
He’d found the kegs of beer, and two
He'd carried up the cellar stair,
And sampled both by boring through.
When charged with stealing half a brew,
“I don’t like drink,” he said, “but may
Take something when it comes my way.”
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.

This used to be a proprietary co., and it
was 13 years old at the time the table
begins. When the public came in, 3oU 11U
pref. shares and 1500 £lO ordinaries (-
in all) were issued in part payment for the
business. Prefs. were limited to 8 p.c., but
in consideration of a further allotment ot
£l2OO the ordinaries agreed to let all shares
rank alike. Now, after eight years, in addi-
tion to paying an almost unbroken 8 p.c.
p.a., the co. has been able to set aside an
amount sufficient to cover goodwill, write ott
certain patent rights it had acquired, and
show the reserve of £689 in the last line.^
No doubt, it has been an anxious time tor
the co. during the last couple of years, with
uncertain supplies and prohibitive price ot
paper and expensive labor; but shareholders
have the satisfaction of knowing that so long
as there is a continuance of anything lihe
“business as usual” goods such as the co.
supplies will be in demand.

The £56,336 at 'me foot of the last column
includes fixed deposits £47,070 (£15,837
having been added during the year) and cash
on hand £9248. Meanwhile deposits with
the co. have increased from £90,968 to
£105,115. That is to say, the co. took
another £15,000 on deposit and passed it on
to a bank on fixed deposit. How this sort
of thing can pay The Bulletin doesn’t pro-
fess to know. The only explanation that sug-
gests itself is that the co. borrows from Peter
to pay Paul in case Paul should want his
money. Really neither Peter nor Paul need
worry, though. Apart from deferred deben-
tures, which rank after share capital as well as
after ordinary creditors, the co. owes £106,81 7.
Cash and liquid assets total £65,657. Thus
there is only £41,160 of debt not represented
by liquid assets, and against that there are
loans, premises and a few sundries valued
at £101,028. The creditors are thus very
well protected. The proportion pf cash held
to liabilities is exceptionally large; but
whether it is any larger than it ought to be
depends entirely upon the nature ot the
deposits. Assume that the co. held an aver-
age of £lOO,OOO throughout the year, and
assume further that deferred debentures cost
£5OO, then the deposits cost as an average
about 2\ p.c.; and that looks like call money.
There might be something m that. It SUU
people who usually have some sort of a
current-account balance established a poo ,
they might easily, through the pool, main-
tain a fixed deposit of 50 or a hundred
thousand, because it could scarcely ever
happen that the uninvested money of all ot
them would be low at the same time. ‘°"
the banks would look at it is another matter.
It isn’t at all certain that the Austral
Malay will get the Trong dredge for its new
flotation. Trong may take over the Lang-
suan option of 160 acres; anyway, it is clear
that the co. is putting itself m its best
possible position for some purpose.
Birthday Tunnel, Berringa (Vic.), has
been sold up after gasping on the call-list
for the best part of a decade. Its last des-
perate resort was to get an advance from
tho Government to test the Kangaroo reef.
Owing to the high price of mining material,
the plant and the scrap-iron realised suffi-
cient to repay the State and give shaie-
holders a small liquidation div.
