“Hannibal”: The heaviest lot of bullocks
ever put through a Queensland meat works
were handled some weeks ago at Alligator
Creek, Townsville. There were 628 in the
mob, and the average dressed weight was
0091 b. They had been driven 300 miles to
Winton, held up there by the late railway
strike for three weeks, and then trucked
370 miles to Townsville. Sid Kidman owned
them.
****
“F.”: The other morning I noticed that
we had a new brown earthenware teapot.
“Ha,” I remarked with a sneer, “more Jap-
anese rubbish, I s’pose. How much?” “A
shilling,” replied Araminta, “but I don t
think it’s Japanese.” “Soon tell,” I said,
and had a look at the maker’s mark under-
neath. Incredible as it may seem, the pot
was made in Australia, and was marked
“leadless glaze”—a good, cheap, substantial
article. Yet, regarding the Victorian co.
which produces this particular ware, a shop-
man told Araminta only a day or two later
that the concern didn’t deserve to succeed,
because it refused to sell its goods at a fair
price. The price lie meant, of course, was
the wholesale. If he were a wise shopman
he would thank God that Australian goods
are comparatively dear. If they weren’t his
customers would be coming in for two-
penn’orth of butter or a ha’p’orth of mar-
garine.
* * *
“Breeder” : “Eloc’s” self-congratulation (B.
20/12/T7) as a prophet in regard to the
effects of Justice Higgins’s station-hands
award, displays an intimacy with pastoral
matters which will surprise some of the old
’uns. But the prophet doesn’t seem to have a
very clear idea of the causes which lead up
to a good lambing percentage. Last year’s
high rates were due, not to anything his
Honor did or didn’t, but (1) to the flourish-
ing condition of the breeding sheep during
the mating season in the early summer ot
1916-17, whereby a high rate of impregna-
tion was obtained, which is obviously the
foundation upon which the result rests ; (-0
to an abundance of feed during tli e subse-
quent lambing season, producing the neces-
sary milk to sustain the born lambs; (3) to
suitable weather at that time. Cold, wet
weather will kill new-born lambs by the
hundred. Those of us who lambed in April,
1917. in mild warm weather following good
March rains, averaged well up towards the
century and without excessive labor; while
neighbors who lambed in May, in incessant
rain, sustained high losses of grown sheep as
well as of lambs, despite the “more atten-
tion” necessary in the circumstances.
“Eloc’s” predictions, especially that one re-
lating to next year, are about as well-
founded as the Pearson’s Weekly yarn
about the versifying bushies. He must, have
a quaint estimate of human nature, if he has
been under the impression that pastoralists
have poor lambings on purpose.
* * * *
“Old Salt”: Victoria’s new shipyard man-
ager, Bomphrey, late of Thorneycroft’s, of
Southampton, and Vickers and Son, of Bar-
row, reached Australia with the expiring days
of the year, and rushed to Melbourne, intent
on starting work on Wednesday morning
early. I wish him luck, and hope he will
continue full of energy. He will need it.
Under the nerveless regime of the Peacocks
the Williamstown State shipyard lias become
worse than a ghastly joke. On the main
slipway is the dredge Lady Stanley, which
has been ready for launching for many
months and is still ready. But for some
.obscure reason —probably because there isn’t
enough moisture to float her —there she re-
mains. Lying about the yard are tons of
iron and steel girders, bars, plates and stacks
of timber and woodwork, mostly exposed to
the weather, which naturally improves
girders and plates, to say nothing of dressed
timber. Spare propellers, crank-shafts, etc.
—which can be picked up for a song any-
where —lie about casually, just as a child
drops its toys. Yes, Bomphrey will need all
his vim.
* * * . *
N. S. Wales has in these days a law which
provides for the preparation of a complete
record of all house and land property in the
State, with the value thereof; and this
record is to be used as the basis for munici-
pal rating, for stamp duty, for compensation
in the event of resumption and for other pur-
poses. The owner may be called upon for
particulars—as a matter of fact, a large
batch of “owner’s returns” have lately been
sent out —but an official inspection will be
made, and thereon a valuation will be pre-
pared. If the owner (who will be told what
it is) doesn’t like it, he can object, where-
upon the valuation will be further investi-
gated; “otherwise the official valuation will
be entered on the valuation roll and cannot
be questioned.” When the job has been done
the owner must keep the Valuer-General
advised of alterations, either additions or
removals; and the purchase of freeholds or
leaseholds has also to be notified. It will be
a convenience, no doubt, to have a complete
roll, and the data will help in arriving at a
value; but the valuation roll obviously won’t
be good evidence of market value except at
the moment the entry was made. If Courts
and others don’t clearly recognise this, the
roll may do harm as well as good.
* * * *
“McNorth”: I nominate the Hann Shire
(N.Q.) as the largest in Australia. Its
headquarters are at Maytown, on the once-
famous Palmer Goldfield —now merely . a
collection of disused shafts and crumbling
poppet-legs. The shire takes in the Cape
York Peninsula and most of the islands of
Torres Strait right across to Saibai, a couple
of miles off the Papuan coast. Distances
are so great and transit so difficult that the
Hann civic dads are often a long time dis-
covering their neighbors. I have been here
on Cape York for over six years, but it was
only the other day that Maytown found it
out and sent in a bill for rates. I don’t
mind paying, but I’m sending in a complaint
about the state of the culverts _and street-
lighting up here to see if the 500-mile-dis-
tant Council understands sarcasm.
The women have scored all right. As soon
as the war started a thousand seedy gamps
waved over Australia and there was a wild
yell to dose the pubs in order to save money
and win the war. Now even the fluff mer-
chants are telling the papers that it was “a
criminal shame” the way the women came
in before and during the holidays and
“bought finery”; and the jewellers tell the
same story—it wasn’t good goods they sold
by the barrow-load, but stuff that looked a
lot for the money. Still, the woman will pay
in the end, as usual. Three-fourths of this
splash is on borrowed money ; and the bill
for interest and principal will come in pre-
sently. The best thing said about Australia
lately was the report of a mercer on his
Christmas trade. His business is, in ordi-
nary times, mostly with young men. .. “This
season,” he said, “things were rotten.”
* * * *
Though war-loan money is being gaily
splashed, it hasn’t taken the place of the
Holman Government’s borrowed quids in
N. S. Wales. In 1913 the new buildings
erected in the bloated city and its suburbs
cost £3,371,617. Last year the total was
down to £3,595,992, and it wouldn’t have
been that much if owners hadn’t been com-
mitted to contracts. Of all the 50 or so
suburbs Randwick had the greatest number
of new buildings, 428; its neighbor Waver-
ley was close up with 304 —-both figures fur-
ther proof of the call of the curling surf, for
Randwick takes in Coogee and Waverley
holds on to Bondi. It is Mosman, however,
that lifts its nose highest, Whereas the
average cost of the 5295 buildings in the
suburbs last year was £607, the average for
the 91 in Mosman was £1660. The most
cheerful circumstance, if there is any cheer-
ful circumstance at all about- the further
bloating of what was too bloated already,
was that the people are going further out
into the clean air. Overcrowded old Red-
fern reported only two new buildings, Pad-
dington 3, and Glebe half a dozen.
* * * *
“Edo” : Kalgoorlie and Boulder toilers are
bitterly lamenting Labor’s popular act of
leaping in the dark and landing in the bog.
Agitators, mostly of anti-conscription and
I.W.W. type, spurred the crowd on to demand
higher wages, but omitted to point out that
while the price of gold has remained station-
ary, all costs have soared kite-high, so that
the present is the very worst time to. come
for a rise in wages in this particular indus-
try. One result is that most Golden Mile
properties have shortened hands, while a
couple of others talk of closing down till the
war is over. It has its humorous side.
During the Referendum campaign the antis,
including Hugh Mahon, yapped that there
wasn’t enough man power left in the coun-
try to keep the industries going. Next day
he received a deputation representing, i
said, some hundreds, who requested him to
find them work!
* * * *
“Joyce” i For some time the Bank of N. S.
Wales has been supplying cheque-forms to
public schools with a view to making com-
mercial instruction more realistic. The goods
are identical with the genuine article, except
that they have “Specimen Form printed
across them. One kid, either for a Doke or
through carelessness —probably the former
—got away with a cheque for £o
payable to bearer, and dropped it in
the y street. It was subsequently picked up
by a park prospector, presented for payment
at a certain branch, and it took the whole
staff including the office-cleaner, to persuade
the good citizen that the spinnaker was
salted. #
AVE GLADIATRIX!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The sandals this season are so attractive that,
one would be tempted to buy even if they served no
practical purpose. Some, coming well up the back
of the leg and lacing in front with brigntly -colored
tapes, are modelled on the lines of a Koman gladi-
ator’s shoe.’’
Ah, Lalage, in ages gone,
When flame was clothed in flesh,
1 sat, a young centurion,
With you to watch the mesh
Of ltetiarius strive to check
The spear, sped clean and true,
Lest be should feel upon his neck
The gladiator’s shoe.
Ah, Lalage, those days are sped;
I now must play the part
Of him whose feet too late have fled
To save his stricken heart.
The net surrounds me ; hope is past;
No more you need pursue:
Upon my neck descends at last
Your gladiator’s shoe!
VICTORIA.
SANDALWOOD.
FRUGAL McDOUGAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At A. McDougal’s there’d been ructions
McDougal, frugal soul, had made
Such drastic, win-the-war reductions
In rations, that a sudden raid
Descended on him in the stable,
And all the angry workers swore
They’d have a really sumptuous table,
Or work for Angus never more.
Reluctantly the boss conceded
Two rashers with an egg for tea,
Which was the meat the toilers needed. '
“ ’Tis sair extravagant,” said he.
Old Angus sliced them, in the morning,
Two rashers each, cut very fine,
And gave the cook a solemn warning:
“Mon, fry them weel —the lard is mine!
The men came in with frightened faces
That evening at the stroke of five.
The harvester hooked Stringer s braces
And Stringer was no more alive!
McDougal gasped in horror holy.
“The Lord He giveth,” said the man,
“The Lord He tak’s awa’!” And slowly
He forked two rashers from the pan.

NOTICE.
RETURN OP INSURANCE PREMIUMS
REQUIRED FROM INSURANCE
COMPANIES.
Pursuant to the Fire Brigades Act, 1909,
and the Fire Brigades Amendment Act, 1910.
ALL insurance companies, as defined by
the above Acts, are hereby notified
that they are required to send to the Board
of Fire Commissioners of New South Wales
returns showing the total amount of the
premiums received by or due to such Com-
panies in respect of risks against fire during
the year ending 31st December, 1917, within
the Sydney Fire District and every other
Fire District in the State of New South
Wales to which the Fire Brigade Act 1909
and the Fire Brigades Amendment Act 1910
apply, less any part of such premiums actu-
ally paid by way of reinsurance to any other
Insurance Companies contributing under the
said Acts.
The time appointed for receipt of the
returns is on or before the 31st January,
1918; subject to a penalty not exceeding
five pounds for every day of default.
Forms, upon which to make the required
returns, may be obtained from the under-
signed.
By order of the Board of Fire Commis-
sioners of New South Wales.
H. M. WEBB.
Secretary.
213 Castlereagh Street,
Sydney.
12th January, 1918.

LOGICAL DYSPEPSIA TREATMENT.
IMPORTANCE OF ELIMINATING
ACIDITY AND FOOD
FERMENTATION.
During the last two or three years reports
have frequenty appeared in the press con-
< the remarkable value of bisurated
1 ia as an antacid ; and its ability to
ix ■ most forms of digestive and stomach
trouble arising from acidity and fermentation
has often been demonstrated. Until re-
cently-chemists could supply bisurated mag-
nesia in powder form only, half a teaspoon-
ful of which, taken in a little water after
meals, almost instantly stops all fermenta-
tion and neutralises acid, but sufferers will
be glad to learn that they can now obtain
this excellent remedy in the form of 5-grain
compressed tablets, two of which are equiva-
lent to half a teaspoonful of the original
powder. These new tablets of bisurated
magnesia can now be obtained of chemists
everywhere, in handy pocket flasks contain-
ing 60 and 150 tablets respectively, and it is
certain that they will prove as popular and
as efficacious as the original powder form of
bisurated magnesia, which of course is still
obtainable.-—Advt.

AT HOYT’S THEATRES.
The opening up of the New Year with a new releasing policy
proves one thing—that Hoyt’s are determined to give their
patrons the best entertainment available in Motion Pictures,
and when they- selected Paramount and Artcraft productions
exclusively for their Sydney and Melbourne Houses, it was like
acknowledging their superiority over all other makes of
pictures.
Paramount-Artcraft stories are wonderfully human interpreta-
tions of life in its various phases, w 7 ith such artists a's MARY
PICKFORD, PAULINE FREDERICK, MARGUERITE CLARK,
DOUGLAS FAIRBANKS, WM. S. HART, BILLIE BURKE,
GERALDINE FARRAR, VIVIAN MARTIN, SESSUE HAYA-
KAWA, FATTY ARBUCKLE, JACK PICKFORD, LOUISE
HUFF and a hundred other great stars of the film world, to
bring laughter and tears with their humor and heart-throbs in
stories by the greatest literary and dramatic writers of the age.
If your nearest theatre is not showing: Paramount Pictures, you are not getting all you ought to get. Let the
manager understand that you would like to see Paramount Pictures. He will when he knows his
patrons are concerned about what he shows, and you will get what is your right—the very
best entertainment for your money.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Hughes Government and its follow-
ing in the Legislature stood pledged as
deeply as men ever were in this world to a
certain course of action if Australia refused
to sanction Conscription. The moment they
were confronted with the job of making
good they began to waver. Left to himself
Hughes would probably have treated his
undertaking with indifference, gulped down
all the dirty water and stayed on his perch.
But the caucus had a haunting suspicion
that the electors ought to be given some sort
of dope; and so it happened that Hughes,
being assured that he had the “confi-
dence” of the caucus, and being in-
structed to save the Empire by saving
the party bacon, went through the
mockery of resigning on Monday, form-
ing the old Ministry into a new Ministry
on Tuesday, and resuming the old busi-
ness of selling gold-bricks at the old address
on Wednesday. So it all boils down to this
—that the Commonwealth has a Ministry
which it had been assured it would not have
unless it did something which it refused to
do.
FINDING AN EXCUSE.
“Well, I’ll let you put your arm round me
just this time, because —because you’re
going to the war and might get it blown
off.”
During the procession round the tart-shop
the S. M. Herald solemnly warned the
nation that anybody who made unmannerly
remarks about Hughes when the proceedings
terminated would be “playing the game of
Germany.” That, of course, is because
Hughes has “the King’s Commission.” Well,
Ryan of Queensland has the King’s Com-
mission, too; so if the Herald makes any
more unpleasant remarks about Ryan we
shall have to come to the unwilling conclu-
sion that the Herald has gone over to the
enemy.
Pearce made a dreadful mistake last week.
Attacked by J. D. Fitzgerald over the Cen-
sorship he declared that there wasn’t any
such thing as political censorship, and as
for other matters he challenged his adver-
saries to produce a single instance in which
war pews censored in England had been
expunged in Australia. That challenge was
the blunder-—Sydney Sun fell upon him
with one instance and promised a battalion
if he wanted them. Now, in the matter of
political censorship, The Bulletin is wait-
ing for Pearce to challenge it to produce
proof that this paper wasn’t prevented from
suggesting that somebody should take his
(Pearce’s) place as Minister for Defence.
The proof is waiting for him any day he likes
to issue the challenge.
Henderson, leader of the Labor party,
whom Lloyd George consulted before he
issued the latest peace terms, has put the
matter of post-war relations with Germany
more plainly even than the Prime Minister.
“We desire,” he said, “the fullest resumption
of international intercourse, and the repudia-
tion of all attempts at economic war.” There
is only one thing on which we can rely for
the protection of our industries from fiercer
competition even than they had before, and
LOOKING FORWARD.
CONSOLATORY ASS : “Well! Well! Beastly
things WILL happen, ye know.”
ENRAGED AND HEATED MOTORIST: “They
will!”
that thing is a high tariff. The question we
have to answer is whether we are going with
a high tariff to help ourselves to pay our
war debt or with a low tariff help the Ger-
man to pay the debt he has incurred in kill-
ing our sons and brothers.
The S. M. Herald may not be so far
wrong in suggesting that whoever now
speaks badly of Hughes will be doing Ger-
many’s work. For it certainly is true that
so long as Hughes remains Prime Minister
and the broken pledge is remembered against
him, so long is recruiting likely to be seri-
ously prejudiced.
Defence Minister Pearce last week de-
fended the censorship,, particularly against
certain mild allegations made by J. D. Fitz-
gerald k of Sydney. At the end of the argu-
ment all the public understood was that one
side was saying that the other was wrong.
Now, if Pearce is genuinely anxious that the
community should know the truth about the
censorship and the work it performs, there’is
an easy way of spreading the light. Let him
withdraw the regulation which keeps The
Bulletin from printing the instructions
issued by the Censor’s office. Then the
people will know, more or less, to what
extent ard on what subjects they can trust
the information (etc.) given to them in the
press, and what dependence they can place
upon the newspapers’ comments.
The Bulletin has little sympathy to
spare for the Labor party in relation to
Hughes. Holman stated the fact quite
plainly: “I have long known that Mr.
Hughes’s pledged word was utterly worth-
less.” - Holman knew no more and no less
than the rest of the Labor party. But the
party didn’t spurn Hughes on that account.
Instead, it regarded him as a valuable asset.
And there you have the rottenness to which
party has brought our politics. Hughes
is out of the party to-day. But there are
still in that party and in other parties men
who are known to be liars and simfffers and
twisters and tricksters, and who are valued
by the parties for the possession of these
qualities.
“Unpontius Pilot” pricks the card :—-
Of course you’ve heard that Ryan is to be next
Prime Minister of the Commonwealth, in which event
this is nearly how the reshuffle will go. Theodore is
to be Premier, Hunter having gone to England.
Assistant-Minister Fihelly is to be Home Secretary
in place of Huxham; Hardacre will be allowed to
join Huxham in retirement. Speaker McCormack
has made up his mind to get out of the Chair, and
he is sure of one of the four vacant portfolios—pro-
bably Works. Tom Jones, of Foggitt, Jones and
Co., will get the Agriculture Department. The other
jobs will Ire filled from the ruck, Gillies and Cooper
being likely. Huxham or Hardacre may get the
Speakership. Of course, in the event of Page resign-
ing his seat in the Reps, in favor of Ryan, he would
almost certainly be given a portfolio in the State
House.
You never lift off the cover and look into
the doings of Government departments with-
out seeing deplorable mismanagement—
mostly because it is only once in 20 times
that a business-man gets into Parliament;
and when he does fluke into Parliament he
vesry rarely reaches office. S. Australia
lately looked into its Government land pur-
chases. When they want land, the Commis-
sion reports, they have a way of saying so
to a land-agent, and instructing him to pick
up some to suit requirements; “but you will
look to the vendors for your remuneration.”
This at once makes him the agent of the
vendors, whoever they may turn out to be:
it relieves him of the moral responsibility of
keeping secret the purchaser’s name, and the
inquiry abundantly proved, what hardly
wanted proving, that even with the most
honest of men prices soar astonishingly when
the Government is the buyer.
Bowser is beginning his labors as Pre-
mier of Victoria by doing the obvious thing,
which is mostly the
hardest thing for the
politician to do, because
he lives mainly on flam
and make-believe. Gipps-
land, which in the early
days of the war was the
scene of a tremendous
Parliamentary excursion,
proposes to send 100 of
its leading whiskers to
Melbourne to interview
the Government on vari-
ous matters. As such a
trip would cost some-
body even more than the
previous excursion did,
Bowser has intimated
that he is going to take a
couple of Ministers and

From the Gallery:—
More honest people are grieving over Willie Watt
than any of the others. If he had held to his first
expressed intention of honoring the pledge, he would
have increased his chances of ultimately climbing
into the principal part. He enjoys a certain prestige
with the Nationalists, and it only needed a show of
honesty at a critical time to stiffen his reputation
with the influential section of Liberals who cannot
think of Hughes's conduct without holding their nose.
It was an exquisite opportunity for Watt, with the
possibility of enough haloes to set him up as a
hatter in the heavenly regions. To give, him his
due, he hesitated for some hours, while his office-
hungry colleagues danced about him in nervous
anxiety, and grovelled to him to respect the sacred
principle of Cabinet solidarity. Watt eventually fell
away, but he is at least entitled to a little credit
for having some compunction about the matter. The
readiness of the others to resume their jobs was
pathetic in the cases of Forrest and Glynn, but only
typical of men like Pearce, Jensen and Russell.
Nothing more could be expected from this precious
trio of dull invertebrates.
The first returns under the Federal war-
time profits tax were due last week, and
nobody in the wide world knows whether
the wretched thing will produce one pound
or five. According to statements made by
men who say they have been sounding mem-
bers, very few of those who voted for the
bills approved of them; which shows what
an enemy to democracy party government
is. One thing is certain: the measure is
putting a brake on industry in Australia.
To increase profit nearly everybody has to
risk something, with no certainty that the
extra profit will accrue; and if the State
collars half or three-quarters of it if it is
made, Private Enterprise naturally says that
it isn’t good enough to take the risk.
What is this nonsense about Hughes’s
police not being needed? Beech worth (Vic.)
Gaol has only one prisoner to four officers,
and there are dozens empty or nearly empty
all over the country.
At the root of the whole trouble with
regard to the utterly unsatisfactory war-
time profits tax lies the failure of the
Federal Government to distinguish between
the position in England and in Australia.
England didn’t want new industries started ;
Australia did- —with shipping scarce it was
a question of (1) starting new industries
here, (2) going without things or (3) pur-
chasing from Japan and other markets
AS YOU WERE.

handier than Europe. We ought, of
course, to have done everything pos-

effect, “If you do, you’ll take all the risk,

Health-Minister Downward is going to
try to do something which will put a halo
on the new Vic. Administration, if it comes
off. For some time spasmodic agitations
have been conducted, without the slightest
success, to get baby clinics established by
the Government. Now, perhaps noticing
that a few persistent people are still
attempting to wake the State conscience,
Downward has been talking the matter over
with Dr. Robertson, chairman of the Board
of Health, and it is quite likely that some-
thing may be done—there may even be as
many as 50 clinics, with a doctor and nurse
at each, to advise mother as to baby’s con-
dition. If Bowser can only get in a few
more reforms like this before the next crisis,
he will be doing pretty well for himself in
the eyes of a lot of people.
The completion of the East-West railway
puts the 4ft. °Jin. ahead of the sft. 3in. in
the race of the gauges: there are now 5455
miles of the one and 5011 of the other,
exclusive of a scrap or two of private lines.
But the two together don't represent half-
of the total railway mileage of the Common-
wealth ; and the bill for unification is being
piled higher, because Queensland continues
to build nothing wider than 3ft. 6in., and
pven the Commonwealth is spending an
extravagant sum on the same gauge in the
Northern Territory.
PROMETHEUS UNBOUND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The party has produced no rival to Mr. Hughes.”
He stands alone, unchallenged and supreme,
The one, the only, the triumphant Bill!
The winds his fame to hill and valley scream ;
And curving waves with admiration spill
Applause upon the shore. His laurels glow,
Fresh washed and green with all a party’s tears,
The brighter for the loud emphatic No
That roared defeat about his trembling ears.
Tremendously ho talked; and still the sound
Of variegated language sweeps and sways
The large astonished landscape round and round
Through all the tangles of the public maze.
On and still on! Hear him excuse, explain.
The mouthpiece of the party and its jaw,
He fills the arid wastes with wordy rain
Till rival mouths are closed in silent awe.
What of that pledge he gave at Bendigo?
Shall mere words hold him from high leadership?
Shall one hot breath of language lay him low
And dash the cup of office he would sip
From his hard-gripping fingers? Never yet
Did such a barrier stay him from his goal!
Sorrow remembers, wisdom should forget;
A dead pledge must not thwart a living soul.
Alone he stands unchallenged and elate!
What need that statesman skill be here employed
Against the pale, place-hungry souls that wait,
By their late referendum triumph buoyed
To some high hope of loaves and little fishes!
Out on the traitors ! Each pledge we recall!
Shall mere dull truth permit the office dishes
Full-charged with pickings to such mouths to fall?
N.S.W.

COMSTOCK’S “DEAD-SHOT” WORM
PELLETS.
A SAFE, SURE, AND RELIABLE SPECIFIC FOR
THE ERADICATION OF WORMS IN CHILDREN
AND ADULTS.
The general symptoms of worms are a
tendency to pick the nose and a sensation of
itching in the anus. Other symptoms are
nervous affections, such as fretfulness, irri-
tation of temper, wakefulness or disposition
to excessive sleep, disturbed sleep, and grind-
ing the teeth, sudden starting out of sleep as
if from fright, dizziness, spasmodic move-
ments of the eyelids, dilated pupils, per-
verted vision, temporary blindness and deaf-
ness. General convulsions are not infre-
quent in children, and symptoms resembling
water in the head have been ascribed to
worms, and have ceased on their expulsion.
The long round or stomach worm' is the
commonest kind of worm from which man-
kind suffers, and is of a light, transparent
color, resembling the ordinary ground worm,
except that the extremities are more sharply
pointed than the ground worm.
Young people are more subject to these
worms than middle-aged or elderly people.
They live in the bowels, and sometimes make
their way into the stomach and throat, caus-
ing sickness, vomiting and choking. Gener-
ally one or two would be the most a person
would be afflicted with, but sometimes a
much larger number is met with. The length
varies from four to eight inches, but young
ones an inch to two inches long are also
found. The first thing to do is to rid the
system of the worms, and Comstock’s “Dead
Shot” Worm Pellets, taken according to
directions, are an effective remedy for the
thorough eradication of the long round or
stomach worm—the common enemy of the
child, the youth, the middle-aged and aged.
A light, nourishing diet, out-of-door exercise,
and. if possible, a change of air, will greatly
improve the general health.
Threadworms or pinworms are of very
common occurrence in children. They are
very small, from one-sixth of an inch to half-
an-inch in length, and resemble cotton
thread—they are sometimes called seat
worms, and reside in the lower bowels. The
chief symptom of this worm is severe local
itching, restlessness and irritability, and a
frequent desire to urinate, picking at the
nose; loss of appetite and anaemia are also
observed. To get rid of the worms and their
eggs their nests also must be removed, and
that requires a specific remedy. Comstock’s
“Dead Shot” Worm Pellets, taken according
to directions, on an empty stomach, followed
by a dose of opening medicine, will succeed
where others fail.
Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets
are packed with full directions, and
are for sale by chemists and stores
generally at 3s. 6d. per packet, or six
packets for 205., and if unable to obtain
locally will be mailed post free on receipt of
price. Prepared only by The W. H. Comstock
Co., Ltd., 23 Lang Street, Sydney, N.S.W.;
Parish Street, Wellington, N.Z.

BITRO-PHOSPHATE FOR
WEAK NERVES.
FRENCH SPECIALISTS CLAIM THIS
ORGANIC PHOSPHATE IS ACTUALLY
CONVERTED INTO LIVING NERVE
TISSUE.
The rapid increase in the use by physicians
and nerve specialists of the organic nerve-
building and easily assimilated phosphate
known among druggists as bitro-phosphate,
is due almost entirely to the remarkably
favorable results obtained by specialists of
world-wide fame and reputation. It is there-
fore only logical that doctors throughout
the country should be urged wherever possi-

narcotic or stimulant habit-forming drugs or
alcoholic preparations in the treatment of
nervousness, neurasthenia, sleeplessness,
nervous debility, lack of energy, mental
depression, etc.
Being a food which, according to Rohm
and other French authorities, is actually
assimilated and converted into living nerve
tissue, bitro-phosphate, which is readily
obtainable of any chemist in the form of
5-grain tablets, is strongly recommended for
the use, not only of those who suffer as in-
dicated above, but also to those who wish to
increase their nerve strength, vim, vigor,
vitality and powers of endurance. It is
especially valuable to aviators, motorists,
gunners and men in the various branches of
the' army and navy, where strong, steady
nerves are of vital importance. Cut out

meal and note how quickly your entire being

SPORTING
NOTIONS
“Mujik”: Just so long as tlie totalisator
is contemplated as ah instrument for yielding
revenue to the State will it be looked at the wrong
way and muddle will prevail in its working. It the
machine is merely this there is little to be said tor
it, since the same results could be obtained by the
State dipping' into Mo’s bag. There is no moral or
practical difference between a State revenue of, sav,
£lOO,OOO from duty stamps on betting tickets and
the same amount obtained by way of a tax on the
turnover of the machine. When people saj 1 the
machine is a failure on N.S.W. courses, do they mean
that if twice or three times the money went through
it would be a “success” simply because the State
revenue would be increased? The truth is that the
“State revenue” aspect of this matter has been given
absurd importance, and that the only argument by
which the totalisator can be justified has lu-en obscured.
That argument is that the machine is a rational
betting-system and a reasonable substitute for a
crowd of idlers who by hanging on the turf derive
a parasitical living from the public. If this conten-
tion is wrong then the machine is an excrescence. If
it is right, then Mo should receive the boot. To
maintain both is merely to drain , the public more
than before and make farce of the proceedings
whether the totalisator investments are large or
small. *
“Quill”: That three-dividend system on
the N. S. Wales totalisator is right enough in a way.
It is also a certain method of not getting the custom
of the man who is out to win. And on any race-
course in N. S. Wales this is the man with the money
that talks. When the Tote Bill was before the
Legislature I suggested that the right lines to follow
were these. Give Ike notice to quit. When he had
gone, and not before, be ready with a double-
barrelled tote system, one machine paying on winners
only, the other on placed horses, a single dividend
being calculated on the three. To suppose that men
who really bet are goinc to nut a heavy dollop on a
horse and get a cut from only 50 per -cent, of the
totalisator investment is absurd. They simply won t
do it. A winner tote with £1 tickets and a place
tote at 10s. would fill the bill and the hole caused
by the evaporation of McShortodds. The cheapen-
ing of the thing, combined with the maintenance of
the ring, looks like a crude dodge to miss the bull’s-
eye.
* * * *
Someone or other seems anxious that the
pony-race promoter shall not go short in wartime.
Queensland Turf Club received a telegram from the
Defence Department the other day that the Minister
had granted John Wren three extra days racing
between then and March 2 at Albion Park, and direct-
ing the Club to fix the dates. Of course the Club
promptlv made a protest against the policy which
curtailed its own racing days and allowed Wren
more days. And there the matter stood last week.
It is time this libera] treatment of Wren was ex-
plained by the Defence Department. Otherwise
people will begin to explain it for themselves.
“MacPhraiser” : Who says horse-racing is
not being restricted ? It is true that down about the
city they only have a couple of meetings a week and
somewhere about 14 events on the programme. But
at Sunny Corner, where they have race-meetings as
often as’ once every year and the prize list runs up
to as much as a tenner, not including the bridle
for the opening handicap, they had to abandon their
annual meeting the other day because the stern
Win-the-W'ar Department refused permission for the
meeting to Tie held.
* * * *
There was more curtailment of sport in
Sydney last week. Rain fell in large lumps from
Thursday to Saturday, with the result that, unless
thev ran the Flying Handicap in boats and fitted the
remaining nags with sails, it was. no good thinking
about giving the thoroughbred a bit more encomage-
ment. As these schemes were considered impractic-
able the two Sydney race meetings arranged tor
Saturday were postponed to the current week.
One of the big six-furlongs events of the
vear will be run at Randwick -on Saturday—the
A J.C. Challenge Stakes. Anniversary Handicap the
following week. Epsom Handicap winner Panacre,
who was up in the front line for three parts of the
journey in the Villiers Mile on December 22, came
into the betting with a run last week, and was a
dead-heat with Poitrina when the odds were men-
tioned. An apparently open race, but La Steppe and
Panacre may be thereabouts. Old Ulva’s Isle lias
been most talked about for the Anniversary, mainly
because he has seemed to be growing younger lately
on the track. Perhaps something with a Jess pro-
longed past may be noticed first. If so, it may he
the improving Kennaquhair.
* * *
Phil Glenister, who. has a first-rate claim
to the title of oldest active N.S.W. sportsman, passed
the 80th milestone the other day. He was one of the
participants in the novel wager of 1000 to eight
drinks which was made by bookie W. Slack against
Crovdon and Nimblefoot winning the Metrop. and
Melbourne Cup double of 1870. The double came
off; and although W. Craig, who took the bet, died
before the Cup was run, whereby the wager was made
null, Slack paid £5OO to the family. The eight
drinks were also consumed, and Glenister is the only
one left of those who shared them. Phil Glenister
has for 51 years been a member of Tattersall’s Club
(Sydney) ; and in the boxing boom days he was
stake-holder in many of the big fights.

The £3150 given for Thana is not the
highest figure a buyer ha % gone when he wanted a
mare. Golden Slipper cost half as much again and
was worth a lot less. But it shows that there is
a plucky man on the premises. Of course lhana
mar yet win a race or two and go to the stud with
a bit in hand, so to speak. If she doesn t then
she will be an even bigger investment, tor oats
and training cost money. The interesting point
about her will be whether she does any good in the
paddock. The mare particularly successful on the
turf is seldom a success in the other place. in
nearly every case she leaves her vitality benind,
though the success of nags bred from the progeny o
such a mare, seem to show that there is a screw
loose somewhere in this theory. American expel 1-
ence is said to show that when a mare taken from
a successful racing career is spelled and well fee
for a year before the nuptials, the output can bt
expected to do better than if she had been rushed
into tile business of maternity. A buyer with
enough cash to pay over £ 3000 for a mare ought
to have enough to try this scheme m Australia.

“Kola”: When I saw Comedy King
knocked down to Norman Falkiner, of Pranjip T’ark,
Murchison (Vic.), last week, it recalled to me Aiarat
of 60 years ago. The diggings were then in full
sw j n o\ We had a farm down near Mortlake, and I
used” to drive the team up with oats and wheat.
Old Frank Falkiner kept a produce store then at the
Pond, a few miles south of Ararat, in the heart of
the diggings. It was called a “produce store, but
vou could buy almost anything there. I bought
sugar, tea and tobacco. Falkiner was. a shrewd old
chap, but popular talk made his missus the chief
wheel in the machine. They kept the store till the
diggings petered right out. Then, ln the late
’sixties, thev sold out, and went up to N.S.W. to
buy a station. And now Norman gives i 300 guineas
for a nag! * * *

being taken too seriously. It s not a couple of years

6000 guineas 'for a champion like Tressady, anrt

Another feather in the hat of the Dunlop
people is beginning to make that article resemble a
Red Indian head-dress. Recently Boyd Edkifts drove
a Dunloped ear from Brisbane to Sydney in 26 hours
S minutes, which completed a trio ot records held b.v
Ihe Australian tyre for the stretches between capitals
pom Adelaide to Brisbane. The previous runs from
Melbourne to Sydney and from Melbourne to Adel
aide by Edkins and Murray Aunger respectively beat
each railway express for the distance. The
miles from ’ Adelaide to Brisbane have now been
motored in 57 hours 52 minutes. #
“Dormant”: Desert Gold could nearly
win at Buninyong (Vic.), anyway. They startei
from the five furlongs the other day, and the ambu-
lance hopped off with them. When the van arrived
at the post the stewards directed the driver to go
back towards the field, which was still staggering
along in the direction of the judge. The sending ot
the ambulance back showed great foresight, because
one of the nags sat down and the medical corps had
to bring the rider home. The recumbent moke then
sprang up and dashed off towards the skyline at top
pace, and might have escaped if a soldier who had
returned with only one leg had not retained his
presence of mind. After a chase he seized the moke
by the tail, and hopping on top ran the winner to a
head. Meanwhile the damaged jockey had been
operated on at the hospital and discharged, and was
waiting on crutches for the to finish.

* * *
An Adelaide S.M. has astonished the
police bv asking* why they did not arrest the betting
clients of an arrested bookmaker. They had not
thought of it, it seems ; yet the amateurs are break-
ing the S. Aus. law just as much as the professional
is. But the whole law as to betting is a solemn
farce, just as every law must be that is passed to
please one section of the community, and does not
reflect the feelings of the people as a whole.
* * * *
“Carvex”: By the death of J. D. Ormond,
of Hawkes Bay (M.L.), the last of an old-time
bunch of racing men who flourished thereabouts went
to where the horse doesn’t matter. Ormond, Russell,
Douglas and Donnelly were for. a long time the best-
known names on the turf in that part of the
Shivery Isles, and all have gone west. Spencer
Gollan, whose horses raced there with more than
average success a quarter of a century ago, vanished
with ”his cash and mokes to England, and is still
winning an occasional event. These men, with
Stead, of Chchurch, and Geordie McLean, of Dunedin,
did a lot of other things besides own racehorses —
Gollan excepted, perhaps, and even he played golf—
but it was through owning horses that most people
knew anything about them. The Stead family is the
only one in which the old man’s passion survives to
any great extent among the young ’uns.
When Desert Gold races in Australia it
will be oniy in w.t.a. events. T. 11. Lowry, the
owner, offered his wife the net profits for the
benefit of patriotic funds in which she was interested.
The lady compromised and decided upon the endow-
ment of six beds in the Soldiers’ Hospital at Rotorua
and six in the Soldiers’ .Hospital to be built at
Napier. When not required for soldiers they are to
be available for any trainers and jockeys that may
be unfortunate enough to need them.
Desert Gold’s record : 08 starts, 29 firsts,
six seconds, three thirds. As a three-year-old she
won the 14 races she started in. Value of stakes
won, £16,890. Carlita won £17,525.
Syd Godfrey and Vince Blackburn have
had further controversy about the featherweight
title at Sydney Stadium, and during most of the
time Godfrey had his championship trembling in
the balance. At the end of the proceedings Arthur
Scott had arrived at a decision to remain neutral.
The draw, of course, left Godfrey with his title and
filled Blackburn with a great yearning to move for a
new trial. It was a good, hard scrap, with God-
frey [smiting the harder and missing the most fre-
quently. In the seventh a smack on the ear made
Blackburn wish it hadn’t happened, but he stood
the gaff well and was soon fighting back as strongly
as before.

“ Jwosp”: Taken off in France, George C.
Wilson, googlie bowler, of Canterbury (M.L.). Wil-
son was overshadowed by Sandman, now at the
Front, but got his chance when Sandman was with
the Maorilanders in Australia in 1913. He had a big
hand in keeping the Plunket bauble on the Plains,
not only with the ball, but al-o as a last-wicket
performer with 11. W. Monaghan, another bowler.
The pair had a habit of putting on 50 whenever they
got together at the tail. Eight wickets for 23 was
Wilson’s best in an inter-provincial match.

“Ranger”: Bombardier Stan Pearse, a
well-known Hawthorn (Vie.) lad, recently killed on
the West Front, was a proper sample of good Aus-
tralian. Small but perfectly proportioned, he was a
pocket Hercules among his ‘ athletic pals. He always
took great pride in keeping himself fit; and from
the day that he helped to drag the first two guns of
the 4th Battery up the cliffs at Gallipoli till he fell
shattered by an enemy shell on October 4 last, he
never, called on the A.M.C. ; and in those three
long strenuous years no cobber heard from him a
word of complaint.
R. D. Hodgins, who is attracting atten-
tion at the Sydney tennis carnival, is a promising
all-rounder. Among other things he and L. S.
Aitkeh defeated chamuion Horace Rice and L. O. S.
PoideVin in straight sets in the men’s doubles
(6 —2, 6—-4) ; and in the singles he outed the wily
G. P. Barbour (6—2, 2—6. 6—2). Hodgins re-
cently captained Sydney Grammar when the School
won the Great Public Schools cricket premiership
for the 18th time in 25 years. And at Rugby the
young man is a brilliant five-eighth.
There lias been only one hurricane inci-
dent in Sydney cricket, this season—W. S. McCloy’s
118 against North Sydney, of which 92 came from
boundary strokes. McGloy played, with Paddington
in Rawson Cup matches years ago. He keeps his
smite in order during winter playing baseball, and
is one of the finest of fields in the pastime which
sends all Yankeeland barmy.

Veteran sailing-man George Holmes, now
living at Cudal (N.S.W.). has a notion of competing
with his old boat Donnelly for the State champion-
shin next month. It is half a century since the
old-timer sailed his first race on Sydney Harbor, and
he reckons he hasn’t forgotten the tricks that made
him a champion.

from Toowoomba (Q.) to swing a racquet at Sydney
tennis carnival, has been heard of before in N.S.W.
sport. ’Way back in 1884 he played cricket with the
Juniors against the Intercolonials at Sydney, hater
he was in the ’Varsity team, and captained Bur-
wood to victory in the' premiership of 1904. Eric
Barbour (in khaki somewhere on the other side), who
smote 365 when Svdney Grammar put up the school s
record of 916 in 1909, is a son. In serious moments
the dad is headmaster of Toowoomba Grammar
School. » »
“Tam o’ Shanter”: The gun of the Vic-
torian coekv that slaughtered 68 sparrows in one
act (B. 10/1/T8) must have kicked a bit. Under
extreme provocation from the spadger pest we once
invited them all to breakfast on wheat and bran. A
muzzle-loader was then attended _to on the Sunday-
school “full-and-overflowing” principle. When all
the guests had arrived our star marksman took bear-
ings and let bang both barrels simultaneously. After
he recovered from what happened we could only
gather 114 sparrows, hut there were some pieces that
might have made up another dozen or so. That was
the year the astronomers said there were spots on
the sun.
* * *
“H.V.E.” : There is no livelier fish than
mullet, when “on the feed.” . I’ve taken over two
dozen in about an hour on the mud-flats of a tidal
lake, or estuary, on the South Coast (N.S.W.). As
to bait, give me the soft parts of cungevoi for choice,
but many others will do, such as small bits of prawn,
crab, mullet viscera and a diminutive red worm
found in the sand of some beaches, but more often
about the mouths of creeks debouching directly into
the sea. This last lure mullet simply can’t resist.
I’ve even, when hard pushed, started operations with
flesh from the breast of a defunct and smellful
mutton-bird (short-tailed petrel) picked up on the
beach, and scored fairly well. It is quite true,
however, that full-grown sea mullet, which run to
about 18in. in length, rarely if ever take a bait;
but juniors up to about a foot may be coaxed sport-
ively into the frying-pan. Mullet contain a good
deal of soft, white fat, and prill nicely on the
coals without any preliminary basting. They should
be split down the back, so as to cook evenly right
through. The fish will thrive in perfectly fresh
water. Most South Coast ponds and lagoons contain
full-grown sea-mullet, but the rarer Eastern perch
(Australian bass) has disappeared from many of
them. These waters harbor enormous brown eels,
which undoubtedly prey on their fellow boarders.
“Wattlebark": Bruthen (Vic.) people
claim that the Tambo River is the finest place for
bream in Australia. Anyway, it s good enough.
Someone took a census one Sunday of the bream
caught within half a mile on each side of the bridge
over the river and worked the number out at 3000.
Two fishermen alone accounted for about 250. Down
at the Bemm River, away out in East Gippsland,
they reckon the catch, largely made up of bream, by
the ton; but then that’s net-fishing, so it doesn t
compare with the Tambo, where it is all line-work.
During the six months that the Bemm is open to
netting two waggons make the 37-mile trip to
twice a week, each carrying 30cwt. of fish, mostly
bream, will! a sprinkling of native salmon, luderick,
mullet and odds and ends.
“Ranger’': When “Geo. L. Hobson'’ (B.
27/12/’l7) says “once a target puncher gets off the
grass-mound and has to shorn without his sling he is
at sea,” he is all wrong. I know, because 1 was
reared in XE. Victoria. There the schoolboys
learned the rudiments of shooting at the target, and
then spent Sundays and wet days in the bush
strafing the marsupial population. No lad was
allowed to use “the big rifle” alone in the bush
until he could plant a reasonable average of shots
upon the “bull” at 500 yards. In the opinion of those
good experts, target-shooting is the way to learn
to shoot. The man 'Who is a mug at the target is
never, in my experience, a good field shot; but the
lead-slinger who can find the “bull” with regularity
needs little field practice to become an expert there.
Now about the cost to the Government, as to which
“Rio” worries (B. 22/11/’l7). I cannot say for
certain what they do now, but during my 10 years
experience of a club we paid all expenses ourselves,
except that the Defence Department issued 150
rounds of cartridges a year to each qualified marks-
man. 1 judge that each of the chaps used at least
1000 rounds a year, 850 of which he paid for.
Likewise he bought. his own rifle, oil, wind-gauge,
etc., paid travelling expenses to matches, , paid
markers’ fees, and most of the repairs on the butts
were done by the members in their spare time. Our
men used to pay exes, out of fox and wallaby
skins, which according to “Geo. L. Hobson” we
should not have been able to hit.
N.S.W. Education Department’s estimate
that 80 per cent, of the girls and 70 per cent, of
the boys learned to keep afloat during the vacation
swimming classes sets a main for other State Educa-
tion Departments. The instructors handled over 5000
children, and are optimistically looking forward to
7000 next time. The Department might go farther
and make the scheme more practical by including
life-saving classes. Instruction in “what to do ’ and
“how f to do it” would finish the job off properly.

F. AY. Young is the first S. Australian
Agent-General to get knighted during his
term of office.
Professor Barry is agitating in Melbourne
for compulsory domestic training for girls.
He says most young wives ruin the tempers
of their babies by their ignorance of baby-
craft. The Bulletin is thoroughly in
accord with this new peace movement.
Westralia, v/hic’h has kept itself free from
the imported sparrow pest, notes with alarm
that the bird is steadily making its way
west along the new railway line. That
shows how wise the old Groper was. In
1840 South Aus., then less than four years
old, wanted to make communication by land
with its western neighbor, and the latter
showed “fierce opposition,” chiefly on the
ground that a road would enable bush-
rangers from the east to make “raids” upon
it!
“C. Ockney”: Foster Fraser has turned
aside from his guide-books and perpetrated a
play. Like the immortal baby born to Jack
Easy’s wetnurse, it is only a little one, but
proves an acceptable war-time item. It is
called “The Grousers” and tells of a typical
Cockney couple who are continually grum-
bling over war conditions, and a tram-con-
ductress’s daughter who is instrumental in
bringing them to a less grousey frame of
mind. The novelty is being played in the
Stoll vaudeville houses.
Barring political disputes, the life of a
poll-clerk would seem to be as free from
risk as any. But there is no dodging luck.
At the little polling-station of Avon, near
Balaklava (S.A.), a brown snake appeared
from under the floor of the room while the
Referendum voting was going on. Arthur
Reid, one of the officials, •'went for it with a
broom, which broke when it hit the floor,
and by a piece of incredibly rotten luck a
piece flew up into his right eye, which ulti-
mately had to be removed at the Adelaide
Hospital.
Edmund Jowett, the liard-faccd old gent,
who climbed into the House of Reps, over
his pile of wool-bales, is doing the handsome
thing where it will do most good. He has
passed over -a large cheque to the Argus for
distribution, and- gets his name in the paper
for these amounts, among others: 100
guineas to the Sunday School Union prize-
fund, £5O for the Trinity College chapel
organ fund, a fiver for the North Melbourne
Congregational Sabbath-school, and three-
pun-ten for the Methodist Mission at Fitz-
roy. Every little helps; ,and no doubt
Jowett reckons he will need it all at the next
elections, since he hasn’t AVon the AA T ar yet.
Even the aproned bloke who pares the
. gun-horses’ hoofs hasn’t allowed himself to
be left out of it. Charles Curtayne, of Chew-
ton (Vic.), has won the Military Medal.
He is a shoeing-smitli, and is the first Aus-
tralian on this job to get it. He took time
off from his farrier-sergeant’s job last Octo-
ber to go out with the boys on a succession
of risky raids, and his fighting style brought
him ilito notice early and often.
From' Illustrated London News, Aug.
21, 1552 : —-
The Postmaster-General has entered into a con-
tract with the pi opi ietors of the screw-steamer Great
Britain for the conveyance of mails to Melbourne
and Sydney in Australia. The Great Britain will
leave Liverpool on the 21st inst., and will probably
reach Melbourne about 50 days afterwards. This is
the first instance of the Postmaster-General entering
into an ...extra mail contract; but the size and speed
of the Great Britain, together with the great immi-
gration movement now in progress, fully justifies the
unusual proceeding. The postage of letters by this
packet will be one shilling each and newspapers will
be sent free. All letters for 1 Melbourne and Sydney
posted up to the 21st will be sent by her, unless
specially addressed to be otherwise sent.
The Great Britain on that voyage was com-
manded by Capt. Mathews, the chief officer
being John Gray (afterwards skipper, and
mysteriously lost overboard). Among the
passengers was Reginald Bright, a cadet of
the house owning the ship. He is still atop.
The Great Britain is now a hulk at Port
Stanley, Falkland Isles.
“Binnacle”: There is at present in an
Australian port, which I mustn’t mention, a
nueer-lookip" craft Hat was at one time
reckoned a floating palace, the City of Syd-
mj. She was biu.i in 1875 by the Facific
Mail Co., and after being for some years pen-
sioned off was rudely awakened lately from
her slumbers in Rotten Row. her innards
gutted out and a line of six masts stuck into
her. One of the newest things in war-time
sailing ships, she has started again to earn
dividends. Incidentally, the “fast and
favorite” Gabo of the ’eighties has been sold
“to the East,” and the one-time popular
coaster is to go off the Australian register.
She did more than earn her bunker coal for
Howard-Smith during the last 37 years, and
now she has brought a price that a couple
of years ago would have sounded incredible.
The old steamer is figuring in a queer case
of alleged fraud in the Sydney law courts.
The defendants are- stated to have separated
some prominent city men from considerable
cash on their say-so that they owned the
boat. One would think it as easy to raise a
mortgage on the G.P.O.
About a wreck and a banquet: —
Died Christmas week at Mount Victoria (N.S.AV.),
in her 79th year, Johanna Bentley, widow of John,
who predeceased her 35 years. The couple were the
chief figures in a romantic story which goes back to
1855, when Johanna arrived, a fine lass of 16, from
Ireland, in a ship which was wrecked off Kangaroo
Island (S.A.). When the survivors sorted themselves
out, the colleen found herself in the arms of her
future husband, a stalwart trooper in the S.A.
Mounted Police, who took part in the rescue. After
marriage the pair went to Sydne.y, and after many
years’ service in the mounted police John died at
Hartley, leaving a family* 0 f 10. Their con-
nection with the S. Australian family was
recalled by the death of the last of John Bent-
ley’s brothers, Thomas Friend Bentley, a bachelor,
intestate. lie was an eccentric who lived in a state
of siege at the Burra (S.A.), and had saved a tidy
fortune of five figures. On the news of his* death
38 nephews and nieces sprang forward to claim his
wealth. The Blue Mountains family naturally put
in for their share, but some of the S.A. branch
opposed this addition to the list of claimants. The
John Bentleys promptly employed a firm of lawyers
to look after their interests, and as a result the
Supreme Court of S. Aus. has ordered that John’s
children are to be invited to the feast.

Do you live in the home
.A SILENT PIANO?
THERE it stands, in lonely gloom close by die wall,
an expensive tombstone on the grave of vanished
joy. You personally never could play very much,
and lack of practice has still further stiffened your
fingers. The children are only in the five-finger exercise
' stage and other interests have impaired your wife’s
never-to-be-much-talked-about technique.
And so your“silent”Piano stands
deteriorating through lack of
use. Why not alter it all ? Why
not take advantage of the liberal
allowance we make on “silent”
Pianos and enjoy all the pleasure
which a Beale Player - Piano
affords. It is an instrument which
supplies faultless technique with a
rich, resonant tone. Eight ex-
pression devices give absolute
tone control, enabling one to get
the fullest wealth of colour and
feeling out of every selection
played. A knowledge of music is
quite unnecessary. All that is
required is an intelligent appre-
ciation of rhythm, accent and
expression. Informal concerts are
held daily.
Call and let us demonstrate how easy it is for you to
play all your favourite music the “Beale" Player-way.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

Once when an Angel was walking
* Down a quiet country lane,
She came across a small boy,
Crying as if in pain.
The Angel softly bent and asked him,
“Tell me, child, do you cry?”
The boy lifted his tear-stained face,
And this was his reply:
“I went to church last Sunday,
Where they tell you the way to Heaven.
And the preacher said, ‘Sorrow for your sins,
If you wish them to be forgiven.’
“I know' they must be cleverer than I.
So here behold I sorrow.
So though to-day I weep and pray,
I’ll be forgiven and happy to-morrow.

“Ambulance”: Many Australian nurses
have already won the Royal Red Cross in
this war. No medal is worn with greater
pride, for the traditions of the profession
are high, and the army nurse who. gains
it must have proved herself a capable leader
over and over again. The pity of it is that
when the strain of campaigning has weak-
ened the nurse’s health and shortened her
professional career, there is no pension for
her. As the effects of war often only show'
themselves years afterwards, an army nurse
with a . good record should be eligible to
apply for an adequate military pension any
time after the completion of service. As
for the R.R.C.-winner, high military honors
won at the cost of health are liable to
embitter the struggling possessor in after
years; the decoration then becomes merely
the symbol of a nation’s empty thanks.
“Scray” : I think I met a coming Prime
Minister at court the other morning. The
case was one in which a push had dealt with
an enemy by dropping a 9 x 12 bluestone
channel-pitcher upon his head after first fell-
ing him with a beer-bottle (full). The de-
fence was an alibi, supported by ground and
lofty perjury. “I never touched ’irn,” said,
the accused; “why, I don’t even know the
man!” The instructing John Hop whis-
pered the prosecuting inspector. “Isn’t it
a fact,” asked the latter, “that you and he
are mates—you go about together?” “We
ain’t,” protested the injured innocent in the
box. “I don’t know ’im, I tell y’!” “What
do you mean, sir, by such a statement?”
interposed the Chairman of the Bench sternly
(he keeps a shop opposite the pub head-
quarters of the push). “Why, I’ve seen you
together! How dare you say ybu don’t
know the man?” “W T ell, I don’t,” persisted
the poor persecuted one. “I orfen seen him, o’
course, but I never had a drink with ’im in
me life.”

on Neolin Soles
United States Government Contract Specifies Neolin
The first big U.S. Government contract
specifying Neolin Soles was awarded to a
large New England manufacturer.
This unqualified recognition and endorse-
ment of Neolin came after rigid competitive
tests under the most exacting demands of
service.
Numerous manufacturers tendered when the
U.S. Government officials were considering
a scientific substance to supplant leather soles.
But Neolin best withstood the rigorous tests
which had to be passed. So, in this the
first contest to decide a ‘better than leather’
soles, Neolin won out.
Just as Neolin proved itself the ‘better than leather’
sole for the footwear of U.S.A. fighting men, so it
will prove itself for you if you will give it the
opportunity.
-- - s
For Neolin soles, by giving better, longer, more
comfortable wear, can save for you the first day
you wear them.
Get Neolin soles and comfort your feet with the
spring and buoyancy which they cannot help but
impart.
Neolin’s appearance can be imitated, but its qualities are the
result of methods and materials known only to Goodyear.
Now, there are other soles that look like Neolin, but there is
only one Neolin and every pair of Neolin soles is branded
Neolin on the instep, and every pair of half soles bears
the word Neolin which is your absolute guarantee of
satisfaction. The shop from which you buy your footwear
can supply boots or shoes with Neolin soles ; if they can’t,
advise the nearest Goodyear branch or agency.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION
WITHOUT ROUGE OR POWDER.
Many women—both young and
suffer greatly from sallow complexions.
Sometimes this is the result of climatical
conditions, sometimes the cause is constitu-
tional ; but whatever it be, it is decidedly
unnatural and unpleasant. Bathing in milk
occasionally is said to be most helpful and
soothing to the skin, but just try the follow-
ing recipe, which any chemist will make up
for you: Two oz. of rose-water, 2oz. flowers
of oxzoin and one dram tincture of benzoin.
Apply this night and morning with a sponge
after washing, and let it dry on, then dust
the skin lightly with a soft cloth. This
home-made lotion is one of the best, and
supersedes rouge or powder, leaving the skin
heautifuflv soft, clear and smooth. —Advt.

AT POVERTY POINT.
No manager has deserved his luck more
than Asche. Eight years ago, when he
came to Australia, he gambled on his chance
of getting good support if he did the thing
handsomely. He spent his last shilling,
so to speak, on equipment. Many hundreds
of pounds w r ere devoted to the outfit of Lily
Brayton alone. Nothing short of big suc-
cesses for “Taming of the Shrew,”
“Othello,” “Count Hannibal,” etc., would
have built up another bank account for
Oscar. With the money he made in Aus-
tralia he took another risk when he pro-
duced “Kismet” in London. There was no
encouraging precedent for this venture. The
play was in a class by itself, and it had
to be a costly production. “Kismet” was
such a great go that Asche practically gave
up a certainty for an uncertainty when he
stopped the run in order to fulfil his con-
tract to return to Australia. The unusual
play gave him another boom on this side,
and then he kept his managerial reputation
boiling with a great, original production of
“Antony and Cleopatra.” There never was
anything meanly commercial about Oscar
A.sche. He never played for safety. To
think of doing “Chu Chin Chow” at all
was to think of doing it extravagantly, and
that’s how Asche did it.

panto, is said to have been made in Aus-
“Aladdin,” at Mel-
bourne King’s.

tralia, and only the
hypercritical would
deny that the re-

for anything. The

and smart and

has arrived at a

and opulence when
it can put up a
very imposing

body or anything.
“75”: Frank Crossley, who used to sup-
ply comic relief in Anderson and Marlow
dramas, is now in France, and has made
himself popular with brother Billjims by
producing special versions of “Dick Whit-
tington,” “The New Barmaid” and “Fun
on the Bristol” for their private consump-
tion. The authorities have provided Frank
with a theatre, scenery and a company, and
lie was lately doing two shows daily. Gen-
eral Birdwood and the Wales princeling
dropped in to see “Whittington,” in which
Crossley played the Dame.
Richard Bellairs, who for some years has
figured (mostly as the villain) in dramas
under Bilanderson and Bailey-Grant man-
agement, is swapping the slapstick for the
yard-wand or some such weapon. He is
joining a Flinders-lane warehouse that deals
in woollens. The stage won’t lose a great
actor, but the profesh will miss a fine fellow,
wholly free from the airs of the average
mummer. Bellairs pushes a neat pen, too —
his name has been at the foot of a good
deal of Bulletin verse and prose.
Robert Parker, the operatic baritone who
was one of the court cards in Quinlan’s
pack in Australia, is now with Sir Thomas
Beecham in London. He was leading voice
recently in an elaborate Drury Lane produc-
tion of Rimsky-Ivorsakov’s Russian opera,
“Ivan the Terrible,” and successfully sang
the tremendous name part.

David Souter’s ideas on “Have Australian
theatrical managers given Australian authors
a fair show?” (B. 10/1/TS)
J. C. Williamson asked me once “How can they?”
Dates are booked at least a year ahead for shows
which have already achieved success. They arrive
“ready to wear” —scenery, costumes, business, props
and performers. To produce a musical play from
script and score runs into some thousands of
pounds. Why should the Australian manager gamble
on an unknown horse when there is a tried per-
former ready to run? For one thing, he would have
to raise his prices of admission considerably to
cover the loss on plays which didn’t meet their
printing bill. The opportunity for the Australian
author seems to be in either amateur production,
which is quite rotten as a rule, or getting some
friends with enough faith in his ability to spar up
a few hundreds and give him a show. “The Gray
Kimono” got a two-nights’ run at Sydney Criterion
this way. “Mrs. Pretty and the Premier” was heard
the other way, and Arthur Adams got it staged in
England on the strength of its Australian notices.
“The Rajah of Shivapore” knew enough of Alfred
Hill’s friends and mine to pay the Maternity Hospi-
tal exes. The baby is now walking, and if it
doesn’t fall down and break its neck in the next
week or two it may develop into a champion.
“8.J.” : If you took a vote in the profesh
regarding the fatness or otherwise of Hamlet
I would have no doubt of the result. It is
considered the fattest part in drama.
Dorothy Brunton has made her N’Yark
debut in “Follow the Girl,” a new musical
show.
“A.M. J.” : The curtain dropped suddenly
in London on Fred Billington, of operatic
fame. He was a Yorkshireman and made
his first appearance with D’Oyly Carte in
1579, and was without interruption a mem-
ber of the famous combination till the end
of his long career. C. H. Workman, of the
J.C.W. Firm, played Ko Ko to Billington’s
Pooh Bah in the “Mikado” several hundred
times. The big man had been Lord High
Everything-else more than 3600 times. His
favorite part was Wilfrid Shadbolt in “The
Yeomen of the Guard”; but the one he
played oftenest was that of benefactor to
struggling mummers. Death was due to
syncope.

“Masetto”: The remarks of Irma Caron
in a recent Bulletin on the lack of stage
deportment and so forth in our lady concert
artists applies in some cases to male per-
formers. Take the case of the young Mel-
bourne violinist, Cecil Parkes. The last
time I heard him play was at one of the
municipal concerts. He was recalled and
bowed his acknowledgments, said bow being
a lifting of the chin towards the ceiling, fol-
lowed by a violent ducking of the head so
that the back of his collar could be seen.
At these concerts encores are not allowed;
but the delighted audience brought back the
talented youth half a dozen times to repeat
the strange gymnastic feat.
The father of just-dead Horace Rogers,
doyen of Sydney reporters, was a soldier
in Tasmania who outshone all the offi-
cers in garrison theatricals. In his
professional career he took old-man parts
principally, but he could act anything.
Jefferson found Lord Dundreary in him for
the Sydney season. Rogers came to Mel-
bourne from the London Theatre, Launces-
ton, in ’45 with Coppin’s co. They opened
in “The Lady of Lyons,” with Rogers as
Damas. The next night they gave “The
School for Scandal,” with Coppin as Sir
Peter Teazle and Rogers as Sir Oliver
Surface. Later, however, Rogers established
himself as a matchless Sir Peter, and took
the part at the opening of Melbourne Royal
in ’55. Mathews, much later, had the high-
est opinion of him.
“S.”: There is no conviction in “The Bird
of Paradise,” there is no reality in Muriel
Starr’s supposedly semi-barbaric Hawaiian.
You sit out this kind of drama, watch the
gyrations of the characters, and are never
under the delusion that you ary- concerning
yourself with the real troubles of real people.
When the fairy-tale element is abandoned in
dealing with primitives like the Hawaiians,
there is one satisfaction left. The heroine
of “The Bird of Paradise” is merely an ordi-
nary enough white woman a little possessed.
She is not half as barbaric as Hedda Gabler,
for instance; and Muriel Starr’s island belle,
even in her most emotional moment, is not a
whit nearer the cave-girl than any bit of
skirt might be oh St. Kilda sands on a nice
night. One column-and-a-half story of the
South Seas by Louis Becke is worth the
whole three acts of “The Bird” and all the
actors.

Terry’s fortune was within £29 of Tree’s. A
few others: Wilson Barrett, £30.862; Edwin
Booth, £24,000; Irving, £20,527; George
Grossmitli, £19,628; Corney Grain, £18,950;
Fred Leslie, £16,113; Dan Leno, £10,994;
George Coppin, £10,232; George Rignold,
£11,000; William Rignold, £4196; Charles
Kean, £35.000; Ada Rehan, £35,000. and E.
S. Willard, the first Spider in “The Silver
King,” £14,650.

LADS AND LASSES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A well-loved voice from the prompt wing calls ;
A blare of brass and a roll of drums ;
A cheer from gallery, pit and stalls —-
Down to the footlights the g'irl-“boy” comes.
Her well-graced figure and face aglow
Recall names, still with a pow’r to thirl,
Of jolly “boys” of the long-ago—
But what was the name of last year's “girl” ?
Meredith Meredro, Florence Young,
Sybil Arundale, Vi Loraine—
Often the praise of these lads we’ve sung ;
Memory murmurs their praise again.
The Iris fair that we knew as “Lil,”
Ravensburg, Imeson—one could hurl
Names by the dozen of “boys” ; but, still,
What was the name of last year’s “girl” ?
Life is a panto., with jokes and joys;
Our minds hark back to the laughing past,
Bringing to memory names of boys,
For they were our friends, and friendships last.
The loves that thrilled us we now forget
(Whose was this garter? And whose these
curls?) ;
The boys of the past we remember yet—
But uhat were the names of our principal girls?
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.
THE VENGEANCE OF THE HEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We were a company dramatic
Whom rascal Fate had treated ill;
Our failures frequent and emphatic,
From Tooraloo to Pinlcer’s Mill,
Had left us reft of grub and money,
And also minus half our duds.
We robbed the wild bees of their honey,
And Maurice Meeghan of his spuds.
These things alone must serve for dinner
Before we played that night in Yam
The stirring drama, “Clive, the Sinner” ;
But where we sat beside the dam
There came a rooster and a pullet,
And Cuthbert stole his gun to find.
He slew the rooster with a bullet.
The pullet clamored while we dined.
For once our audience seemed contented.
The barn was full, and I insist
Old Cuthbert finely represented
The noble Earl, and no one hissed,
Till broke a yell of frantic laughter,
And after that our hope fell dead—
That pullet, perched upon a rafter,
Had dropped an egg on Cuthbert’s head!
VICTORIA.

In the Overland Big Four-cylinder touring
car and the Overland Light Four-cylinder
touring car, the luxurious and the practical
are in proper balance to produce lasting satis-
faction.
The Big Four is finished in a deep rich
brown, while the colour of the Light Four is
a beautiful shade of gray.
These two models exemplify the remark-
able values made possible by tremendous pro-
duction.
Size does not of itself constitute greatness.
An industry is as great as its service to
the public.
But size does usually constitute oppor-
tunity for service.
In the case of Willys-Overland, size origi-
nally was an indication of foresight and courage.
It constituted an opportunity to establish
new values in motor cars —this was a service.
Growth came as a reward of this service,
and with growth came opportunity for further
service—further revision of values.
Today’s immensity means experience,
facilities and resources, plus the public con-
fidence, which gives these things stability for
yet greater service.
The Willys-Overland ideal of service is in-
tegrity of value in the product throughout
the whole life of that product’s service.
The Willys-Overland Motor Cars which
we have to show you exemplify this ideal
and constitute the most comprehensive range
of models ever built by any one motor car
producer.

London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly Selections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing for the English Press.
Simple and Effective Methods.
By Olga Amsdell, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
The Killing of Superfluous Hair.
It is easy to remove superfluous hair tem-
porarily, but to remove it permanently is
quite another matter. Not many women
know that for this purpose such a simple
substance as powdered pheminol may be
used, applied directly to the hair. The recom-
mended treatment is designed not merely to
instantly remove the hair, but also to event-
ually kill the roots entirely. Almost any
chemist could supply an ounce of pheminol,
Ivliieh quantity should be sufficient.
Renewing Complexions by Absorption.
If your complexion is marred with sallow-
ness, moth patches, pimples or freckles, it is
useless to potter with powders and paints,
lotions, creams and things in an effort to get
rid of the trouble. Unless you have some
ability as an artist, you will mar your ap-
pearance still more. The new and rational
way is to take off the complexion itself, with
all its offensive marks. Just get some pure
mercolized wax at the chemist’s and use at
night the same as cold cream. Remove next
morning with water and soap, following with
dash of cold water. The mercolized wax
absorbs the half-dead scarf skin in flaky
particles, so gradually that no one guesses
you are treating your face—unless it be by
the result, which is truly wonderful. There
is nothing like it for bestowing a natural,
healthy and beautiful complexion.
A “Blackhead” Secret.
Blackheads, oily skins and enlarged pores
usually go together, but can be instantly
corrected by a unique new process. A tablet
of stymol, obtained from the chemist, is
dropped in a tumbler of hot water, which will
then of course “fizz” briskly. When the
effervescence has subsided the face is bathed
with the stymol-charged water and then dried
with a towel. The offending blackheads, of
their own accord, come right off on the
towel, the large oily pores immediately con-
tract and efface themselves naturally. There
is' no squeezing, forcing, or any drastic
action. The skin is left, uninjured, smooth,
soft and cool. A few such treatments should
be taken at intervals of three or four days
thereafter in order to insure the permanence
of the pleasing result so quickly obtained.
Don’t Have Grey Hair.
Grey hair is often a serious handicap to
both men and women while still., in the prime
of life. Hair dyes are not advisable because
they are always obvious, inconvenient and
often down-right injurious. Few people know
that a very simple formula, which is easily
made up at home, will turn the hair back to
a natural color in a perfectly harmless man-
ner. You have only to get two ounces, of
tammalite concentrate from your chemist and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum to prove
this. Apply' tins simple and harmless lotion
for a few nights to the hair with a small
sponge, and the greyness will gradually dis-
appear. The lotion is neither, sticky nor
greasy and has been proved over and over
again for generations past by those in pos-
session of the formula.
About Shampoos.
To quote an eminent London beauty
specialist : “The slimy egg cannot -dissolve
the,; scalp impurities, but only adds to the
trouble by completely choking the pores with
animal matter.” The substance sticks, de-
composes, thereby causing the hair to assume
a dead, “dull color.” Avoid eggs (and soap
too);, on your hair. Try the delightful pre-
paration made by stirring a teaspoonful of
stallax in a cup of hot water. It will bring
out new beauty possibilities in your hair,
will clear and prevent dandruff, and leave
that fine fluffy effect so much sought after.
It also removes excess oil or greasiness. [1
