[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I idolise my blue-eyed Floss!
I humbly bring her gifts and flowers
And worship her for hours and hours.
But there are other days, old hoss,
When I would love to beat my joss!
N.S.W.
AH TOO SOON (Washing done).
The spitefulness of fate. Mrs. Richard
Darling, of Carlton (Vic.), had five sons
on service. At last word arrived that one
was coining home; and she bustled out to
buy a few odds and ends for his welcome. ..
The verdict was “Accidental death” —killed
by a tramcar.
What is the origin of the word Bispham,
anyhow? The now-decorated Attorney-
General for the Speck is William Bispham
Propsting, and there is a famous old bari-
tone singer named David Bispham, whose
son was the first American soldier to be
killed in France. If Propsting was ever a
prop with a sting in it. Bispham was pro-
bably a ham of the bisp variety. But what
was a bisp?
Randolph Bedford writes :
“Cato” (B. 27/12/T7) sidesteps my reply except
to repeat the lie of the Ryan Government funking
the strikers. The facts! The strike would have
cost millions had it spread ; the Queensland Govern
ment, holding to the Arbitration law rigidly, and
giving away nothing, fixed it at a cost of about
£l5O, representing Ihe exes, of the unionists’ dele-
gation to Brisbane. The gang who would have pre-
ferred the 'loss of millions and the misery of a
general strike in order to blacken the Government’s
face, roared even at the £l5O.
About Japanese dogs t
Sessue walked into the Thursday Island Hospital
wifh a little dog in his pocket. I wanted it. “Can
I buy ?” I asked. Sessue drew his breath through
his teeth and bowed his head. It was a dear doggie
and looked like a silk chrysanthemum. Sessue said
as I was so kind to the sick Japanese he would leave
the dog now. “But ask what he wants for it,” said
wise old Captain John Strachan, who was waiting to
see the doctor about a pearl concession. I asked,
“As madam is so good and kind to our sick Japan-
ese,” said Sessue, “she may have it for £70.” 1
gasped. The Captain explained that £7O was cheap,
and that the dog probably had a pedigree five hundred
years long. Nowadays I always ask the price.
William Uden has ended a useful but
monotonous life's Work. An English sailor
from boyhood, South Aus. caught him young
and put him on a sandhill outside the Port
River mouth, his job being to work a sema-
phore when ships were sighted. Then official
whaleboats went off and boarded them—and
the crews sometimes got drowned in the pro-
cess. Uden stood on that sandhill for just
half a century, and saw the Semaphore grow
into a popular seaside suburb, though the
building of an Outer Harbor has taken away
most of its old importance. The old survivor
of sailing-ship days lived on to be S 3.
“Rookus” : A neat little swindle is worked
on you and me by some cute railway travel-
lers. The Mountain train which leaves
Mount Victoria at G a.m. pulls up at Pen-
rith, where tickets are collected. A lady
recently presented one for Strathfield. The
collector told her that she would have to get
out at Homebush. as that train did not stop
at Strathfield. She thanked him, and the
train moved on. At Homebush I remarked
to her that she had reached her destination.
She smiled sweetly and said she had changed
her mind and was going to Sydney. By this
little dodge, you see, you save the fare from
Strathfield to Sydney.
One big woman about another:
I speak of Beatrice Grimshaw by name. I have
just returned from London, where they always do.
Pearson’s and such folk star her, solus, on their City
of London posters: “A brilliant new short story by
Beatrice Grimshaw.” I endorse what she says about
growing things in Papua. One can grow almost any-
thing there. I was assured by the head of the Agri-
cultural Department that rice was impossible. Others
who knew more about the country said the same.
Not'long afterward I had a bag of seed rice sent
me from Java. I scraped trenches under my house
in Moresby and planted the seed and kept it well
watered. It grew. And I ate rice. Not much, as I
only tried with a little. But rice, and of my own
growing. Returning to her. With all her fine, big
outlook on life 1 never met a woman who was more
womanly. She wears clothes that make us gasp and
say “How sweet!’’ And I never saw another who
can put on a glove so perfectly.
A century ago Napoleon summed up Prus-
sianism. In the memoirs of Madame Junot
it is recorded that her husband secured
Nap.’s consent to say that any of those sol-
diers who would eschew their queues and
hair-powder would do what was agreeable
to their general, hut that there was to be
no compulsion. There was much hair-cut-
ting next day; also, because of tall talk
among those who wanted to insist on the
thing being done, a few duels. Napoleon soon
heard of this and wrote: “Junot, —-I ap-
proved of your plan because I conceived it
to be useful, but I forbid all Prussian
measures. I will have no improvements
effected in my army either by fighting or
flogging.— Bonaparte.”
“Pejay”: A reunion of old scholars was
held last month at Gape Bridgewater, Port-
land (Vic.), to celebrate the 52nd anniver-
sary of the local State school. Of the 30
children who attended on the opening day,
29 are still alive and well. The unlucky one
was fatally burnt a few years ago. Oh, it’s
a most debilitating climate !
On a recent breathless Melbourne night
which had followed a blistering day, about
five dozen (assorted sizes) of Adela Pank-
hurst Walsh’s Socialist pals could think of
nothing better to do than serenade her. She
is serving her sentence in the Women’s
Prison at Coburg, and the 60, tuning up,
gave a manifold imitation of Blondel outside
the-Lion Heart’s prison-tower. “We’ll keep
Australia Free” gathered a crowd; but if
people swarmed like bees, police advanced
like hornets. The angel choir was scattered
and two arrests were made. While bail for
the captives was arriving, the choristers
gave tongue in a little house opposite the
police station. Next day the two who had
been arrested were fined a fiver each. A
month was the alternative, and they took it
out.

and it is unmistakable.
In a large proportion of cases anaemia and
debility are responsible for neuralgia, parti-
cularly in women. To remedy the neuralgic
condition it is necessary to build up the
blood and improve the general health, and
for this purpose there is nothing better or
more convenient than Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills. , . . .
Sciatica is nothing more than neuralgia ol
the sciatica nerve. .
If you have neuralgic or sciatic pains do
not go to great expense for treatment until
you have tried building up the blood. . If you
are pale the chances are very great that the
treatment with Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills is
all that you will require. Hot applications
and liniments may be used at the same time
if you desire. They may relieve the pain,
and temporary relief is the best you can ex-
pect from them anyway. The pain m the
nerve is caused by ner\e staivation. Di.
Williams’ Pink Pills give to the blood the
elements that the nerves need. They con-
tain no narcotics and are not pain-killers.
They remove the cause of the pain.

The Inky Way.
“What Happened to Jones” is a detail
compared with what happened to its author
the other day, when the Age presented him
with a baronetcy! The royal George delighted
to honor Edward Broadhurst, a financial
magnate, and the Age promptly announced
that “George Broadhurst, dramatist, would
in future write “Bart.” after his name. No
doubt much less merit has been sentenced to
a title ; but Broadhurst is for practical pur-
poses a Yankee, having left England in his
* . »
Died suddenly but peacefully, on Decem-
ber 20, 1017, with a considerate bailiff at
its bedside, the Perth Press Club. It left
goods and chattels valued on paper at
approximately £GOO and liabilities for nearly
the same amount. A voluntary-liquidation
petition has been filed ; but as the late lam-
ented managed to get incorporated under a
Act which was intended to apply only _to
non-trading concerns and makes no provision
for liquidation, it is doubtful if the Court
will agree to this. If it doesn t bankruptcy
seems the only alternative. Thei Club was
started three years ago by 13 of the press
and by taking in outsiders the mem-
bership soon grew to 150. Then a move was
made to more stylish premises, and by the
middle of 1917 yearly receipts had topped
£3OOO Then the price of liquors soared ana
cut into the profits, a number of good
spenders went into camp and others left the
State. As the Club was tied to a bill tor
£ll a week, and had to find for steward s
wages, counter-lunch, lighting and geneia
expenses an additional £l5 to £lB a week,
death came soon, all efforts to build up an
attenuated membership having failed.
* * *
Enlisted, and now peeling spuds in camp,
Walter Richmond May, for six years printer
of Melbourne Herald, and prior to that
sub-overseer of the Argus for many years.
As a babv May learned to walk on the deck
of a sailing-ship, and was expected by his
friends to adorn the King’s Navee; but he
will do just as well in the Army Medical.
His father was printer of the Argus before

“Jim M”: Yol. 1 No. 1 of Light and
Shade, the Melb. National Gallery students’
mag., is one of the curiosities of the Inky
Way. It consists of 30-odd pages of letter-
press and illustrations. As all the
press is type-written only one copy is pro-
duced. Director Barney Hall opens with a
photograph of a Chinese portrait; then < -
low color sketches and black and white stuff
from all sorts, including scene painters,
architects and litho. men. Colin Colahan
gives the infant a leg up with clever pen-
and-ink work, and Barney Hall has a sonnet
beside which Bernard O’Dowd is simplicity.

monthly. Now, it would be interesting to
know who are com-
piling the readers
and preparing them
for publication.
The work must be
going on now. Are
Australian writers
getting a chance?
They never had
much in the Sup-
plementary, a n
average of one Aus-
tralian book annu-
ally out of seven.
That’s not enough.
I call upon my
countrymen to r ; se
and ask questions
before it is too late.

ME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Modesty hath no part in the making of an artist.”
Amazed am I all do not see
A fact that’s ever plain to me —
My marked superiority.
I write the story terse and true,
With rich, red life-blood pouring through.
And lit with humor keen and new ;
Then vainly wait to hear it named
The equal of the world s most famed.
To have my genius acclaimed.
I make some poems sweet and low
Where winged angels come and go,
And vital passions ebb and flow ;
Then wait to feel in touch with mine
The world’s pulse beat with rapture line
Eor those tremendous things I sign,
And see, alas! the whole world wide
Pass by upon the other side,
With others verses occupied.
I set out whimsies quaint and squibs
At which the world might crack its ribs
To win me eminence and dibs,
And see the world sit up and grin
At some mere clowning mannikin.
Obtuseness is man’s grievous sin.
But this I know, I am the best;
Yet hope of triumph loses zest
When one uneasy fact’s expressed:
That when the world's acknowledged me,
No longer quite supreme will be
My unique perspicacity!
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.

The Challenge of the Guns.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
COOL and sweet the song of Evening,
fraught with haunting purple glooms,
When the wind with ghostly fingers
stirs C o grasses’ feathery plumes,
Culling Jrcer "c from the censers of the
lionc.. cush-flower blooms;
But above the bushland music, beating, beat-
ing from afar,
I can hear the deeper throbbing of the trum-
pet voice of war,
And the burden of it runs, in the cnalle.ige
of the guns:
“Send your nearest, send your dearest! Send
your sons , send your sons!”
Say the rumbling, grumbling guns. “Send
your sons, send your sons!”
All the bush has subtle music, when the
silver-sandalled Night,
Crowned with mystery and magic, gowned
with robes of pale moonlight,
Softly croons her dreamland runes to speed
the slow-winged stars in flight;
But above the bushland music, beating,
beating from afar,
1 can hear the deeper throbbing of the
trumpet voice of war;
And the burden of it runs in the challenge
of the guns:
“Send your fairest, send your rarest! Send
your sons, send your sons!”
Say the rumbling, grumbling guns. “Send
your sons, send your sons!”
Loud the triumph song of Morning, when
the light begins to flow,
Sweeping back the dim-grey dawning from
the sleeping fields below,
And the magpie, lark and song-thrusli carol
in the early glow ;
But above the bushland music, beating, beat-
ing from afar,
I can hear the deeper throbbing of the trum-
pet voice of war ;
And the burden of it runs, in the challenge
of the guns:
“Send us valor, send us beauty! Send your
sons, send your sons!”
Say the rumbling, grumbling guns. “Send
your sons, send your sons!”
Clamorous the chant of Noonday, pealing
from a heart of gold,
When the. yellow waves of sunlight through
the vibrant bush are rolled,
Beating back from leaf and blossom lined
with colors myriad-fold ;
But through all the bushland music, ringing,
ringing out afar,
I can hear the women’s answer to the trum-
pet voice of war, -
Hear the bushland women’s answer to the
challenge of the guns:
‘ Talce our nearest, take our dearest! Take
our husbands, take our sons!”
And the lovers: “Take the ones who should
father future sons!
Take our tears, take our fears” —oh, the
heart blood faster runs
At the glory of the answer to the challenge
of the guns!
TASMANIA.
H. W. STEWART.
Only for Bill.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE room in the slab hut was papered
with sheets of illustrated weeklies, and
had a hardened mud floor. The only
light came from a big log fire, which threw
out waves of flicker. A man with ginger-
grey beard sat smoking on one side of the
fire, and a thin, care-worn, middle-aged
woman sat darning socks opposite. At the
table sat a rather pretty girl. The glow
gave her a really beautiful complexion. Her
eyes lit up when she heard the sharp clatter
of a horse cantering towards the house.
One could say that she had been waiting for
the sound.
“That teamster come again,” said the
man, spitting in the fire.
“Seems he comes often,” said the woman,
with a complaining glance at the girl.
The girl said nothing.
The door opened, and a lithe young fellow
came in quietly, and took a seat by the far
end of the room. He looked ill at ease, as if
he knew that he was not welcome. The
older man gave him a nod of recognition,
and spat in the fire.
“Rather chilly of evenings now,” said the
teamster.
Nobody spoke. The older man smoked
and spat, and the woman went on with her
darning, and the girl began to drum with
her fingers on the table. When she thought
the young man’s eyes were not fixed on her
(which they were nearly all the time) she
stole a quick look at him from under sly eye-
lashes.
“Y here yer camped to-night?” asked the
older man after another spit.
“By Adam’s Crik.”
“Feed good there?”
“No; I had ter put the team in Brown’s
paddock.”
“Didn’t think he’d let yer.”
, ; I strapped down the wires an’ put
em in. He won’t know. I’ll have ’em out
before the mornin’.”
Didn't think yer’d get that fur,” said
the older man. “Yer must have shoved a
bit. It’s 16 mile. Rather fur to ride here
of evenings, anyway.” He added this
meaningly.
I thought it would be a bit lonely at the
camp, said the teamster, simplv.
The girl shot another of those quick
glances at the young man. She then hung
her head, with her face crimsoning.
Conversation lapsed. The older man
knocked the ashes out of his pipe, and filled
and lighted it up again. You coidd hear
him puffing at his pipe, and the girl drum-
ming on the table with her fingers.
Y ish yer’d stop doing that with yer
fingers, said the mother after a time. “It
lair gets on my nerves.”
1 es. mother,” she said, and put her hands
on her lap.
Can t see why yer don’t do something,”
complained the old woman. “Leaving every-
think for me. I’m sure gels nowadays isn’t
what they was in my time.”
“Lovely night outside,” said the teamster.
“Never see such a beautiful moon. I could
have fair written poetry as I come along.”
At this point he seemed to get something
stuck in his throat. It took a minute or two
for him to work it clear.
“Thought yer would like ter see it, Liza.”
The girl looked at the father, and then at
the mother, but both assumed a more than
usually stolid manner, the old man puffing
vigorously at his pipe. The girl looked
irresolute.
“Best time of the day on these frosty
nights,” said the teamster. “It’s a sight
yer shouldn’t miss. I wouldn’t ’ave missed
it for five pounds.”
The girl still sat looking irresolute.
“En I’d like to talk a little business with
you. You see, it will be too fur to come
here again,” he said, and added: “Forty
miles to-morrer night.”
The last words seemed to settle matters.
With a defiant look towards both her parents
she stood up, snatched the wool shawl from
a hook and passed quickly out.
The teamster rose slowly and awkwardly,
as if he were not sure that matters were
progressing amicably all round. He gave
a conciliatory smile towards the old folks,
which they ignored, the man spitting into the
fire more emphatically than ever.
When the young man came out into the
bright moonlight the girl stood with her back
towards him. She did not look round, but
when he approached she began to walk
slowly beside him towards the sliprails,
where the horse was hitched. Neither
seemed to notice the lovely moon, or think
about versifying it. The man found con-
versation awkward. He said:
“Frosts is early this year.”
She “Yes.”
“Makes it hard for ploughin’.”
She said “Yes.”
Then a long silence occurred.
“Didn’t think I’d come along ter-night,
did yer?”
She said “No,” but this was not true.
“Couldn’t help meself, somehow,” he said.
Then he struck another conversational dead
wall. She gave him no help. They were
now leaning on the sliprails, or rather he
was. Her hands rested lightly on them, and
she kept looking away from him.
“I’ve been thinkin’ a lot lately,” he said,
and then waited for her to ask him what
he was thinking about, but she did not speak.
There was a wonderfully serious expression
on her face. Her eyes were luminous in the
moonlight, but she did not let him see that.
Perhaps it was for that she kept looking
away from him.
“I’ve bin thinkin’,” he said, “that it must
be a bit lonely for you livin’ here with the
old people, an’ so far away from other
people.”
lie put his hand towards her arm as if
to take it, but seemed to think better of it.
“There’s plenty to do,” she replied. “It
is never lonely when you have work ter do.”
“But I mean,” he began, and then that
lump came in his throat again and kept him
from speaking a time. “I mean for a nice
girl like you,” he said thickly.
She did not stir. She'did not speak. But
she seemed to tremble.
“I was wonderin’ what ’mu think of me,
Liza?” he asked.
She was very slow in replying.
“I thought you was a nice feller,” she
said. Then she paused, and after a time:
“I was only tellin’ Susie Barnes yesterday
what a nice feller you was.”
At this he mustered up sufficient courage
to lay his hand on her arm. She let it
remain there, to his astonishment.
“Somehow, I don’t think your folks likes
me, Liza.”
“Oh, yes, they do. Mother says yer seem
a decent feller, en father says he s’poses yer
all right, en that’s a lot for him ter say.”
“When I asked you out, Liza, just now,
it was just to let you know ’ow it was with
me. I’ve bin wantin’ since I first saw you
a fortnight ago to tell you ’ow, nice I think
you are, but you was never alone, en to-night
was me larst charnce, en it will be a month
before I’m back on the next trip. Give
me the straight tip, Liza. Do you care for
me?”
Liza gave him a full emotional look, and
then her face fell, flushing blood red. There
was that in her glance which made him fold
her in his arms and kiss her full on the lips.
Then she struggled away from him, and
looked at him with frightened eyes.
“I didn’t oughter,” she said.
“Yes, you ought,” he said emphatically.
“You are mine now.”
“I promised Bill I’d never kiss a feller
any more.” she said. “I promised him last
thing when he went away.”
His face became set and stern, and he
seized her roughly by the arm.
“Who’s this ’ere Bill?” he asked.
“Bill’s me ’usband,” she said. “He’s gone
shearin’.”
He fell back a few paces and seemed to
crumple up.
“Just my luck!” he said. “Liza, I thought
you the nicest girl I ever met. I was never
much on gels till I saw you.”
“Yes,” she said in a whisper. “It seems
a pity, don’t it.”
“Why did you marry him?” he asked bit-
terly.
She was a long time replying. When she
did it was with a sob in her throat. “I
didn’t know there was you then.”
They stood looking at each other, a few
paces still between, and a long silence
occurred. He seemed to be fighting an in-
ward battle.
“Oh, well,” he said, “I s’pose I must be
goin’.”
But he did not go.
“S’pose you’ll call in when you’re pass-
in’.” she asked, “same as usual?”
He held his arms out to h£r as if he
would snatch her to him, and then suddenly
turned away.
“I would, Liza, I would, only—only for
Bill.”
Then he leaped into the saddle and gal-
loped away. And she never stirred till the
sound of bis horse’s hoofs had died away in
the distance.
ROBERT BROTHERS.

A Song.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHAT wizard wind a changeling
brings
Unto my garden sod,
That where we planted foxglove springs
A golden-rod?
And down November’s yellow aisles
A bird-call comes to me
Like laughter ov.r misty miles
Of lonely sea.
A weed beside my garden gate,
A wild bird in the skies,
The blowing of a wind of fate
Wee golden eyes,
Ye know a path to peaks of blue
Like some white altar-stair
Where there is only God, and two
Dark pines at prayer !
Ye know the hills where afternoon
Hangs like a saffron flame
Above a hidden altar hewn
To Love’s high name.
Ye know a path that twain have trod,
Where Esk goes leaping through—
O golden-rod, my golden-rod !
O hills of blue !
VICTORIA.
MARIE E. J. PITT.
The Release.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ O! OILENCE !”
An uprising of heavy policemen
betokened the arrival of Justice.
Justice was not much to look at. He was
probably representative in his person of the
sort of justice which is dispensed in the
police court. But the formal respect was
not the less observed on that account. The
word “Soilence” went round the court with
urgent whispers to laggard or inexperienced
persons to “Remove that ’at!” or Stand up,
there!” Then, Justice being seated, the
solicitors reseated themselves, the policemen
without special duties to perform sat down,
and the laity fortunate enough to have a
seat seated itself. The prosecuting con-
stable sorted himself out from the ruck. The
deposition-clerk clicked his typewriter; and
the court was open.
By the time I had finished with a hoarse
individual who drew me outside, and tried
to square me about a case of which I knew
nothing, and refused to believe that I was
not a witness for the prosecution, and
appealed piteously to me to “be a sport,
after threatening to have the push on me if
I put him away, the Court had got through
a lot of business. It had dealt with the
drunks. Two of them had signed the pledge,
which for a first offender is the easiest
method to escape without a fine. It had
dealt in a brief fashion with the guilties
and suspended for further treatment the not
guilties.” It had dosed as a police court
and opened as a petty debts court, and i
proceeded to deal with me. Then it closed
as a petty debts court and re-opened as a
police court.
It was an uninteresting day, and nothing
more than the usual happened to show how
great an ass the law is and how wooden-
headed are some of the idiots who dispense
it. A milkman who apparently had, iiKe
Pharaoh’s daughter, drawn a profit from tne
water, was lined heavily to the extent ot
10s ■ and a woman who had taken a parcel
which didn’t belong to her from a grocer s
shop was told to produce a fine of id an l
Os. costs within 48 hours, or go to gaol.
Then Michael Kerrigan was called.
Michael Kerrigan slopped forward and
anticipated things.
“Not guilty, y’ Anner. You see, its this
“We’ll learn all about that,” said his
Anner. , , ,
“Sit down !” commanded the sergeant.
“I never done it, y’ Anner, s’elp me
Gawd !”
“Will you sit down !”
“S’elp me Gawd!” repeated Kerrigan im-
pressively. .. T ~ , lT
“If you don’t sit down,” said Justice, 1
will deal with you for contempt.”
Then Kerrigan subsided. . Although when
the prosecuting sergeant read the charge he
narrowly escaped being “dealt with lor
exclaiming “You’re a liar!” he escaped
because the personification of Justice d d not
catch the remark and accepted an explana-
tion that it was someone else —Michael had
apparently made a practice of despoiling
empty houses of gas-fixtures, coppers, iron
roofs and similar things, and disposing or
them. There was no evidence about it. It
is a remarkable thing that in our police
courts the police are quite frequently allowed
to give their suspicions about accused men
as evidence. But the fact of his having
disposed of a quantity of these things,
amounting in value to £2O-odd, for £4, was
proved by the testimony of the man who pur-
chased them, and the man who lent him the
cart on the day on which the robberies
occurred and the goods were sold, and by
people who had seen the cart in the
vicinity of the rifled houses. Michael s
defence was that he had met a man
—a man with a tanned face and a red mous-
tache, whom he knew as “Ginger” —and this
man knew another bloke (laughter - Order
in the court!”) who had a lot of old cop
pers and iron to dispose of. and that, when
he got the cart, intending to go in for a bit of
dealing. Ginger had met him and introduced
him to the man-—name was Smith—who had
taken him to a vacant lot and filled his cait.
“I sold the stuff, y’ Worship, but s’elp me,
I didn’t know it was stolen. I gave the
money ter Smith, and I haven’t been paid
meself yet. Smith said he’d pay me when the
cheque was cashed.” _
“The cheque was open,” said the prosecut-
ing sergeant.
“So help me Gawd!” said Kerrigan, im-
pressively. He had an undying belief in the
power of the phrase. He looked at Justice.
It was referring to notes with a pretence of
understanding them, while it waited for the
deposition-clerk to present a copy of the
evidence. He looked at the prosecuting ser-
geant, who was sorting out his papers for
the next case. He looked towards the re-
porters. There was only one, and he
was considering how “Kerrigan’s Ivurious
Kapers” would look as a headline. And
Kerrigan uttered again, “So help me Gawd!”
“Witnesses?”
Kerrigan had no witnesses. He relied on
the look of celestial innocence he tried to
assume.
“Cross-examination ?”
“You say this man’s name was Smith?”
“Smith —s’elp me Gawd !”
“Where did Smith cash this cheque?”
“With some bloke, I don’t know his name,
s’elp me Gawd !”
“Don’t say that.” said Justice severely,
for the half-dozenth time. “It’s blasphemy.
You’re on your oath; that will do. Don’t
use the words again!”
“I won’t, y’ Worship, s’elp me Gawd, I
won’t, then!”
The cross-examination concluded with
Kerrigan still calling upon the Almighty to
s’elp him.
“Previous convictions?” queried Justice.
“None,” admitted the police. “But the
accused consorts with bad characters, and
has been under suspicion for some time.”
It was one of those loose statements that
are submitted in the police-courts without
any evidence in support; and Kerrigan did
not know enough to point out that parsons
mix with bad characters sometimes, and that
now and again- an absolutely honest man mixes
with Prime Ministers and other members of
Parliament. It probably wouldn’t have
helped him much, anyhow; he had elected
to be dealt with summarily, and the man
who fined a milk-and-water man ten shillings
dealt with him as summarily as the neces-
sary dissertation on the ungodliness of
offences against property would allow.
“Luckily for you, it is a first offence,”
concluded Justice, severely. “Five pounds or
thirty days’ hard labor.”
“Time?” said the stunned Michael.
“No time!” said Justice, sharply. “Next
Ca ßut while Kerrigan, protesting that he
was “ennercent,” was being removed, a
woman’s voice rose in a wail.
“Yer Anner, yer Anner, he’s me ’usband,
a good ’usband, too, and ’e’s me sole sup-
port an’ the support of me childer. An’ if
’e goes to gaol, yer Anner, we’ll starve. ’E’s
bein’ led away, yer Anner, that’s what ’e
’as —led away by propogate companions.”
A worn but neatly-dressed woman, sobbing
excitedly, had burst from the back of the
court, and the end of her discourse landed
her as near to the bench as she could get. _
“Who is this woman?” inquired Justice
with asperity.
“She’s Kerrigan’s wife,” explained the
police. “A hardworking, respectable woman.”
“Yes, an’ there’s five childer, yer Anner,
and their father going to gaol means they’ll
starve. ’E’s a good man, truly, yer Anner:
Vs bin misled! For the love of ’Eaven, yer
Anner, let him off! ’E was a ’ardworking
man till the union made the strike”
"Was he in a strike?” Justice.
“’E was, yer ’Anner, but he’s left the
union, and ’e’s bin out of work since,” she
said, pacified by a hope that Justice was
relenting.
“My good woman,” said Justice, “your
husband has been convicted, and nothing can
be done. If the fine is paid”
“Oh, yer Anner,” wailed Mrs. Kerrigan,
“an’ where am I to get five pound from —me
with me ’elpless children! For the love of
’Eaven, yer Anner —’e was a good man, an
a good ’usband. An’ a good father, too; if
he ’adn’t been, he”
“My good woman,” began Justice, per-
turbed.
“Y’ Worship,” said- the smartest police-
court solicitor, seizing the opportunity, as
the smartest police-court solicitor knows
how, “perhaps, before the court closes, the
fine may be I myself am pre-
pared”
“Very well, Mr. Longtongue,” said Jus-
tice, and he proceeded to hold out hope to
Mrs. Kerrigan, while someone at the solici-
tors’ table started a collection. The police-
prosecutor gave five shillings. The police
made a collection amongst themselves and
the audience. One of the solicitors who
knew me drew me into the matter.
That was one of the bright moments of
my life. I noticed that the unforgiving credi-
tor who was responsible for my presence had
lingered, like myself, to hear the case. I took
my solicitor friend over and told my creditor
that if he liked to give ten shillings I would
give five. lie was glorying in the verdict a
foolish magistrate had given him by cutting
the difference between what he claimed and
what I admitted I owed ; and he didn’t real-
ise at that time the hollow thing a verdict
(which costs six shillings to obtain) really
is. He reckoned that coming from a loser
my offer was generous ; while I gloated over
the fact that in addition to the money that
he had sacrificed by taking me to court, my
antagonist was going to pay ten shillings for
his morning’s enjoyment.
Longtongue, my friend and myself were
able to go to Mrs. Kerrigan with the good
news and the cash, and the fine was paid.
The poor woman in piteous language ren-
dered her thanks; and we went with her
and the released husband outside the court.
He was not as exuberant in his gratitude
as his wife. In fact, he did not appear to
be certain about being grateful at all. We
watched them as they went up the street.
By the time the corner was reached the
woman’s piteousness had left her. She was
a volcano of wrath in full eruption.
“I’ve got you now !’’ she screamed at him,
filling her hands with blue-metal. “Think I
wanted yer for yerself, yer loafing carcase,
yer! I wanted ter ’ave it out with yer
before I cooled! Got that money, did yer,
an’ never even brought ’ome a bottle o’
beer! Where’s the money, ye brute —what
’ave yer done with it?”
